To prevent inflammation, bind the wound with a thread from linen cloth and then treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
Cut the two arteries in the temples.
If a person suffers from chronic sinusitis or acute attacks caused by noxious humidity and heat in the temple muscles, or severe chronic headaches, and various medical treatments have been ineffective, we have found that cutting or cauterizing the arteries in the temples, as described, can be effective for these conditions.
The procedure for cutting them involves shaving the hair from the temples, and then identifying the visible artery in the temple, as its pulse is usually apparent and it rarely goes unnoticed except in rare cases.
Or, if the weather is extremely cold and it is difficult to locate the artery, the patient should tighten their neck with a piece of cloth, and you should rub the area with a cloth or apply hot water until the artery becomes clearly visible. Then take the scalpel whose image is shown here. Then gently cut the skin with it until you reach the artery, then insert a needle into it and pull it up to remove it from the skin, freeing it from the fascia beneath it on all sides. If the artery is thin, loop it with the tip of the needle. Then cut a portion of it according to the distance between its ends, ensuring that it constricts without causing bleeding. If it is not completely severed and does not stop the flow of blood, the blood will not clot properly. Then let out six to three seers of blood.
If the artery is large, it should be tied at two places with a strong folded thread, and let the thread be either of flax or of horn strips so that corruption does not reach it before the wound has healed, thus causing hemorrhage.
Then cut off the excess between the ligatures; do this at that time or later. If you wish to cauterize it with a cautery heated by a knife until its ends are consumed, it will serve as well or better, as we have said.
Except if the patient has a fever or a hot temperament, for cauterization helps to consume moisture and is more beneficial. After tying off the arteries, the place should be stuffed with old lint and covered with secure bandages. After untying them, treat with drying medicines that promote flesh growth and use bandaging until he is healed, if God wills.
If bleeding occurs from the artery during your work, immediately cut it off either by cauterization or fill the place with a plug and press firmly until the blood stops. If neither of these is available to you, place your finger on it until the blood clots.
Cool the area with very cold water until the sharpness subsides and then secure it as necessary. A less severe and easier method than tying off the artery is to cauterize the vein with this double-edged cautery after applying medicine to the area.
Apply it very hot until it reaches the bone and the vein is cut in two places to separate its ends, as it will not heal with this cauterization at all. This is a picture of the cautery.
It should be as sharp as two small spoons, but much less sharp than a knife, for if it were as sharp as a knife, cold would reach them more quickly and flesh would not separate quickly. If there is some thickness in them, the heat of fire will cling to them.
Cut through the flesh quickly; this work is better than all others, lighter and easier, and let there be between the two blades a thickness equal to that of a finger.
Chapter Four.
On the treatment of continuous hot tears flowing to the eyes.
If the tears are continuous and their flow is from the veins on the skull's exterior, and no benefit is derived from medicinal treatments, and you observe that the patient's face has turned red. There is a sensation on his forehead like the movement of ants, and his eyes are sunken and watery, with their lashes weakened from the intensity of the tears. Therefore, they resort to this treatment, which is called treatment with the sword, and it involves ordering the patient to shave the hair from his forehead.
Then, three parallel incisions are made on the forehead along its length. Each incision is about two finger breadths long; one in the middle of the forehead parallel to the length of the nose, the second a little away from the movement of the muscle near the temple, and the third on the opposite side.
Be cautious not to cut the two arteries on the sides and keep your hand away from where the jaws meet. There should be a distance of about three clenched fingers between each incision. Have pieces of sponge prepared or a dry cloth at hand to stop the bleeding.
Then insert the sharp two-ended scalpel whose shape is as follows. From the incision next to the temple to the middle incision, and peel off all the skin between the two incisions along with the subcutaneous tissue on the bone; then do the same from the middle incision to the third incision, then remove this scalpel. And insert another tool into the first incision, called a sharp knife on one side and smooth without a blade on the other side; this is its shape:.
And direct its sharp side upwards towards the flesh attached to the skin, and its smooth side towards the bone; push it until it reaches the middle incision and cut all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes without cutting through to the outer surface of the skin.
Then do the same for the middle incision to the other side; after a moderate amount of blood has flowed, press the areas where the clotted blood is cut; then in each incision place a wick made of boiled cotton and put on it a poultice soaked in a mixture of syrup, oil, or vinegar and oil.
To prevent a hot swelling; on the third day, remove the bandage and use plenty of warm water baths, then treat it with a salve of basilicon with rose oil, and other treatments used for wounds until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Five.
On the Treatment of Tears and Discharges to the Eyes from Inside the Head.
When a person experiences frequent, severe, hot discharges to the eyes that are continuous, and you notice the eyes becoming sunken, smaller, with weakened vision, the eyelids become ulcerated, and the eyelashes fall out. There will be a sharp and painful pain in the head's depth, and continuous sneezing. Know from these symptoms that those substances and discharges come from many deep veins. The best treatment for this is to order the patient to shave his forehead, then make one incision in the middle of the forehead.
There will be a sharp and painful pain in the head's depth, and continuous sneezing. Know from these symptoms that those substances and discharges come from many deep veins. The best treatment for this is to order the patient to shave his forehead, then make one incision in the middle of the forehead. Or raise it slightly in width and start the incision from the left temple to the right temple, and make the incision to the bone, keeping your hand away from the moving temples' muscles when chewing until the bone is exposed and all the blood is cleaned with a sponge.
Then separate the edges of the incision with old cotton or a bandage made of linen, and secure it from above with dressings. Soak the dressings in liquid and oil to prevent a hot swelling. When you remove them and see that the hot swelling has subsided, you should scrape the bone so that flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with a drying regimen that promotes flesh growth, such as taking two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony to prepare an ointment, which is used for the growth of flesh in wounds like these.
Chapter Six.
On the Treatment of What Falls into the Ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a metallic stone or something like a stone such as iron and glass; or plant seeds like chickpeas, pits, and the like; or a liquid substance such as water, vinegar, and similar items.
And if it is an animal [stone], when a stone falls into the ear or something like a stone that does not exceed in size within the ear, then turn the ear towards the sun. If you see the stone, drip some oil of violet or serge into it, then try to remove it by moving the head or sneezing with condys. Plug the nostrils when sneezing after placing a bandage or wool around the ear and extend the ear upwards. Often, this treatment helps to remove it; if not, then try to remove it with thin wax, which is shown in this illustration.
If it comes out with the wax, otherwise try to remove it with a delicate hook with little bends. If that does not work, then make a tube of copper and insert one end of the tube into the ear hole carefully.
Plug around the tube with softened pitch mixed with oil so that there is no other way for air except through the tube. Then draw it out strongly with your breath; often, this will work. If not, take some Arab gum or prepared birdlime in small quantity and place it at the tip of the probe.
After wrapping it tightly with cotton, then gently insert it into the ear hole after drying the ear of moisture. If it does not come out despite all the methods described, proceed to make an incision before a hot swelling or spasm occurs.
The incision method is as follows: First, let blood from the patient in a cupping vessel according to their strength. Then seat the patient between your hands and turn their ear upwards. Make a small cut at the base of the ear near its cartilage in the lower part.
The incision should be made in a crescent shape until it reaches the stone, then remove it with whatever instrument is available to you. Then sew up the incision as quickly as possible and treat it until it heals.
And if the object that has fallen into the ear is one of those seeds that swell and become inflamed, try to remove it using the methods mentioned. If it does not come out, then take a thin, delicate blade in this shape.
Try to cut that type of seed fallen in the ear with it. Do this only if you are certain that the seed has become softened by the moisture of the ear, making it break into many small pieces, then remove them with a hook, a delicate probe, or by suction.
As mentioned, it becomes easier to remove them. As for water that has entered the ear, it is advisable for the patient to sneeze using pepper first while lying down with some old cotton plugged in the affected ear. If this method works, the water will be expelled.
If not, take many thin, smooth pebbles and warm them slightly over fire. The patient should then insert one of these stones into the ear canal while hopping on one leg, and strike another stone against the one in the ear.
He should continue doing this with each stone until all the water is expelled. Alternatively, take a piece of linen or a feather, insert one end into the ear, and hold the other end over fire until it burns mostly away, then replace it with another feather.
Do this repeatedly until all the water is removed, or use a tube as described for removing a stone. As for extracting an animal that has entered it, if it is small like a flea or similar, treat it as mentioned in the division [of cases] and so on.
If its body is large and visible to the senses, try to extract it using cotton and tweezers; this procedure is easier than dealing with any issue in the ear. As for extracting worms that have developed in the ear if you treat it as mentioned in the division of cases in the chapter on tumors and your treatment does not succeed,
You should look into the ear towards the sun; if you see any worms, remove them with cotton or fine tweezers. If you do not see anything, take this tube which has the following shape.
It is narrow at the bottom and wide at the top. Insert the thin end into the ear as much as the patient can tolerate, then suck strongly with it; do this repeatedly until all the worms are removed. If they do not come out, seal around the tube with wax as mentioned for removing a stone.
If they do not come out with the methods mentioned, use cauterization as I have described; it has been tried by earlier practitioners to kill the worms. You will find this in the chapter on cauterization. Your application of oils and medicines into the ear can be done using this device, which has the following shape:
As you can see, make it from silver or copper, narrow at the bottom with a small hole and wide at the top. If you wish, the tube inside the device can be made of solid copper. Alternatively, you may take a wick, wind cotton around one end tightly.
Then pour the oil or juice or any other desired medicine into the tube while it is in the ear, then insert a wick made of cotton from above and squeeze it gently with your hand until the oil flows into the inner ear and you feel it.
Have the patient enter, and make sure what you pour into the ear is slightly warmed in fire. Beware that what you drop into it is not too cold or too hot, for the ear cannot tolerate that.
Chapter Seven.
On the Treatment of Obstructive Conditions of the Ear.
Some children are born with their ear canals not perforated.
It may also occur that some people have an obstruction in the ear canal due to a wound or flesh growth, and this obstruction may be deep within the ear canal, beyond the reach of sight.
It may also be partially exposed outside the ear canal and within sight. That which is deep within the ear and not visible is usually difficult to remove, while that which can be seen should have the patient's ear placed in sunlight. Examine it; if you see the obstruction clearly, then open it with a delicate scalpel. This is how it should appear:.
Its tip should be slightly broad and its sides smooth to avoid harming the ear. If the obstruction is due to flesh that has grown in the ear canal, grasp it gently with a fine forceps and cut it carefully until all the flesh is removed.
If the plug is deep in the ear, take a thin, smooth probe and warm it slightly over fire, then insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the plug and the patient feels the heaviness of hearing, gently push it aside.
Be careful not to injure the nerve of the ear, then place a thread in the ear according to the size of the hole, wrap it with Egyptian ointment; do this for days until you are safe from the wound healing over. Or take a thread, moisten it with water and sprinkle it with powdered antimony.
Use it, and if you see the ear swell with a hot swelling, then remove the thread and replace it with another thread wrapped in keratin ointment made with rose oil until the hot swelling subsides, then continue treating it until it heals.
If bleeding occurs, dip a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, and use the other treatments that stop the mentioned bleeding as described in the article on hemostasis.
Chapter Eight.
On the Treatment of Warts That Appear on the Eyelids.
These warts that appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry; it is necessary to hold them with tweezers or a clamp and cut them at the root with scissors, then apply powdered antimony to the area.
If bleeding occurs from them, treat it with a gentle convex iron; applying the iron after cutting is better. Often they return if any of the root remains, but if you burn it with fire, it will destroy that root and will not return.
Chapter Nine.
On the Treatment of Temporary Catarrh in the Nasal Passages.
It often happens that something resembling severe and persistent catarrh appears in the nasal passages.
And for this reason, it is called catarrh, which is the accumulation of thick moisture in both the upper and lower eyelids. The procedure here is to observe; if the condition appears prominently on the surface of the eyelid and can move freely in all directions, then the task is easy, and you should proceed by applying pressure to it gently.
Then carefully peel it away from all sides until it is completely loosened, then lift it out with forceps and cut it off. If you cannot cut it immediately without piercing the eyelid, doing so will not harm the patient. If the incision is large, stitch it up and treat it until it heals.
If the condition is small, there is no need to worry about it; ointment will heal and seal it. If the catarrh is inclined towards the inner surface of the eyelid, flip the eyelid over and lift the catarrh with forceps without needing an incision, then remove it from all sides.
If you pierce the eyelid in the process, it will not harm the patient. Then wash the eye with salt water after removing the catarrh, and treat the area to promote healing until the patient recovers.
Chapter Ten.
On the Treatment of Sties That Appear in the Eyelid.
The sharnaq is a growth that occurs in the layers of the upper eyelid, and it affects boys more often. It burdens their eyes and they are prone to nighttime coughing fits and are unable to look at the light of the sun. Because tears hasten towards them.
That is why you often see them leaning on their faces and sleeping in that position; their eyelids under the eyebrows are moist, with puffiness and the marks of the sharnaq clearly visible. When you press the area with your fingers, you can feel the sharnaq between them. The method of treatment involves having the patient place his head in your lap.
Then take a piece of linen and prepare a wick from it, and make a circle with that wick around the sharnaq on all sides. Then place it over the area and press it with your fingers from all sides to gather the sharnaq in the center of the circle.
Then make a horizontal incision in the center of that moist area with a sharp scalpel, and the incision should not be larger than the one made in bloodletting. As for the depth, it is necessary to cut through the entire skin until you reach the sharnaq.
In most cases, the sharnaq will protrude from the incision in the form of a white fatty piece. You should pull it out with a linen strip you have rolled between your fingers to the outside while rotating your hand left and right until it is completely removed. Be careful not to extend the incision too much so that the scalpel does not injure the eye.
If the sharnaq does not appear at the beginning of the incision, you should gently extend it a little until the sharnaq becomes visible. Then pull it out as previously described. Afterwards, dip a piece of cloth in vinegar and water and place it on the area, securing it gently with a bandage. Some people crush salt for this purpose.
He places it in the cavity of the incision to dissolve any remaining moisture, then treats it until it heals. If a hot swelling occurs in the area, he treats it with poultices that reduce inflammation until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On Methods of Eyebrow Grooming.
If extra eyelashes grow outside the natural course underneath the normal eyelashes and persist, they harm the eye and cause various diseases such as constant tearing, drooping of the eyelids, whitening, thickening, until it leads to impairment of the vision.
Eyebrow grooming is done in four ways: either by cauterization with fire, or by using a sharp medication as previously mentioned in the section on cauterization, or it can be done by cutting and sewing, or with reeds as I will mention.
Place the patient's head in your lap, then flip the eyelid with your left hand. If it does not flip, insert a needle with thread from beneath the eyelid and pass the needle through the thread from above, near the hairline itself, and pull the thread up with the eyelid.
Flip it with a probe, then make an incision inside the eyelid, below the extra hair, from the larger canthus to the smaller canthus. Then pass the thread and place a small pad of cotton or cloth under the eyelid, and secure the eyelid with adhesive.
Like the shape of an oak leaf, but it should be sized according to how much you want to raise the eyelid, as people's needs differ. Some may need a portion of the eyelid to be cut in proportion to how droopy the eyelid is.
And there are those who need to cut less of all that, according to the laxity of the eyelid, then incise with the scalpel along the two lines you have been taught, starting from the greater commissure to the lesser commissure, and each incision should be near the natural hairline at about the thickness of a thread.
Then insert the spatula into one of the corners of the skin, then peel it all off, then stitch together the two lips with a fine needle and thread. Clean the blood and secure any remaining threads above the eyebrows with some adhesive if necessary.
Do as you wish to do it, or else you may leave it be. Then let the stitches remain for about three or four days, then treat it; if you prefer, you can leave the wound unstitched and treat it with something that dries and contracts, for the eyelid will rise when the wound closes together. Stitching is better however.
This aspect of suturing was mentioned by earlier practitioners, except that it places a burden on the patient; however, it is good work and carries no risk. Another aspect of suturing is to shape the eyelid like the form of an iris leaf as described. Then raise the eyelid with three spatulas that may be spread apart or gathered together in this manner:.
Then cut off any excess of the eyelid with a small scissors in this manner evenly:.
If you cannot hold the spatulas in place and they do not stand firm, take a needle with thread and insert it at the center of the shape. Insert another thread near the greater commissure and a third thread near the lesser commissure. Gather the threads between your fingers evenly, then lift them with a moderate pull.
Raise the entire prepared skin flap as we described, then stitch together the edges of the wound and treat it until it heals. If a hot swelling appears when you cut or incise, apply a plaster like the qirwat and similar soothing ointments to alleviate that swelling.
It may also happen to the lower eyelid that its lashes turn inward, in which case you should use what we mentioned about cutting, stitching, and using cautery with fire or a sharp medicine. Using cautery with fire or a sharp medicine is more effective and less burdensome on the patient than cutting and stitching; cutting and stitching are better for these cases.
Cauterization with reed is done in this manner: Turn the eyelid inside out and make an incision as described from the inner part, then prepare two thin pieces of reed or wood whose length matches that of the eyelid and width less than that of a scalpel blade.
Make small incisions at the ends of both sides where the stitches will be placed, then gently bring together the remaining parts of the eyelid. Tighten the two pieces of reed or wood securely on both sides and leave it for several days. The tightened skin will die, turn black, and decay.
Until it falls off on its own; if it delays, cut it with a scalpel and treat it until it heals. Once it has healed, the eyelid will rise and the lashes will no longer touch the eye. This is an image of the reeds that should be made in this shape and exact measurement for length and width:
The Twelfth Chapter.
On Removing Eyelashes That Irritate the Eye Using a Needle.
If there is one or two hairs, the procedure is to take a fine needle and pass through it a thin smooth silk thread, then gather its ends and tie them with a very delicate knot; let the length of the thread be about an inch.
Then, place another thin thread under the first one, shorter than it, and tie its ends if you wish. Then have the patient rest his head in your lap, preferably near a source of sunlight so that the work can be seen clearly, for it is delicate. Then insert the needle with the thread at the base of the extra hair.
Then pass the needle quickly through the eyelid until it emerges above the natural hair, then pull the thread over the knot and insert the thread into the knot if there is one, two, or at most three hairs.
Then pull with your hand until the hair comes out with the knot along with the natural hair from the eyelid. If you pull the knot and the hair does not come out with it, pull the knot down with the thread that you placed in it until the knot comes out of the upper hole.
Then replace the hair in it and pull them both; do this until you see the hair with your own eyes at the upper hole. At that moment, release the knot and thread and remove them, then tighten the eye and leave it tightened for two or three days until the hair settles in its place and flesh grows around it.
If the hair is short, either add a longer hair from the natural hair to it so that it rises with it, or leave it until it grows longer after some time, then raise it.
Chapter Thirteen.
في علاج الشرطة التي تحدث في الجفن الأعلى للعين.
التي تتعرض لها هذه الشرطة تسمى أرنبية، وتكون هذه الشرطة إما طبيعية وإما عرضية. فالعربية تكون من اندمل جرح أو شق أو كيٍّ أو نحو ذلك.
ووجه العمل فيها أن تشق ذلك الاندمال وأن تفرقي شفتيه وتصير فيما بينهما فتيلة من كتان وتربطها حتى تبرأ، ولا ينبغي أن تستعمل في علاجها الأشياء التي تجفف وتقبض.
فإنك إن فعلت ذلك رجعت الشرطة بأشد مما كانت، بل استعمل فيها الأشياء التي ترخي مثل الحلبة والتنطيل بماء قد طبخ فيه خطمي وبزر الكتان ومرهم الدياخيلون قد ذوب مع شىء من الأدهان ولطخت به الفتل وعالجت بها.
وملاك علاجها أن تروم بكل حيلة أن لا تلتحم على الهيئة التي كانت عليها أولًا. وهذا العمل الذي ذكرنا إنما هو بعض إصلاح الشرطة لا أن ترجع هيئة المكان على حسب ما كانت تمامًا،.
Chapter Fourteen.
في علاج الشرطة التي تكون في الجفن السفلي.
هذه الشرطة التي تكون من أسفل هي التي تسمى بالحقيق شطرة، وتكون طبيعية وتكون عرضية. فالعربية تكون من جرح أو شق أو كيٍّ ونحو ذلك.
The method is to take a needle with doubled thread and insert it into the flesh, passing it from the left jugular vein to the right jugular vein until the thread is at both ends of the flesh. Then stretch the flesh upwards with the needle and cut it with a broad scalpel.
If the eyelid returns to its proper shape, that is sufficient; otherwise, take a probe and place it at the site of the incision, then flip the eyelid with it. Make two incisions on the inner side of the eyelid, the ends of which should be at the corners of the cut you made, so they meet to form an angle. Until it comes together and takes on a shape similar to this shape:.
It is the Greek letter L, then remove that piece of flesh so that its sharp edge is lower than the eyelid. Then sew together the separated parts with two stitches using wool thread and treat it with the relaxing medicines and bandaging as mentioned until it heals.
And if the scar is wide from an incision, suture, or cauterization, it is necessary to make a simple incision without cutting the eyelashes as mentioned previously. Then separate the two edges with bandaging as described.
In summary, the treatment of a scar whether it is above or below should be done according to what you find suitable based on the appearance of the scar. It may vary greatly in shape, and the skilled craftsman will devise a way to manage it as best as possible.
Until the shape is restored to its natural form or comes close to it, and the disease itself will guide you on what treatment and tool is suitable in most cases.
Chapter Fifteen.
Regarding the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This adhesion occurs in many people, where the upper eyelid adheres to the white of the eye, preventing its easy movement and hindering its natural functions. It is necessary to insert a probe under the eyelid.
Lift it up or extend it with a pulley, then cut the adhesion with a very sharp scalpel, but not as sharply as when cutting other tissues to avoid disturbing the patient and potentially injuring the eye with its edge. Your cutting should be such that you remove the adhesion as if peeling it away.
Wait until the eyelid returns to its natural position and the adhesion is completely removed, then pour salty water into the eye to wash it or drop a solution of zanjari medicine diluted in water into it. Then use a cotton swab to separate the eyelid from the eye.
Place a piece of wool soaked in egg white over the eye. Starting on the third day, use ointments until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
Regarding the excision of nail tumors and removal of flesh from interdigital spaces.
A nail tumor can be of two types: either it is neural, resembling a hard, thin swelling, or it is non-neural, similar to solid white moisture that breaks into pieces if touched by iron or pulled with a pulley.
And no clamp is used in this. Both types begin at the maxilla and proceed little by little until they cover the eye and prevent light and movement of the eye, and the way to cut it is that the patient places his head in your lap.
Then he opens his eyes, and you raise the eyelid with your hand, then grasp the fingernail with a slightly curved clamp and extend it upwards. Then take a needle and insert a hair from horsehair or oxhair along with strong thread into it, bend the tip of the needle slightly, and pierce it in the middle of the fingernail.
Then pass it through with the needle and tie the fingernail with thread, extend it upwards, and peel off the side of the fingernail next to the pupil as if you are spreading it out with the hair until the end, then cut the rest at the base of the maxilla with a fine scalpel or small scissors, leaving the natural flesh of the maxilla.
To avoid continuous tearing from cutting it, you can extend the fingernail with the clamp alone or with thread as we said, then peel off the smooth edge which has this shape:.
Be careful that the scalpel does not touch the corneal membrane to avoid causing a tear and thus injuring the pupil. After cutting, apply some powdered salt or zanjari salve to the eye, then leave it for another day before treating it as necessary until it heals.
If the fingernail is not painful and you cannot insert a needle or clamp into it, this type of fingernail is often treated with medications and heals as described in the classification. If you wish to cut it, open the patient's eye.
Take a fine, smooth, weak-edged scalpel in this form:. Smooth the nail with it gently from above. If you see that it is loosening and crumbling, and there is an effect in it from smoothing, then drop some of your saffron medication or red medicine or powdered salt into the eye, and leave the eye for another day.
Then, repeat the process until it is all gone, unless a hot tumor appears in the eye, in which case leave it and treat the hot tumor until it heals, then resume smoothing it until it heals. As for the swelling of the flesh of the throat:
If the swelling severely irritates the eye, attach that swelling to a trap and cut some of it, but do not overdo the cutting so as to avoid causing tears, then drop red medicine or saffron medication into the affected area until it heals.
Chapter Seventeen.
On cutting wortmannia and what grows as excess flesh in the eye.
Red flesh may grow in the eyes of some people, overlapping until it covers the pupil or comes close to it or overflows onto the eyelids, and the eyelids may turn outward, resembling the rose of gallnut.
The procedure involves the patient placing their head in your lap, then opening their eyes, and you grasp all that red flesh with appropriate forceps.
Or you can hold it with a needle or a probe, then cut the flesh little by little until all of it is removed, being careful not to harm the eye during the procedure. Your cutting should be done either with the scalpel we described for cutting nails or with a small scissors. As you will see its illustration in the following chapter.
When the cutting is complete and all the bleeding has stopped, fill the eye with powdered salt or sprinkle red aloes or similar corrosive drugs. Apply a pad held in place by egg white from the outside to prevent inflammation.
If any bleeding persists and the blood overwhelms you, fearing inflammation, leave the eye and treat it with what soothes the swelling. Then return to work on it until it heals. Do the same for excess flesh that appears in the eye of this type.
However, you should avoid treating any weak eye by nature or appearance with iron instruments. Do not proceed with these treatments until you are certain that the organ can withstand such work.
Chapter Eighteen.
On Extracting Cataracts from the Eye.
Cataracts are red veins that form on the eye, impeding vision and weakening the eye over time. First, you should examine the eye; if it is strong and there is no other disease besides the cataracts.
Then you should proceed with the extraction. Instruct the patient to place their head in your lap, then clamp those veins with one or two forceps according to your skill. The forceps should be delicate and curved as shown in this figure:.
Or they may be double forceps in one body as shown in this figure:.
Then use a fine pair of scissors to extract those veins gently, and wipe the blood from time to time until you see with your own eyes that those veins have been removed and the blood has flowed. Be careful not to injure the eye with the tips of the scissors, and let your work be done halfway through the day facing the sun.
And be very diligent in your work so that you do not cut anything other than those veins. When you finish, drop some healing green or red extract into the eye to cleanse any remaining passages with its sharpness. If you cannot remove everything at that time, dress the eye with something that alleviates the pain.
Leave it for days until the pain subsides and you are assured that there is no inflammation. Then repeat this procedure in the same manner until the eye is healed of these symptoms.
Leave it [with the patient's head in the lap of a student and another wiping away the tears, examining the front and back of the eye carefully] and you [the surgeon] should hold the eyelid open with forceps that are either fixed or triangular.
Hold the eyelid taut, with the patient's head in the lap of a student and another wiping away the tears and examining the front and back of the eye. You have the option to start extracting veins from the top of the eye or from the bottom. It is preferable to place two ligatures on the upper side, one at the end of the eyebrow and the other at its beginning.
Hold them between your middle and index fingers, then place ligatures underneath corresponding to them. Hold them between your little finger and ring finger, lifting your hand slightly until you see all the passages have risen; then cut from the lesser outer side with scissors or a fine scalpel if necessary.
And insert the needle into the incision and clean all passages until it rises above the gum, but do not cut the nasal passage, for tears and blood will soften it.
Then cut with scissors what exceeds the circumference from above and below, so that nothing remains embedded except in the cornea. If possible, raise it from the cornea using an incision; this is better. It may become necessary for some people.
And when you cut it, the area will be inflamed for days, the pain will subside, and the hot swelling will diminish. Then repeat the procedure on it.
Chapter Nineteen.
On Replacing the Septum of the Nose.
The doctors call the septum "nasorus" when they treat it by cauterization or with a harsh, burning medicine as previously described. If it does not heal, the only remedy is to incise the abscess when it is ripe and remove all the moisture within or the pus.
Until the bone is exposed. If the bone is exposed and you see that it is diseased or black, then remove it with a tool whose shape is as follows:.
And it is called the rough-headed tool, made of Indian iron. Its head is rounded like a seed and engraved with fine engravings similar to those on scissors or razors. Place it on the diseased part of the bone and rotate it between your fingers while slightly tightening your hand until you know that the diseased part has been removed.
Do this repeatedly, then treat the area with drying and astringent medicines. If the area heals, flesh grows in it, and no discharge occurs for a period of time, and you keep it for forty days without it reopening or any incident occurring.
Know that he is cured, otherwise there is no recourse but to return the fistula to the nostril in this manner: Reopen the bone with an iron tool or a sharp medicine. When the bone is exposed, take a bifurcated probe of this form:.
Make the tip of the probe triangular, and its shaft conical, tapering to the tip as you see. Then place it on the bone itself; this should be near the throat, but keep your hand a little away from the eye. Then move your hand with the probe until it penetrates the bone and the patient feels it.
When he finds that air comes out when he covers his nose with his hand, at that moment apply astringent and drying medicines to the area as I have taught you. When the flesh grows and the place strengthens, the substance that was protruding outward will return inside the nostril, which will be easier for the patient, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
On the Treatment of Eyelid Protrusions.
If the eyelid protrudes without causing any harm or reduction in vision, it is advisable to ease the patient and then perform cupping on the occiput. Place a leech on the occipital area without any specific condition and let it take a light meal.
Then apply a poultice to the eye made of acacia, soapwort, benzoin, and storax. Then bind the eye from above with many bandages. The preparation of the bandages is as follows: Take a large amount of soft lint and fold each piece into four layers to make many bandages.
Then place them one on top of the other as much as the eye needs, then secure it with a strong piece of cloth tightly, and repeat the bleeding without restriction. Then remove the leech and leave the eye bound for a day and a night.
If the ligature becomes loose during this time, it should be tightened again. Remove the ligature after a day and a night as we said. If you see that the eye has improved, otherwise, repeat the dressing, medication, tightening, and bleeding until it improves.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On Cutting the Urethra.
If a hernia occurs in the urethral membrane and it protrudes outside the eyelids, resembling a grape, disfiguring the person's appearance, and you wish to cut it, then proceed as I describe.
This involves inserting a needle into the base of the hernia from bottom to top, then inserting another needle with a doubled thread from the side of the neck and passing it through, leaving the first needle in place.
Then cut the midpoint of the doubled thread, tie it around some part of the hernia along with the needle and tighten it firmly. Then remove the needle and place wet wool soaked in egg white over the eye, leaving it until the threads fall off with the hernia. Then treat the eye with what strengthens it.
Until the wound heals. The hernia may also be constricted from all sides with a circular bandage to increase its firmness, then treat it gently with a fine scalpel reaching deep into the eye to drain any excess moisture. Allow the eye to settle and shrink to its proper place, then bind it until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
في علاج الكمنة.
هذه العلة التي تسمى الكمنة إنما هي مدّة تجتمع في العين تشبه الماء النازل وليس به، ووجه العمل في ذلك أن تقعد العليل على كرسي منتصبا ثم تأخذ رأسه بيديك من الجهتين.
وتحركه حتى ترى المدّة تصير إلى أسفل بعينك ثم تثبت ولا تزول وينطلق النور ويرى العليل الأشياء كما كان يراها، وإن لم تنزل إلى أسفل علمنا أنّه الماء، فإن لم يتهيّأ نزول المدّة بما ذكرنا.
وإلا فأجلس العليل بين يديك ثم خذ مبضعا رقيقا وشقّ به فوق الغشاء القُرني قليلا عند اتصال الملتحم بالغشاء القُرني في الإكليل حتى تخرج المادّة.
فلما خرجت فقطّر في العين ماء حارا قد مزجت فيه عسلا أو ماء قد أغليت فيه حلبة وعسلا ثم تعالج بسائر العلاج حتى يبرأ،.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
قد ذكرنا أنواع الماء في التقسيم وأيّها يصلح للقدح بكلام مشروح مفسّر فتأخذه من هناك على الصحّة، فحينئذ ينبغى أن تجلس العليل بين يديك متربعا قبالة الضوء قرب الشمس.
Bind his healthy eye tightly, then raise the eyelid of the affected eye with your left hand if it is the left eye, or with your right hand if it is the right eye.
Then place the tip of the lancet near the cornea, at the thickness of a morsel in the white part of the eye towards the lesser sigmoid vein. Then push the lancet forcefully while rotating it with your hand until it penetrates the white of the eye and you feel that the lancet has reached an empty space.
It is advisable that the depth to which you insert the lancet should be equal to the distance from a child's pupil to the edge of the iris, for copper can be seen clearly through the cornea due to its clarity. Then move the lancet upwards to the area where the fluid is located.
Then press it downward repeatedly. If the fluid comes out immediately, the patient will see whatever is opened to his sight at once, with the lancet still in his eye. Then let him rest for a while; if the fluid rises again, release it a second time without removing the lancet.
When it stabilizes and does not rise again, gently remove the lancet while twisting it slightly little by little. Then dissolve a small amount of pure crystal salt in the fluid and wash the eye with it from the inside. Then place a wet piece of flax or wool over the entire outer surface of the eye.
Apply rose ointment and the white of an egg, and bind the healthy eye with it. As for us and people of our time, we place ground cumin mixed with the white of an egg on it. If the lancet refuses to enter the eye due to its hardness, as some people have very hard eyes indeed. You should take the scalpel known as al-birid, which has this shape:.
Then, with it, pierce only the conjunctiva and do not deepen the incision; rather, gently tap the area where you will make the puncture, then insert the probe as previously described. When your work is complete, prepare for the patient a secure bed to lie on, so that they can sleep on their back in a dark room.
He must avoid all movements and coughing. His food should be whatever softens his nature. He must not move his head left or right at all. The bandage remains in place until the third day, after which it is removed in that dark room, and then you test his sight.
Show him various objects, then replace the bandage until the seventh day. It is not advisable to do this during treatment or immediately after piercing, as it is best to avoid this before because excessive fluid can rise quickly due to intense looking.
If a hot swelling occurs, you should release the eye before the seventh day and treat it with what soothes the swelling until it subsides. Then, at that time, allow him to open his eyes and place a scarf over his face through which he can look for days while in that dark room, then.
He should gradually leave the house and proceed with his recovery. Know that the learner cannot do without observation when performing punctures, so he must proceed with caution. I have heard from some Iraqis that one of them mentioned making a perforating probe in Iraq that absorbs water.
I have not seen anyone in our country make such a thing, nor have I read about it in any of the books of the ancients. It is possible that this is a recent innovation. This is an image of various types of probes for those who are unfamiliar with them:.
It is made of copper and its tip should be of this thinness, triangular and sharp.
Chapter Twenty-four.
On the Treatment of Excess Tissue in the Nose.
Different types of excess tissue may grow in the nose. Some resemble long-legged scorpions, some are hard and cancerous-like, pale in color, and others are soft and not pale in color.
For those among these tissues that are soft, not malignant nor cancerous, the patient should sit before you facing the sun. Open his nostril and insert the probe into the tissue, then pull it outwards. Then cut what you can reach with a fine, sharp scalpel from one side.
Continue until you are sure that all the tissue has been removed. If there is any part left that you cannot cut, remove it gently with one of the fine instruments so that nothing remains. If bleeding becomes overwhelming or if a hot swelling appears,
counter it as appropriate. If it is a malignant tumor, act promptly and remove it until the bleeding stops and all the tissue is gone. Then after cutting, pour vinegar and water or a solution into the nose to allow the moisture to pass through to the throat.
Know that he has recovered, but if the moisture does not pass as it should, know that there is excess tissue growing on top of the porous bones which the instrument did not reach. In such a case, you should take a linen thread of moderate thickness.
And it is tied with many knots, and there is the length of a finger or less between each knot. The patient devises to insert one end of the thread into his nose with a probe or whatever means he can after shaping it like a button and pulls it until it reaches the nostril.
It is then brought out at the throat, and boys in school often do something like this. It is easy for one who wishes to do it. Then gather the two ends of the thread, the one that came out of the mouth and the other that remained in the nose.
Then use the knots in the thread to separate the flesh, do this until you know that the flesh has been divided by the knots of the thread. Then remove the thread and after wiping away the blood, insert a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment for three days or more.
Until the ointment consumes all that remains of the flesh. Then finally, insert a lead tube into the nose for days until it heals. If drying treatment is needed, use that.
This is a picture of the dropper used to administer oils and medicines in the nose:.
He places one made of silver or copper, resembling a small lamp with an open oil reservoir and its channel designed similarly.
If you prefer, you can make the tube closed like a reed and have the dropper's reservoir flat.
It has a handle at the end as you can see, which is held when oil or any juices or flowing substances are heated in it.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
في الثآليل النابتة في طرف الأنف.
كثيرًا ما ينبت في طرف الأنف ثآليل تعظم وتزداد مع الأيام حتى تقبح منظره.
ولذلك ينبغي أن تقصه في أول ظهوره وتستأصل جميعه ثم تحمل على الموضع إما الكي وإما الدواء المحرق الذي يقوم مقام الكي، فإن فات قصه حتى يعظم فانظر فإن كان متحجرا صلبا كمد اللون قليل الحس.
فلا تعرض له بالحديد فإنما هو ورم سرطاني وكثيرًا ما رأيت من قطع هذا الورم فعادت منه بليّة عظيمة على صاحبه، وإن كان الورم ليّن المجهس غير كمد اللون ورأيت أن القطع يمكن في جميعه. فاستفرغ العليل واقطعه بلا حذر ولا توقَّ وعالج الموضع بما يجفف ويقبض حتى يبرأ،.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
في خياطة الأنف والشفة والأذن. إذا تفرق اتصالها عن جرح أو نحو ذلك أعلم أنه متى حدث تفريق في اتصال أحد هذه الغضاريف قلت ما ينجع فيه العمل إلا في بعض الناس.
فينبغى متى عرض لأحد شئ من ذلك فانظر إن كان الجرح طريًا بدمه أن تجمع شفتي الجرح بالخياطة ثم تعالجه حتى يبرأ، وإن كان تفريق الاتصال قد افترق شفتاه وصار كل شق صحيحًا فينبغي أن تسعل كل شق بثخن جلده الظاهر حتى يدمى.
Then the lips are sewn together and tightened, and powdered mastic and benzoin are applied to them. A plaster of Aleppo ointment or another adhesive ointment is placed on top of the bandage. It is left tightly bound for two or three days, then it is loosened.
The dressing is changed, and it is left until the stitches come out by themselves. Then treat it with ointment until it heals. The method of sewing involves bringing together separated parts either with needles as described for sewing the abdomen or with thread as I have mentioned there.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On the Removal of Knots that Appear in the Lips.
Many people have small hard tumors inside their lips, some resembling corns and others varying in size. You should flip the lip and make an incision on each knot and extract it with forceps.
Cut it from all sides, then fill the area after cutting with powdered myrtle until the bleeding stops. Then rinse with vinegar and salt, and treat the areas with astringents until the wounds heal.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On Removing Excess Tissue from the Gums.
Excess tissue often grows on the gums, which Apollonius called "abulus." You should grasp it with forceps or hold it with a probe and cut it at its base. Allow any bleeding to stop, then apply powdered myrtle to the area.
And if one of the contracted dried pieces of flesh returns after treatment, it often recurs, so cut it again and cauterize it, for it does not return after cauterization.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On examining teeth with iron.
Crude and ugly scales may gather on the surfaces of teeth, both inside and outside, as well as between the gums. These scales can cause discoloration, turning the teeth black, yellow, or green, leading to decay that reaches the gums and thus making the teeth unsightly.
You should seat the patient between your hands, with their head in your lap. Then remove the tooth or tooth that shows scales resembling sand until nothing remains. Do the same for black, green, yellow, and other discolored areas until they are clean.
If what is inside disappears after the first examination, otherwise repeat the examination on a second day, then a third day, until you reach your goal. Note that the tooth may require various tools of different shapes and forms according to what suits your work.
Because the tool used to examine a tooth from inside is different from the one used to examine it from outside, and the one used between teeth has a different shape. Here are several types of tools that you should have prepared:
Chapter Thirty.
On Extracting Teeth.
You should treat the tooth for its pain with every possible method and hesitate to extract it, as there is no substitute once it is extracted. This is because a tooth is a valuable substance, so extraction should only be done when absolutely necessary.
It is necessary when the patient decides to have a tooth extracted that you be certain until the diseased tooth and its pain are clear. Often, the patient is deceived by the pain and thinks it is in the healthy tooth, so he has it extracted, but the pain does not go away until the diseased tooth is removed; we have seen this happen repeatedly with barber-surgeons.
When you are certain of the diseased tooth, it is then necessary to outline around the tooth with a moderately sharp knife so that the gums are separated on all sides, and then move it with your fingers or with delicate forceps little by little until it becomes loose.
Then firmly grasp it with the large forceps, and place the patient's head between your knees to stabilize it so that it does not move. Pull the tooth straight to avoid breaking it; if it does not come out, take one of those instruments and gently but firmly work under it from all sides as you did initially.
If the tooth is hollowed out or decayed, it is necessary to fill that hole with a piece of cloth and tightly seal it with a thin end of cotton so that it does not break when you grasp it with forceps. It is also important to carefully outline around the gums on all sides.
Be cautious with your effort so as not to break the tooth, for if you do, part of it may remain and cause greater harm to the patient than their initial pain. Beware of doing what ignorant barber-surgeons do in dealing with the front teeth, rushing to extract them without using the methods we have described.
Often they suffer great calamities, the least of which is that a tooth breaks but its entire or part of its root remains. Or it may be pulled out along with some of the jawbone, as we have seen many times. Then rinse the area with a solution or vinegar and salt. If bleeding occurs from the site, this often happens, so crush some colocynth and apply it to the area.
Otherwise, if the colocynth does not help, here are images of the delicate pincers used to move the tooth; initially, they should have long prongs, short handles, and be thick so that they do not bend when you grasp the tooth with them. These are images of the larger pincers:
They should have thick handles so that when you grasp them, they do not bend or yield; the prongs should be short, and they should be made of Indian iron or well-tempered steel. The tips should have serrations that fit into each other to provide a firm and secure grip.
The prongs can also be shaped like a scalpel, making them strong and precise.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On Pulling Out Tooth Roots and Extracting Broken Jawbones.
If the root remains after extracting a tooth and it is broken, you should place a cotton pad soaked in fat on the area for one or two days until the area relaxes, then insert the probe or pincers. which resembles the beak of a bird called balrajah, and this is the picture of crayfish:
It should be made like a scalpel from the inside or like an iscalfaj. If it does not suffice to remove these crayfish with this method, you must dig around the base and expose all the flesh with a scalpel, then insert the instrument that resembles a small lever, which is pictured here:
It should be short and slightly thick, but not too thin to avoid breaking. If the base comes out with this method, otherwise use these other instruments which are pictured here, the first one having a triangular end and some thickness:
Another delicate triangular image:
We may also use this instrument with two prongs, which is also pictured here:
And other instruments and tools that were mentioned earlier for extracting teeth. We may also use this instrument resembling a large fish hook, which is pictured here:
With a triangular curved end and some thickness to avoid breaking, and it should not be too thin. Know that the instruments for teeth are numerous, as are other tools which cannot be counted. The skilled craftsman, guided by his work and experience with diseases, may invent tools for himself according to what the work and conditions suggest.
Because there are diseases for which earlier practitioners did not provide specific tools due to the variety of types. If a bone in the jaw or one of the mouth bones breaks or becomes infected, examine it in its place with an appropriate tool or crayfish forceps mentioned for extracting roots.
And we make use of this poultice, whose appearance is as follows:.
It should have some thickness to control the bone so that it does not slip until the bone comes out, and we treat the area with appropriate medicines. If the bone has slight decay or blackening, remove it until it is clean, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Two.
On the Extraction of Teeth That Have Emerged Incorrectly.
If the teeth emerge from their natural course, they disfigure the appearance, especially if this happens in women and slaves. It should be examined; if a tooth has emerged behind another tooth, then appropriate action must be taken.
And if it is not possible to extract or reduce it, remove it. If it is attached to another tooth, cut it with this instrument whose appearance is as follows:.
It resembles a small beak and should be made of sharp Indian steel. The cutting should be done over several days due to the hardness of the tooth and to avoid disturbing other teeth. As for reducing it, use a file from India whose appearance is as follows:.
It is entirely made of Indian material, with a finely engraved part that resembles the file used to make needles. Use it to file the tooth little by little over many days gently so as not to dislodge the tooth and cause it to fall out. Finally, polish it and clean it with some scraper.
If a tooth is broken and causes discomfort to the tongue when speaking, it should also be filed until the roughness of the break is removed, making it smooth so that it does not hurt the tongue or impair speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
In joining loose teeth with silver or gold wires.
If the front teeth become loose or move due to a blow or fall, and if the patient is unable to bite on anything for fear of losing them, and if treatment with constrictive medicines has been unsuccessful.
The solution is to secure them with a gold or silver wire, and gold is better because silver can corrode and decay after days, whereas gold remains unchanged forever. The wire should be of moderate thickness, suitable for the space between the teeth.
The method of joining is to take the wire and insert its loop between the two healthy teeth, then weave the ends of the wire around the loose tooth or teeth until it reaches the other healthy tooth on the opposite side.
Then, repeat the weaving back to the side where you started, pulling gently and wisely until it is firmly secured. Tighten the wire at the base of the teeth so that it does not slip away. Finally, cut off the excess ends of the wire with scissors, tuck them together with wax, and hide them between a healthy tooth and a loose tooth.
To avoid injuring the tongue, leave it secured this way until it loosens or breaks. If that happens, secure it again with a new wire so that it remains in place for the rest of its life. This is an illustration of the teeth and the method of joining two healthy teeth and two loose teeth as you can see:.
A single tooth or two that have fallen out can be returned to their place and reattached as described, and they will remain in place. This is done by a skilled craftsman, who may also carve bone from the bones of an ox to make it resemble a tooth and place it where the tooth has been lost. It is secured as we said, and it remains in place, and one benefits from it.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On cutting the ligament that appears under the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament that appears under the tongue may be either natural, with which a person is born, or it may be acquired from a wound that has healed.
The procedure involves opening the patient's mouth and placing their head in your lap, then lifting the tongue and cutting that nervous ligament across its width until the tongue is free from restraint. If it has some hardness or complexity and is due to a healed wound, apply a probe and cut it transversely until the ligament is severed and the knot loosened.
Be careful not to cut too deeply into the flesh and sever an artery, which could cause bleeding. Then, after the cutting, rinse the area with rose water or vinegar diluted with cold water. Place a wick of flax under the tongue that the patient will hold each night.
To prevent it from rejoining a second time, if bleeding occurs, place powdered frankincense on the area. If the blood overwhelms you, treat the area with a convex iron heated for this purpose. Then treat him with all forms of treatment until he recovers.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On the Removal of a Toad Developed Under the Tongue.
A swelling resembling a small toad may occur under the tongue, preventing it from functioning normally; it may grow so large as to fill the mouth. The procedure involves opening the patient's mouth towards the sun and examining the swelling.
If you see that it is pale or black and hard, and the patient feels no sensation in it, do not attempt to treat it, for it is a cancer. If it tends towards white with moisture in it, use forceps to grasp it and carefully cut it open with a sharp scalpel, then remove it completely from all sides.
If bleeding overcomes you during the procedure, apply powdered zinc oxide to stop the bleeding, then return to your work until it is completely removed. Finally, rinse with vinegar and salt.
Then treat him with all appropriate forms of treatment until he recovers.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
On the Treatment of Tumors of the Tonsils.
Any tumors that develop in the throat, including glands inside the throat that resemble those outside and are called tonsils, if treated as described in the classification and do not heal.
Examine it; if the tumor is hard, pale, and insensitive, do not treat it with iron. If it is red in color and its base is thick, also do not treat it with iron to avoid excessive bleeding. Instead, leave it until it matures, and either it will subside on its own or burst spontaneously.
And if it is white in color, circular, and its origin is thin, then this is what should be cut. The procedure is that you observe before working on it whether the hot inflammation has subsided completely or there has been some reduction.
Then seat the patient facing the sun and place his head in your lap. Open his mouth and have a helper beside you who will press down his tongue with an instrument, which has this shape:.
Make it from silver or copper, thin like a knife. When you press down the tongue with it and the swelling becomes visible to your sight, take a hook and insert it into the lymph node, pulling it out as far as possible without pulling out any of the intestines along with it.
Then cut it with an instrument whose shape is like scissors, but its ends are curved, with each end very sharp. It should be made from Indian iron or Damascus steel:.
If this instrument is not available, then cut it with scissors of this shape:.
Sharp on one side and not sharp on the other. After cutting one lymph node, cut the other in the same manner. Then after cutting, gargle the patient with cold water or vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, gargle with water in which the peel of pomegranate or elm leaves or something similar that stops bleeding has been boiled until the bleeding stops. Then treat him until he recovers. Other tumors may also grow in the throat besides the lymph nodes, and you should cut them as mentioned for cutting the lymph nodes, similarly.
I treated a woman who had a tumor that had grown inside her throat, extending towards the esophagus with little sensation, which nearly blocked the throat. The woman was breathing through a narrow passage and could not eat or drink water.
She was on the verge of death if she had waited one or two more days, as branches from the tumor had emerged through her nostrils. I acted quickly and inserted a hook into one of them, then pulled it out, successfully removing a suitable piece.
Then I cut it where I could reach it from the nostril. Then I did the same for what protruded from the other nostril. After that, I opened her mouth, pressed her tongue aside, and inserted the hook directly into the tumor itself, then removed some of it without much blood loss.
Her throat was cleared, and she immediately began drinking water and eating food. We continued to remove parts of the tumor repeatedly over a long period of time, as the tumor kept regenerating what was cut. This process went on for some time.
I devised a strategy and treated the tumor inside her throat, which then stopped growing. She later left the area, and I do not know what happened to her after that.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
On Cutting a Tumor in the Throat.
If it descends to the throat and swells, it is called a grape if it is white and elongated, and a pillar if it is thick at the bottom and round. If it is treated as described in the classification and the treatment does not succeed, it is referred to as a grape.
I saw that the hot tumor had subsided in her, and she was thin; therefore, it should be cut. But if it is collected into a round mass and has no length, and if its color is like dough or black or insensitive to touch, then cutting should be avoided.
There are risks for the patient in this case. Therefore, when you see it as described with its whiteness and length, sit the patient facing the sun and clamp their tongue with the tool that was previously described, then insert the instrument into the swelling.
Pull it down and cut it with one of the two tools mentioned for cutting the tonsils, but do not cut more than what exceeds the natural amount, as cutting more will harm the voice and speech.
After cutting, use the methods described for treating the tonsils until they heal. If the patient is hesitant to have it cut, then use a cautery without fear or hesitation. The method of cauterization involves using a sharp medicine, placing the patient's head in your lap.
Then clamp the patient's tongue with the tool mentioned, then take the sharp water described in the chapter on cauterization and mix it to form a paste that is neither too thick nor too thin. Fill this mixture into the hollow of the tool, and here is its image:.
The end of the tool where you place the medicine should be hollow like a spoon. Place this tool with the medicine on the uvula itself, while the patient lies on their side so that saliva flows from inside their throat without dripping down to harm them.
Then grasp the medicine in your hand and squeeze it onto the sore for half an hour until you see it has darkened and the sting of the medicine has subsided. If you wish, take some cotton, wrap it around the tip of a rod, and dip the cotton in the medicine.
Insert the rod with the cotton into a tube from above until the cotton sticks to the sore. Do this repeatedly until you achieve what you desire in shrinking the swelling. Then leave it alone; it will wilt and fall off after three or four days. If you need to repeat the medicine, do so.
After cauterization, wipe around the swelling with cotton soaked in fat to clean the area of any remaining medicine. Then rinse with cold water and treat externally with poultices and internally with gargles until it heals. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and removes more fear.
The sore can also be treated with something gentler than cauterization and cutting, such as treatment with incense. Take fuzang, uzofa, sausar, sudab, shih, babuna, qisun, and similar herbs. Gather them all or some of them in a pot and immerse them in vinegar.
Boil the mixture with the pot covered in clay from the hilm (a type of clay), and make a hole in the middle of the lid. Fit an empty tube, made of silver or copper, over this hole as follows:
Insert the end with the bulb into the patient's mouth so that the steam rises to the sore along the tube until the sore is sufficiently treated. Repeat this several times until the sore wilts. Beware of performing this treatment at the initial onset of the swelling.
For often it increases in the tumor, and you should do this when its hot tumor subsides. If this instrument does not come to hand, take a reed and attach an eggshell to one end so that the patient's mouth is not burned.
Because the eggshell prevents the hot vapor from burning the mouth, and this is excellent in treatment and safe.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
On removing splinters and other objects embedded in the throat.
Often a bone or fish spine or something else gets stuck in the throat, so you should remove what is visible after pressing the tongue with an instrument near the light.
So that you can see what is in the throat. What is hidden and concealed within should be induced to vomit before the food digests in the stomach; perhaps the stuck object will come out with vomiting, or the patient may swallow a piece of flax or a radish root.
Or the patient may swallow a piece of dry bread, or take a piece of soft sea sponge tied to a thread and swallow it; when it reaches the location of the splinter, pull the thread quickly. Do this repeatedly.
Often it will adhere to the splinter or bone and come out. If it does not come out as mentioned, then use an instrument made of lead in this form:.
It should be slightly thicker than a pipe and have a curved end that the patient gently inserts into their throat with their head raised, being careful not to touch the vocal cords to avoid causing coughing. The doctor may also insert it by hand to push out the bone or splinter.
And introducing the patient is better for his knowledge of the position of the embedded object, and he pushes it downward or pulls his hand upward with the instrument; all of this according to what is prepared until it is removed.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
On the Removal of Embedded Leeches from the Throat.
If the leech is treated with the methods mentioned in the division of medicinal treatment and it does not succeed, then at that time look into the patient's throat when the sun is present after pressing his tongue with the instrument described to you.
If your gaze falls on the leech, then pull it out with a small hook or a thin and firm twig. If you are unable to do so, then take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat near the leech, then insert an iron rod heated by fire into the tube.
Do this repeatedly, and have the patient abstain from water for his entire day. Then take a container filled with cold water, open the patient's mouth to it, and gargle with it without swallowing any of it, and stir the water from time to time with his hand.
If the leech falls to the place when it senses the water, if it does not come out as described, then fumigate the throat with urine and hartshorn using the instrument described for laryngeal fumigation; do this repeatedly, and it will fall out.
The purpose of the fumigation work is to take a container with hot embers from the fire, and cover the container with a lid that has a hole in it. Then attach one end of the instrument to this hole, then place the fumigant inside, and have the patient place his mouth on the other end of the tube and seal his mouth so that the smoke does not escape until he knows that the fumigation has reached his throat.
For the fluke falls on the seat, if it does not fall then the incense is repeated several times. The patient waits for thirst and eats salted foods and garlic but does not drink water, so it must come out with this management. This is a picture of an instrument that attracts the fluke from the throat when it comes into view.
It resembles pincers as you see, except that it has this curvature which enters the throat, and its ends resemble the mouth of a bird; if it catches onto something, it does not let go.
Another instrument is used to press the tongue; it is a wide hoop of iron, large enough to fit over the mouth above the tongue. It is inserted into the mouth on its side so that the mouth remains open without the patient's will, and they are unable to remove it at all until it is taken off.
The description of the instrument used to open the mouth in exactly this manner.
Chapter Forty.
It deals with the discussion of binding tumors and incising them.
Tumors have many varieties, as will be mentioned one by one in this book, and they differ in binding and incising them based on two aspects. One of these is the nature of the tumor itself and what moistures it contains.
The second aspect is based on the body parts where they occur, because a tumor occurring in the head is different from one occurring in the buttocks, and a tumor occurring in a fleshy part is different from one occurring in a joint. Each has its own rule for treatment.
There are tumors that should not be incised until the pus within them has matured and fully developed, and there are others that should be incised while they are still immature and have not yet fully ripened, such as tumors that are close to joints.
Because when a tumor occurs near a joint and persists until it corrupts the surrounding area, it may damage the ligaments or nerves of that joint, leading to prolonged disability of that limb, or the tumor may be near a principal organ.
Because if you delay incising it until it matures, you will harm that principal organ. Or the tumor may be near the buttocks; therefore, you should incise it while immature. It is necessary to incise a tumor that is not fully mature when it is near the buttocks so that it does not腐烂并侵入臀部内部，从而形成瘘管。
It may result in a condition that does not heal completely. You should know the time to incise tumors that have fully matured, which is when the pain of the tumor subsides, the fever disappears, the redness and swelling decrease, the head of the tumor becomes soft, and other signs are evident.
It is advisable to make the incision at the lowest part of the tumor if possible, so that the discharge can flow downward more easily, or in the most tender part of the tumor. The incision should follow the length of the body if the tumors are near the hands and feet.
Incisions should be made in areas away from muscles, tendons, nerves, and arteries, generally in flat areas without bends. If the area has bends, the incision should follow accordingly. However, if the tumor is in fleshy areas, it is better to leave it unincised.
Until it is fully mature as we have said, for if you incise it before that time, the purulent discharge will flow longer, and there will be much soiling and dirt. And his lips and nostrils may become adherent, and some tumors may be incised on the surface of the body in emergencies or according to what the part requires.
It is appropriate to use a single incision for small tumors and a wide incision or many cuts for large tumors, according to the size of the tumor. There are also tumors that require the skin to be stretched and cut if it has become like a rag.
It has become to some extent dead, like what is seen in many abscesses and hidden tumors. Some of them require a triangular incision, and others are cut in the shape of a hazelnut leaf, such as an arbie tumor.
Some require a circular incision or a crescent-shaped incision, and similar cuts. For those that do not have a head, such as smooth flat tumors, it is appropriate to use only a simple incision. If the tumor is large and has been around for a long time, when you incise it, do not rush to remove all the pus at once.
Instead, remove some of it and then compress the tumor until another day, then remove some pus again. Do this repeatedly in stages until all of it is removed.
Especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an old man, for often the vital spirit dissolves with the sudden expulsion of pus, and the patient may die unexpectedly. Beware of this greatly.
After you incise these tumors, you should clean the wound and examine it. If the tumor incision is small or if there is a single simple cut, use linen bandages or old cotton. If the tumor is large and the cuts made during the incision are numerous, do otherwise as described.
You should insert a wick into each cut until they reach each other. If the tumor required cutting through some skin or creating a hole, you should fill it with old cotton or flax lint without moisture and keep it compressed for three days.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If bleeding occurs during the procedure, use cold water and vinegar after soaking a piece of linen in them and apply it to the bleeding area repeatedly.
If the bleeding persists, you should employ treatment and remedies as described in many places in this book and in the relevant sections. If it is winter and the area of the tumor has many nerves, you should moisten the bandages with warm syrup and oil.
Place them on the affected area. If it is summer and the areas are inflamed, you should apply bandages soaked in cold water and oil or in cool syrup and oil. As we said on the third day, you should then unwrap the tumor and clean it.
Use similar treatments for it until it heals. This is what you need to know about the general treatment of tumors. As for the detailed method, I have described each tumor how to treat it, categorized and summarized.
Chapter Forty-One.
في شق الورام التي تظهر في جلد الرأس.
تظهر في جلد الرأس ورماً صغيرة، وهي من أنواع السلح التي تحويها صفاقات كأنها حوصلة الدجاجة، وأنواعها كثيرة: منها شحمية، ومنها ما يحوي رطوبة تشبه الحمأة.
ومنها ما تحوي رطوبة تشبه الدشيش والحساء ونحو ذلك، ومنها ما هي متحجرة صلبة. وكلها لا خطر في شقها وإخراجها ما لم يعترضك عند شقها شريان.
والعمل في شقها أن تسبرها أولاً بالآلة التي تأتي صورتها بعد هذا والتي تسمى المدس حتى تعلم ما تحوي. فإن كان الذي تحوي رطوبة، فشقها شقاً على الطول شقاً بسيطاً على هذه الصورة:.
