They should be straight, sharp scissors with the screws for adjustment, and the length of the blades should be equal to the handles. As for the mistake that may occur during cleansing, it might turn the inner skin completely or partially when cutting; therefore, you should stretch it immediately with your fingernail before the area swells.
Cut it evenly. If you cannot hold it with your fingernail, pull it with a needle and cut it. If three days have passed and the area below the urethral opening remains swollen and red, leave it until the inflammation subsides, then peel it off gently.
Cut it as seems appropriate to you, but be careful around the urethral opening. If you accidentally cut part of the urethral opening, it does no harm; treat it with substances that promote wound healing as described in the chapter on powders.
If you cut more than the required amount of skin and it retracts upwards, this does not cause significant harm either. Treat it as we have described until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-Eight.
On the Treatment of Retained Urine in the Bladder.
Retained urine in the bladder is due to obstruction by a stone, coagulated blood, pus, or a growth, and similar causes. If you treat this with the methods of treatment described in the classification.
If the urine does not pass and you see that its retention is due to a stone damaging the neck of the bladder, then have the patient sit on his knees, then lie down with one leg over his back, arching his abdomen upwards, and hold himself still as much as possible.
At that time, the neck of the bladder will push the stone backward so that urine can flow out. If it does not flow out as mentioned and the patient's condition worsens, it is necessary to use a device called catheters, which have this design:.
It is made of silver and should be thin, smooth, and hollow like a bird's feather tube, about one and a half inches long, with a delicate nozzle at the end. The way to draw out urine with it is to take a folded thread and securely tie wool or cotton to one end.
Insert the thread end into the bottom of the catheter and trim any excess wool so that it fits inside the tube like a plug. Then lubricate the catheter with oil, butter, or egg white, and have the patient sit on a chair.
Moisten the bladder and urethra with wet lubricants or oil and warm water, then gently insert the catheter into the urethra until it reaches its base. Then bend the urethra upward toward the navel and push the catheter further inside.
Once the urethra is bent, push the catheter inside until it reaches near the buttocks. Then tilt the penis downward while the catheter is inside and continue to push it until it reaches the bladder. The patient will feel when it enters an empty space.
It is made this way because the urinary passage has curves. Then pull the thread with the wool tightly for a moment, as the urine will follow the wool. Then remove it and pour out the urine, and reuse the catheter.
Continue doing this until the bladder is empty and the patient feels relief.
Chapter Fifty-Nine.
How to Inject the Bladder with a Syringe.
And the instruments suitable for this purpose are made of silver or hollowed ivory, with a long tube as thin as pus. If you want to drop water and medicine into it, it should be done with an instrument called a syringe, and this is its shape:.
It is made of silver or hollowed ivory, with a long tube as thin as silk, entirely hollow except for the end, which is solid and has three holes: two on one side and one on the other side as you can see.
The hollow part where the tube is should fit snugly without any excess so that when you pull it, it attracts moisture, and when you push it, it pushes the liquid to a distance, as is done with a device used to throw oil during sea battles.
If you want to remove moisture from the bladder, insert the tip of the syringe into the moisture and pull with the tube upward; the moisture will be drawn into the hollow of the syringe. Then insert its end into the urethra as described for catheters, and then push the moisture out with the tube.
This moisture will reach the bladder, and the patient will be aware of it. This is also a delicate syringe used to inject the bladder:.
It is made of silver or spadarium, with the top resembling a small funnel, and below it there is a groove where the strap fits. Then take a camel's bladder, place the moisture you want to inject into the bladder in it, and tie it securely between the grooves with folded thread.
Warm that moisture slightly over the fire, then insert the tip of the catheter into the urethra. Then grasp the bladder with the moistened hand firmly so that the patient feels that the moisture has reached the bladder.
If a bladder is not available, take a piece of thin leather and make a circle from it. Pierce it all around and then insert a tight thread through those holes as it rotates. Then gather the circle together like folding a napkin after placing in it what you want of moisture, oils, or water.
Then tie it to the machine and use it as you did with the bladder by pressing your hand until the moisture reaches the bladder.
Chapter Sixty.
On the Removal of Stones.
We have mentioned in the classification the types of stones and their treatment with drugs, and I have described the difference between a stone formed in the kidney and one formed in the bladder, as well as all the signs of this. I also discussed the stones that require manual removal or surgical incision.
These are the stones formed in the bladder, particularly those that obstruct the urethra. I will describe this with a clear explanation and conciseness.
I say that the stone formed in the bladder most often affects children. One of its signs is that urine exiting the bladder is similar to water in thinness.
Rash appears, and it causes irritation and discomfort. It often hangs down and then spreads, causing the buttocks to protrude in many cases. Children can easily be relieved of this condition until they reach fourteen years of age, while elderly people find it difficult. For young adults, it is somewhere between these extremes.
And the one whose stones are larger is easier to treat, and the smaller ones are the opposite. When we proceed to treatment, it is first necessary to administer an enema that expels all the feces in the intestines, for it may prevent the detection of the stone during examination.
Then, take the patient by his legs and shake him downward to allow the stone to descend to the neck of the bladder, or have him jump from a height several times. Then seat him upright in front of you with his hands under your thighs so that the entire bladder is tilted downward, then examine him.
Examine him externally; if you feel the stone in the space, proceed immediately to incise for it. If it does not come within your reach at all, then smear the index finger with oil from the left hand if the patient is a child, or use the middle finger.
If the patient is an adult male, insert your finger into his rectum and search for the stone until it comes within your reach. Then move it slowly toward the neck of the bladder and press on it with your finger to push it out towards the place where you intend to make the incision.
Order a servant to press on the bladder with his hand, and order another servant to raise the scrotum with his right hand and pull down the skin below it from aside the place where the incision will be made. Then take the scalpel, which has this form:.
Make the incision between the rectum and the scrotum, not in the middle but towards the left side. The cut should be directly over the stone, with your finger in the rectum pushing it outward gently so that the external opening is wide and the internal part narrow enough to facilitate the passage of the stone.
Do not enlarge it, for the finger may press on the stone in the bladder when cutting, and it may come out without difficulty. And know that there may be stones with edges and corners that make their exit difficult for this reason.
And there are smooth ones that resemble acorns and rounded ones that easily come out. Those with edges and corners should be cut slightly more to facilitate their exit. If they do not come out this way, it is necessary to devise a method to grasp them with a tightly folded cloth whose end is like a blade.
He should secure the stone so it does not slip from his grasp. Or he may insert a delicate tool with a curved tip beneath it. If that is not possible, widen the incision slightly. If bleeding becomes excessive, stop it with cautery.
If there is more than one stone, push the larger one first towards the bladder's mouth and then make an incision on it. Then push the smaller ones after that. Do this similarly if there are more than two stones. If they are extremely large, it is unwise to make a large incision because it risks two outcomes for the patient.
Either he may die, or he may suffer permanent incontinence because the area will not heal properly. However, try to push them out until they come out, or devise a method to break them into pieces with forceps so that they can be removed.
When you are finished with your work, dress the wound with cotton wool and bandages, then secure it with a cloth soaked in oil and syrup or ointment made of frankincense and cold water to soothe the hot swelling. The patient should lie on his stomach, and do not remove the dressing until the third day.
When it dissolves, wash the area with a lot of water and oil, then treat it with the powdered frankincense ointment and the balsam ointment until it heals. If there is a hot swelling or ulceration in the wound or something similar, such as blood clotting in the bladder and preventing urination, which can be known by the presence of blood coming out with the urine.
Insert your finger into the wound and remove that blood, for if it remains it will lead to the deterioration and infection of the bladder. Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, and treat each type accordingly until it heals.
It is advisable at all times during treatment to bind the thighs together and bring them close to ensure that the medicines applied remain effective. If the stone is small and lodges in the urethra, obstructing the flow of urine, treat it with what I will describe before it reaches a point where incision becomes necessary.
This treatment often suffices without the need for incision, as I have experienced. It involves taking a tool made of steel, which looks like this: .
The tool is a sharp-pointed triangle attached to a stick. Then, take a thread and tie it around the penis below the stone so that it does not return to the bladder. Then insert the pointed instrument gently into the urethra until it reaches the stone itself.
Rotate the instrument gently against the stone bit by bit, aiming to pierce it until you can pass it through from the other side. Once this is done, urine will flow immediately. Then grip what remains of the stone with your hand outside the penis; it will break apart and be expelled with the urine, and the patient will recover.
If this treatment is not available to you due to an obstacle preventing it, tie a thread below the stone and another thread above the stone, then make an incision on the stone itself in the penis between the ties, then remove it.
Then loosen the ties and clean out the clotted blood that has formed in the wound. The thread is tied below the stone to prevent it from returning to the bladder, and the other tie above it so that when the thread is loosened after the removal of the stone, the skin returns to its place and covers the wound.
Therefore, when you tie the upper thread, you should lift the skin up so that it returns to its place after you finish and covers the wound as we said.
Chapter Sixty-One.
On Removing Stones from Women.
Stones rarely form in women. If a stone does occur in one of them, treating it is difficult for many reasons. One reason is that the woman may be unmarried, and another is that you cannot find a woman who would permit herself to be examined by a doctor.
If she is chaste or has relatives who guard her honor, the third reason is that you cannot find a woman skilled in this craft, especially manual work. The fourth reason is that the location for the incision to reach the stone in women is far from where the stone is, requiring a deep incision.
There is danger in that. If necessity requires it, you should seek a skilled female physician, but such are rare. If she cannot be found, then request a virtuous and gentle male doctor or bring a midwife who is knowledgeable about women's matters or a woman who can advise on this craft to some extent.
Open it and instruct her to do all that you order her to do regarding the examination of the stone, which is to look if the woman is a virgin; then she should insert her finger into her rectum and examine the stone. If she finds it, she should press it under her finger.
At that point, order her to make the incision. If she is not a virgin but a married woman, instruct the midwife to insert her finger into the patient's vagina and examine the stone after placing her left hand on the bladder and squeezing it well.
If she finds it, she should push the stone from the mouth of the bladder downward to the extent of her ability until she reaches the top of the thigh. Then she should make an incision opposite the midpoint of the vagina at the top of the thigh, wherever it is easier for her and where she can feel the stone.
Her finger should not leave the stone, pressing it underneath. Initially, make a small incision, then insert the probe into this small incision. If she feels the stone, increase the size of the incision as much as necessary for the stone to be removed. Know that there are many types of stones.
Some are small, some large; smooth, rough; long, round; with grooves. Recognize their types to guide you in what you seek. If bleeding overwhelms you, sprinkle the powdered zag on the area and hold it for an hour until the bleeding stops, then return to your work.
Until the stone is removed. Be sure to prepare for yourself the instruments mentioned for removing stones from men, as they will assist you in your work. If excessive bleeding overwhelms you and you know by the pulse of the blood that it comes from a severed artery, place the clamp on the area.
Apply the bandage tightly and leave it as such without retouching it, and do not remove the stone, for the patient may die. Then treat the wound; when the heat of the blood subsides after several days and the area becomes infected, return to your work until you have removed the stone.
Chapter Sixty-Two.
On Incising for Hydatid Cysts.
A hydatid cyst is an accumulation of moisture in the serous membrane, which is under the skin covering the testicle and is called the scrotum. It may also occur in a special membrane prepared by nature on one side of the testicle.
It may be mistaken for another testicle, and it occurs between the skin of the testicle and the serous membrane we have described, but this is rare. This cyst arises from a weakness in the seminal glands, which leads to the formation of this substance, and it can also result from an impact on the genital area.
This moisture can have many colors; it may be yellowish, reddish like blood, dark and blackish, or watery and white, which is the most common. The signs by which it is recognized are as follows:
Where the water accumulates, if it is in the serous membrane we have described, the swelling will be somewhat elongated, like the shape of an egg, and the testicle will not be visible because the moisture surrounds it on all sides.
If the moisture is in a special membrane for it, then the swelling will be round on one side of the testicle.
For this reason, a person imagines it to be another egg. If the moisture is between the skin of the testicle and the white serous membrane, then it can be perceived by touch. As for knowing the color of the moisture, probe the swelling with the square probe whose appearance was described previously; whatever comes out after using the probe is judged according to what is inside it.
When it comes to treatment with iron, you should order the patient to have bloodletting if it is possible for him and his body appears full. Then he should lie on his back on something raised slightly, and place many pieces of cloth underneath him. You then sit at his left side.
Order a servant to sit at his right side and extend the penis towards one side of the skin of the testicle and toward the side of the inguinal canal. Then take a wide scalpel and make an incision in the skin of the testicle from the middle along its length, close to the pubic region.
Make the incision straight, parallel to the line that divides the skin of the testicle into two halves, until it reaches the white serous membrane that contains it. Peel it carefully without cutting it, and peel more from the side that adheres to the egg (testis), as thoroughly as possible.
Then carefully and widely suture the serous membrane filled with water, remove all the fluid, then separate the edges of the incision with forceps. Extend the serous membrane upwards without touching the skin that encloses the testicle. Cut the serous membrane as much as you can, either in its entirety or piece by piece.
Especially pay attention to its thin side, because if you do not thoroughly cut it, you cannot be sure that the fluid will not return. If the testis protrudes outside of its skin during your work, after completing the cutting of the serous membrane, return it and then gather the edges of the skin of the testicle with stitches.
Then treat it as you would other wounds until he recovers. If the egg has become diseased with another illness, it is necessary to tie off the vessels in the scrotum to prevent bleeding, then cut out the testicle from the scrotum and remove the egg. Afterward, treat him with what we have mentioned.
If the water has gathered on both sides, then know that they are both hydroceles. Cut the other side as you did the first, whether it be necessary to treat them separately or if it is sufficient to treat them together, and then wipe away the blood.
Insert wool that has been soaked in oil or rose fat into the incisions. Then cover it with another layer of wool that has been soaked in a mixture of syrup and oil, and spread this over the testicles and the groin veins. Place a folded cloth on top, which is called a bandage. And secure it from above with a bandage that has six ends in this manner:
Then take two pieces of cloth, sew them together and fill them with loose wool as in this example. Make the ends that are used to tie it from cotton or wet wool. Gather them by pulling into a belt around the waist, and one end should be taken towards the buttocks to the back.
The second end should be taken towards the groin, and the other two ends on the thighs from below, and the remaining two ends on the calves. Gather all by pulling into a belt around the waist. This hydrocele can also be made by cauterization with iron instead of cutting.
This is done by taking a fine knife-like iron, and while it is hot, use it to cut through the skin of the testicle as described until the white serous membrane containing the water is exposed. Then take another iron like this:
It resembles the Greek eye, then wrap it with that sackcloth; it is hot until all the moisture comes out. Then patch the sack with small patches and peel off the layer containing it with an iron; cut as much as possible to remove it entirely and be careful not to let the fire touch the egg.
Know that this method is safer, farther from bleeding, and better than incision work. If an obstacle arises during the process of cutting or cauterizing, such as a hot swelling or bleeding, you should treat each issue appropriately with the appropriate remedy as previously discussed.
If the patient is timid and cannot endure this work due to its harshness, then let him use a poultice as I will describe it, and if it proves effective according to the evidence we have provided.
The discharge is watery; therefore, seat the patient on a high chair, then incise the swelling at its base with a broad scalpel. Apply a poultice along the length of the body, and make the opening wide enough for all the water to drain out completely. Then place cotton on the area and secure it.
Leave it to heal as the remaining water drains out, then treat it until the wound heals. If the water does not come out when you apply a poultice, it is because the white membrane is obstructing the opening of the wound, preventing the water from coming out.
In such a case, you should insert an eagle feather or the tool used to extract the water of hernias, whose image was previously shown, or increase the opening of the wound slightly.
Know that water may return and collect after six months or so. When it collects, apply the treatment in the same manner and defend the patient for his days.
Chapter Sixty-Three.
On Incising Fleshy Humps and Their Treatment.
Know that incising these fleshy humps is a dangerous procedure that often leads to death. Therefore, we prefer to leave them untouched and avoid them. I will now describe the procedures related to them and the types of tumors.
I say that many tumors may occur in the parts from which the genitals are composed, and this happens for various reasons: either due to excess moisture that flows to the genitals or due to a blow. The color of the tumor is the same as the body's color, and it does not contain water.
The tumor may be soft, or it may be hard like a stone, with no sensation. It can result from the complexity and swelling of arteries as previously mentioned, or from the swelling of veins. If it is due to the swelling of arteries, this can be identified by the dispersion of the tumor.
If you press it with your fingers, it should not be done at all. As for the one that results from the swelling of veins, nothing disperses when pressed with your fingers. This type may be incised, as well as other types of flesh.
This involves incising the skin of the scrotum, then lifting the testicle upwards and removing it from the white serous membrane. You should free the spermatic cord from the vessels, tie off the vessels, and cut the cord after freeing it from all sides around the testicle. If the testicle is attached to that growing flesh,
It is necessary to remove the egg and cut it open. If the adhesion is between any of the serous membranes or between the organs, it is necessary to free all of that adhesion and cut it in a circular manner.
If the growth of flesh is in the adhesion area that is behind, it is necessary to cut all of it and remove the egg as we have said. Once your work is completed, dress the wound with wool soaked in rose ointment and solution, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-Four.
On the Treatment of Hemorrhoids with Dilatation.
Dilatation is a twisted tumor, some of the twisting resembling a bunch of grapes, with relaxation of the sphincters and difficulty for the patient in movement, exercise, and walking. The treatment of this condition is closely related to what has been mentioned previously.
However, it is necessary to describe the procedure as performed by earlier practitioners. I say that you should seat the patient on a high chair, then insert a dilator into the anus and push it downward, then grasp the skin of the scrotum with your fingers along with the vessels that are close to the penis.
Another servant should hold it tightly and stretch it very hard, then make a wide, sharp incision along the vessels carefully until the vessels are exposed. Then peel them from all sides as I have mentioned to you in the peeling of arteries in the groin area, then insert a needle with doubled thread into them.
She should tie it at the first places where the drainage tube was shown and also at the last, then she should cut it in the middle along the length of the body and remove what has accumulated in it of foul, corrupt moisture.
Then, the incision should be treated like other wounds that require a period of time to allow the vessels that nourish one of the ovaries to atrophy; there is no harm in this. However, if the drainage tube affects all the vessels, it is necessary to remove one ovary with its vessels so that the egg does not lack nourishment. Before cutting the vessels, they wilt and cannot be used.
Chapter Sixty-Five.
On the Treatment of Intestinal Drainage.
This drainage occurs from a cut made in the peritoneum extending across the abdomen near the iliac regions of the abdominal quadrants, causing the intestines to protrude through this hernia into one of the ovaries.
This hernia may result either from a tear in the peritoneum or from its extension. These two types can occur due to many reasons, such as a blow, a jump, a shout, lifting heavy objects, and so on. If it is caused by an extension of the peritoneum, it develops gradually over a long period of time.
It does not occur suddenly, and the swelling is relatively flat in depth because the peritoneum constricts the intestines. If it is caused by a tear in the peritoneum, it begins with intense pain immediately, and the swelling appears visibly different under the skin nearby.
This is the intestine coming out and going to outside of the mesentery, and sometimes the appendix comes out with it; this condition is called intestinal and appendicular hernia. It may also occur with flatulence, and fecal matter may flow into the intestine and be trapped there, leading to the patient's death.
Because it causes severe pain and rumbling, especially when strained. Treatment of these conditions with iron is dangerous, so you must be cautious not to fall into this risk. The procedure involves instructing the patient to push the intestine back inside their body with their hand if it can be returned.
Then, he lies on his back between your hands, raises his legs, and you stretch the skin near the groin upwards. Then, cut through all the skin of the scrotum lengthwise, and sew the edges of the incision as needed to open it properly.
The incision should be as large as necessary to remove the testicle. Then, peel back the layers of the scrotum until the white, tough membrane of the tunica vaginalis is visible from all sides. At this point, insert your index finger where the testicle is located.
Between the white membrane beneath the skin of the testicle and the second layer, release any adhesions behind the testicle. With your right hand, fold it inward toward the scrotal skin, and with your left hand, lift the white membrane upward along with the testicle towards the incision.
Instruct the assistant to lift the testicle upward, and thoroughly release any adhesions behind it. Examine with your finger to ensure there is nothing like twisted intestine within the white, tough membrane of the tunica vaginalis. If you find anything, push it downward towards the abdomen.
Then take a needle with a thick thread that is twisted from ten threads and insert it at the end of the foreskin under the skin of the scrotum near the crease, then cut the ends of the thread's bend so that there are four threads. Then arrange some of it over each other in a stiff manner and tie the foreskin that we mentioned is under the scrotum's skin tightly from two sides, then wrap also the ends of the threads and tie them very tightly so that nothing from the vessels that nourish it can reach. To prevent a hot swelling from occurring, also make a second external tie that is slightly away from the first tie, less than two fingers' width, and after these two ties, leave some of the foreskin under the scrotum's skin, the width of a finger bone.
To prevent a hot swelling from occurring, also make a second external tie that is slightly away from the first tie, less than two fingers' width, and after these two ties, leave some of the foreskin under the scrotum's skin, the width of a finger bone. Cut the rest of it all in a circular manner and remove the testicle with it, then cut open the bottom of the scrotum's skin where blood flows as we described earlier, then use wool soaked in oil placed in the wound, and use the tie that we have described.
The white foreskin may be cauterized after cutting it to prevent bleeding, as this often occurs. The tie should be left until it falls off by itself; if its removal is delayed, it can be loosened with hot water and wrapped in something that will cause sweating until it falls off.
Then treat the wound with all the treatments for wounds until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-Six.
In this wind tumor, I have not seen anyone dare to treat it with iron. As mentioned earlier, it should be made as described in the case of the tumor associated with a vine, that is, the vessels should be tied off gently below the incision.
Then it should be incised in the middle and the tumor treated to open it until the vessels fall off, and the wound should be treated as mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-Seven.
Regarding the hernia that occurs in the groin.
A hernia may occur in the groin, as we said; it fixes the location and does not descend to the scrotum much, even if it does, the descent is slight and returns at all times.
However, if time prolongs the condition, the peritoneum tear increases until the intestines and fat descend into the scrotum, as we said, due to the extension of the peritoneum that exists in the groin. This is because the peritoneum extends, then relaxes, and the area bulges; its treatment is by cauterization as I have previously described.
It can also be treated with iron in this manner: the patient lies on his back before you, then make a transverse incision at the swollen protruding site, about three finger breadths wide, and then dissect the peritoneum layers beneath the skin.
Once the white peritoneum is exposed, take a strip and place it on the protruding area of the peritoneum, pressing it into the depth of the abdomen, then suture both protruding areas at the edge of the strip from the peritoneum, and attach them to each other with stitches.
Then pull one end of the membrane, but do not cut the serous layer at all, nor touch the ovum or anything else as I taught you in the treatment of intestinal anastomosis. Then treat it with surgical treatments.
When the sutures are removed, clean them and repair the wound until it heals, for the serous layer will contract and will not increase the size of the hernia.
Cauterization is better in this case because it is closer to safety.
Chapter Eighty-eight.
On the relaxation of the skin of the testis.
Often, the skin of the testis relaxes in some people without the underlying flesh relaxing, making it unattractive. Those who wish to treat this should have the patient lie down on their back and sleep.
Cut away all the relaxed skin from the thick skin, then bring the edges together. If you prefer to sew first, stitch the excess relaxed skin with three or four stitches and make sure they are secure, then cut between the stitches.
Then treat it as you would other wounds until it heals and the sutures fall out.
Chapter Eighty-nine.
On Castration.
Castration is forbidden in our Shariah.
For this reason, it should have been unnecessary for me to mention it in this book. However, I mentioned it for two reasons: one, so that the physician may know how to treat it if asked and understand the treatment for those affected by it; the other reason is that we often need to castrate some animals for our benefit.
Like goats, sheep, cats, and other animals, I say that castration is done in two ways: either by crushing or by cutting. The method of crushing works as follows: we sit the animal in hot water until its scrotum relaxes, softens, and hangs down.
Then you crush it with your hand until it dissolves and cannot be felt upon touch. As for castration by cutting, you should hold the animal and squeeze the skin of its scrotum with your left hand, then tie off the blood vessels and make a single cut on each testicle.
Once the testicles protrude, cut them after peeling them, leaving only the thin membrane that covers the vessels. This method of castration is better than the one done by crushing.
Because crushing might leave some of the scrotum, making the animal desire mating. Then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventy.
On the Treatment of Hermaphroditism.
Hermaphroditism in men is of two types. One type is that an appearance like a female vulva with hair appears either within the space or in the skin of the scrotum between the testicles, and urine may flow from it.
As for women, there is only one type, which appears above the vulva on the pubic region as small male organs, with one appearing to be like a penis and the other resembling the female genitalia. The treatment for all three types.
Two types of men and one type of women should have the excess flesh cut until its trace is removed, then it should be treated like other wounds until it heals. As for the second type of men from whom urine exits through the skin covering the testicles, there is no work to be done on them at all.
Chapter Seventy-One.
On the Excision of the Clitoris and Excess Tissue from the Female Genitalia.
The clitoris may increase in size beyond the natural measure, becoming prominent and unattractive in appearance. In some women, it can grow to a size similar to that of men and interfere with intercourse.
It is necessary to grasp the excess of the clitoris with your hand or a clamp and cut it, but do not overdo the cutting, especially near the base, to avoid excessive bleeding.
Then treat it as you would any other wound until it heals. As for the protruding flesh, this is flesh that grows in the mouth of the uterus, filling it up.
And it may protrude outward like a tail. And therefore, some early practitioners called this condition the "tail disease." It is necessary to cut it as you would the clitoris, and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Two.
On the Treatment of Hernias.
Hernia in women occurs when the female genitalia are not properly pierced or when the opening is too small. It can be either congenital, present from birth, or acquired due to a condition that has been previously mentioned. The acquired type may result from excess protruding flesh.
Or it is thin or thick, and it may be either deep in the uterus, or on its sides, or at the top or bottom. It prevents intercourse, conception, and childbirth, and sometimes it prevents menstruation. Knowledge of this is obtained by the midwife's observation.
If the obstruction is visible and close, then if it is not visible, examine it with her fingers or a probe. If the obstruction is made of thin membrane and is near the labia, proceed to puncture it by placing something like padding on the labia and then pressing with your thumbs from both hands together.
The woman should lie on her back with her legs spread wide, then forcefully stretch the labia until she punctures the thin membrane and opens the obstruction. Then take wool, soak it in oil, and place it on the area. Have intercourse with the woman every day to prevent the area from rejoining again.
If the membrane is thick and dense, it should be cut with a broad blade resembling an oak leaf. If the obstruction is composed of flesh growths, suspend them with forceps and cut them. Have medicines to stop bleeding without causing pain, such as squill, myrrh, and frankincense mixed with egg white.
Then use a lead tube that is wide to prevent the wound from healing too quickly, hold it for days. Use a dry linen wick and treat with other methods of preventing adhesion until healed. Another growth of flesh may appear in the uterus, so it should be cut in this manner.
Unless it is a cancerous tumor, a cancerous tumor in the uterus should not be treated with iron at all.
Chapter Seventy-Three.
Regarding the treatment of hemorrhoids, warts, and red boils that appear in the female genital area.
As for hemorrhoids, they are dilations of the veins to the point that they bleed profusely and continuously. When hemorrhoids become advanced, they turn into warts; I have mentioned their types and signs in the classification, and I will mention them here.
What is amenable to treatment and what is not, I say that hemorrhoids and warts if they are deep within the uterus and do not appear to the senses, then there is no treatment for them with iron. Those that are in the mouth of the uterus and can be felt by the senses are the ones that can be treated.
It is necessary to have the woman enter a cool room, then raise the warts with a needle or a rough cloth and cut them from their roots. Then apply one of the bleeding-stopping remedies when the blood flows, without chewing it.
Such as camphor, cyperus, frankincense, and similar substances. Then raise her legs against the wall for an hour, then let them down into cold water. If bleeding persists, seat her in a bath of sumac peels, pomegranate rind, lint, and similar ingredients. Then take ground barley flour, mix it with honey and vinegar.
Apply it as a dressing on her back. If the bleeding stops, otherwise continue with hard compresses to the heels and breasts without any conditions. Then take a piece of wool, soak it in the juice of rue or the juice of the sow's tongue or the juice of mallow branches mixed with syrup of lint, and apply it to the affected area.
When the swelling subsides, treat the area with ointment until it heals. As for the red pustule, it resembles the heads of hemorrhoids in rough appearance; therefore, cut what appears on its surface as I have mentioned regarding warts, and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Forty-Seven.
On the pus formation that occurs in the uterus.
Various types of tumors can occur in the uterus, such as cancer, calcified tumor, ulcers, fistulas, corrosive diseases, hemorrhoids, fissures, warts, and hot tumors. I have described all these diseases, their types, and their signs in the classification.
It is necessary to mention in this article the hot tumor that occurs in the uterus, if it is among those tumors that accumulate over a period of time. We should examine it; if the pain at the beginning of the tumor is severe with spasms, inflammation, and fever.
And if you see it red and it responds to touch, do not rush to incise it; treat it with what aids maturity until these symptoms subside. Then the woman should sit on a chair with two seats and lean back against it.
She should draw her legs towards the lower part of her abdomen, spread her thighs apart, place her arms under her knees, and bind them with a suitable strap.
The midwife should then sit on the right side and use the instrument used to open the mouth of the uterus, whose image will be shown later. If you intend to use this instrument, estimate the depth of the woman's uterus so that what is inserted by the instrument does not exceed its depth.
If the instrument causes pain to the patient, and if it is longer than the depth of the uterus, then padding should be placed on the edge of the cervix to prevent the entire instrument from entering the depth of the uterus. It is also advisable for the instrument that passes through to come from the upper side.
While you hold the instrument, the attendant turns the screw until the cervix is dilated. When the abscess becomes visible and can be felt by hand, and if it is soft and thin, then it should be incised at its head with a broad scalpel when the dilation is complete.
It is advisable to place a soft, waxed thread soaked in rose oil or green oil containing some astringent properties into the incision, with part of the thread remaining outside the cervix. Clean wool dipped in water that has been boiled with hyena skin should be applied externally on the uterus and the pubic area.
Then treat it with an ointment suitable for this condition after two days until it heals.
The uterus and the wound can be washed by injecting water and honey, then with water that has been boiled with the root of senna or long rubia. Then return to using the ointment. If the abscess is concealed inside the uterus.
It is advisable to avoid treating it with metal instruments; instead, treat it as mentioned in the classification.
Chapter Fifty-Seven.
On instructing midwives how to treat living fetuses if they emerge in an abnormal position.
The midwife should first know the form of normal delivery. One of its signs is when a woman feels an urge to lean downwards, desires to breathe fresh air, and finds it easy despite the labor pains she endures.
It accelerates the birth of the fetus. Know that this delivery occurs naturally, and the fetus comes out headfirst, with the placenta following or attached to its navel. When you see these signs, it is necessary to press her abdomen in order to hasten the exit of the fetus.
If it descends headfirst, the placenta will come out with it, and it should be thoroughly cleaned of any remnants. Any other form of delivery is unnatural and undesirable because the fetus may emerge feet-first or with its hands before its head and legs.
One of its hands or one of its feet may emerge first, or its head and hand together, or it may be folded up, or it may turn over onto its back, and so on for other undesirable forms. The midwife should be clever and cautious about all these things and beware of mistakes and errors.
I will explain each of these forms and how to handle them so that you can understand and apply them. If the fetus emerges headfirst but the delivery is difficult for the woman and she finds it hard to bear the contractions, and if you see her strength weakening, then seat her on a chair and instruct the women present to assist in holding her steady.
Pack her uterus with cooked fenugreek and moist fats, then the midwife should insert a small scissors between her fingers and use it to cut the placenta or scratch it with her fingernail until its moisture is released, and press the woman's abdomen to deliver the fetus.
If it does not descend, then the woman should be injected with fenugreek saliva mixed with shej oil. After the injection, instruct her to sneeze using a cane and hold her mouth and nose; the fetus will come out immediately. If one of the fetus's hands comes out first, gently guide them back little by little.
If entry is not possible, place the woman on a raised platform and raise her legs upwards. Then shake the platform on the ground while the woman holds on to prevent herself from falling. If the hands do not enter and the fetus dies, cut them off and pull out the rest of the fetus.
Or tie his hands with a cloth and pull them gently; he will come out, like the emergence of a fetus on its feet. If the fetus comes out on its feet, you should push them upwards then gradually turn the fetus little by little until it is straightened, then take one of its legs and extend it little by little.
When they reach the thighs, order her to squat and sneeze with camphor; it will come out. If it does not come out as described, then gradually return the fetus little by little until it is in its natural position; it will then emerge smoothly. If it resists despite all that has been described.
Take saliva from the onion and fenugreek, and dissolved gum; mix all these in a mortar well, then apply it to the woman's genitals and lower abdomen. Then have her sit in warm water until it reaches her pubic region.
When you see that her lower abdomen has softened, make a pessary from myrrh for her to hold. When she holds the pessary for an hour, have her sit on a chair and sneeze while closing her mouth and nose, then gently press her lower abdomen; the fetus will immediately come out, emerging on its knees and hands.
If it comes out in this manner, gently insert his hands little by little. Then have the woman lie face down on a bed with her calves hanging down, and clench her hands; then gently press above her abdomen little by little. Then remove what is under her feet from the cushions until she stands with her legs raised. If the fetus does not come out, take both of her legs and move them vigorously. Then squeeze above the cervix little by little until the fetus rises up. Then the midwife inserts her hand and straightens the fetus little by little.
Order the woman to squat until the fetus comes out, if the fetus is coming with one of its hands obstructing it, then guide back the hand. If she cannot guide it back, then make her stand and walk, or if she cannot walk, have her lie down.
Move her to her bed and shake her legs vigorously. Then use the method described with saliva applications. If she experiences contractions, seat her on a chair and work on guiding back the hand and positioning the fetus naturally. Make her sneeze and order her to squat until it comes out.
If the fetus comes out on its back with its hands stretched out and its face towards its mother's back, the midwife should grasp the fetus's hand and gradually turn it gently until it is properly positioned. Then apply the described ointment and saliva applications to it.
Then order her to move little by little. When she does that, seat her on the chair and order her to lean to the right side. Then order her to squat and make her sneeze with the kundus; it will come out, the fetus coming out on its side.
If it descends to the mouth of the uterus in this manner with the placenta, then tear the placenta with your fingernail until it is free from moisture. If it adheres to the face and neck of the fetus, cut it from the navel so that he does not suffocate and die. Then push the fetus inside until it is properly positioned.
Instruct the woman to squat several times, for it will come out easily, like the birth of twins or many fetuses. Know that twins are often born alive, and three or four may be born and live, but this is rare; as for five, it is beyond nature and they do not live at all.
And they are enclosed by a single placenta, even if there are many of them, and they are separated by a membrane barrier attached to each one's umbilical cord. They may be born normally and easily as a single fetus would, or they may be born in a problematic manner as described.
If some of them experience difficulty during birth and become deformed as we have described, treat them as instructed. Let the midwife be gentle and act with care and deliberation, employing whatever means she can to achieve safety for each case.
Describe what may be conceived in the womb and later miscarried. One, two, three, four, five, six, seven, or more than ten may be conceived. It has been verified to my knowledge that a woman miscarried seven, and another miscarried fifteen, all of them conceived together.
By the decree of the Almighty and All-Knowing, all of this should be within the knowledge of the midwife, for she may one day encounter such a situation and must be prepared to deal with it appropriately.
Chapter Sixty-Seven.
On the Removal of a Dead Fetus.
If you have treated the dead fetus as described in the previous section but it does not come out, and circumstances compel you to proceed with manual removal, then examine the woman. If she is strong and there are no signs that might indicate harm to her, she should lie down on her back on a bed.
Her head should be tilted downward and her legs raised, with a servant controlling her on both sides or she may be secured to the bed so that her body does not pull when the fetus is being delivered.
Then moisten the inside of her uterus with wet ointments mixed with saliva from the khitan plant, fenugreek seeds, and flaxseeds. The midwife should then apply this mixture to her hand and gently insert it into the uterus. She should then locate a spot where she can fix the ropes attached to the fetus, in places suitable for doing so.
Observe, if the fetus is positioned to descend head-first, then fix the ropes in its eyes or at the back of its head or in its mouth or on its palate or under its chin or in its temples or near its ribs or underneath its cheeks.
If, on the other hand, it is positioned to descend feet-first, then fix the ropes in the groin and middle ribs. It is important to hold the rope with the right hand and place it between the fingers of the left hand, gently inserting the hand and fixing a rope in one of those places we have mentioned.
Until she reaches an empty space, then fix another rope opposite it or a third one if necessary to ensure that the pull is even and does not tilt in any direction. Then stretch it evenly; however, stretching should not be straight alone but should also move in all directions.
To facilitate its exit, as is done with a tooth when extracting it. Between this process, the stretch should be loosened; if there is resistance from any direction, the midwife should apply ointment to some fingers and insert them in that direction to turn what is resistant. If part of the fetus comes out, then move the ropes to other positions.
It is slightly higher; do this until the fetus comes out entirely. If a hand emerges before anything else and cannot be returned due to constriction, wrap it with a cloth and pull it out until it comes out completely, then cut it off.
Whether it is in the wrist joint or in the elbow, do this for the other hand and for both feet. If the fetus's head is large and there is difficulty in its emergence due to pressure, or if water has accumulated in its head, you should insert between the fingers a sharp scalpel.
Cut open the head with it to let out the water, or use an instrument called a miskadakh, whose image is shown along with other instruments in the section that follows.
Do the same if the fetus has a naturally large head; you should cut open or crush the skull with the miskadakh as we have said, then remove the bones with forceps. If the head emerges and is constricted at the clavicle, it should be cut until the moisture in the chest drains out.
Then the chest will join together. If the chest does not close and delivery is impossible, cut as much as you can. If there is swelling below the abdomen or if it is congested, you should incise it to allow moisture to drain out.
If the fetus is positioned with its feet first, pulling it out is easy and adjusting it to the mouth of the womb is simple. If it is constricted at the abdomen or chest, you should pull it out with a cloth wrapped around your hand and cut open the abdomen or chest so that what is inside can drain out.
If all the members are removed and the head is retracted and obstructed, then the midwife should insert her left hand. If the mouth of the womb is open, she should insert her hand into the cervix and search for the head with it, and pull it with her fingers towards the mouth of the womb, then insert a cannula or two cannulas and pull it with them.
If the mouth of the womb has closed due to a hot swelling, it should not be forced. Instead, at this time, it is appropriate to apply wet, fatty substances and use dressings, and sit in water that relaxes and moistens.
And if the fetus is on its side, then if it can be straightened, use what we mentioned for a living fetus. If that is not possible, cut it in pieces and remove it; nothing of the placenta should be left inside at all. Once you are done with your treatment, apply what we have mentioned regarding the hot swellings that occur in the womb.
If bleeding occurs, then she should descend into binding waters and use all other treatments. I have seen a woman who was pregnant, her fetus died inside her, and then she became pregnant with it again.
Then the second fetus also died, and after a long time, a swelling appeared in her lower abdomen and it swelled until it burst, releasing pus. She was called for treatment, and I treated her for a long time, but the wound did not heal, so I applied one of the strong drawing ointments to it.
A bone then came out from the place, and after a few days, another bone came out. I was amazed by this since there are no bones in that part of the body, so I assumed they were from the dead fetus's bones. Upon examining the wound, I removed many bones, which were from the head of the fetus.
I continued this until I extracted many bones, and the woman was in the best condition. She lived like this for a time, with pus discharging from the wound, and that is why I mention this rare case here.
因为她包含知识和对医生在治疗中用手进行操作的援助。
