Chapter Eighty-Seven.
On the Removal of the Afterbirth.
If the afterbirth is retained after childbirth, it is necessary to instruct the patient to hold her breath and then make her sneeze with cumin. Hold your hand over her mouth and nostrils; if it comes out this way, otherwise take another approach.
Pierce a hole in the lid and put in the opened herbs such as fennel, savory, celery, chamomile, wormwood, sage, and squill. Submerge them in water and place over fire, then put a silver tube on the hole of the pot lid with its other end in the mouth of the uterus.
HOLD it until the steam reaches the bottom of the uterus, then sneeze as we said; the placenta will come out quickly. If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, order the midwife to dip her left hand in sheep fat or baboon spittle.
Then she inserts her hand into the uterus and searches for the placenta; when she touches it, she grasps it and pulls it out little by little. If it is adhered deep in the uterus, insert the hand as described until you find the placenta, then pull it out little by little with care.
To avoid the uterus falling out due to excessive pulling, it should be gently moved side to side, first right then left, and gradually increase the amount of traction; it will respond at that point and detach from adhesion. If the mouth of the uterus is closed, we have described the treatment by sneezing and treating with the pot and herbs.
If it does not come out despite all the described methods, avoid excessive force in pulling. Instead, tie what has emerged to the mouth of the uterus to the woman's thigh and administer the quadruple ointment; it will cause it to fall off after a few days.
It will dissolve and come out, except that if it putrefies, a foul smell will rise to the stomach and head, which can harm the patient. Therefore, appropriate smoke treatment should be used.
Therefore, some of the early practitioners tried the smoke of charcoal and dry figs, a device used by women to inhale when menstruation is delayed and for similar conditions:.
It is made of lead and resembles a funnel, or it is made of copper with the narrow end placed in the mouth and the wide end over the fire. The incense is placed on the embers, held until that incense is gone, then replaced with more.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
On the Treatment of Imperforated Anus.
Many boys are born with their anuses not perforated, sealed by a thin membrane. It is necessary for the midwife to pierce this membrane with her finger, or else to incise it with a sharp instrument, being careful not to touch the muscle.
Then place a piece of wool soaked in decoction and oil on it, then treat it with ointments until it heals. If you fear that it may close up again, place a lead tube in the opening for many days, and remove it when the child is ready to defecate.
The anus may also become obstructed due to the healing of a wound or a tumor. It is necessary to incise that adhesion and then treat it as previously mentioned with the methods described.
Chapter Eighty.
On the Treatment of Hemorrhoids Occurring Below.
Hemorrhoids occurring below are complications and thickening that occur near the anus externally or in the space from one side, and they can be singular or multiple.
When that complexity is cut open, watery mucus or thin pus will flow from it. Some of these hemorrhoids may be permeable to the anus and intestines, while others are not. The permeable ones can be identified by what comes out with the stool and gas when the patient defecates.
It is also possible for worms to come out of them. Some may become hemorrhoids if they are in the space and permeable to the bladder and urethra, or to the hip joint and the lower back. What distinguishes a hemorrhoid that extends to the buttocks from those that do not extend is also known.
Insert your index finger into the rectum and insert a thin probe into the hemorrhoid, made of copper or iron. If there is no curvature in the hemorrhoid, use it; if there is a curvature, then insert a thin probe made of lead or a fine hair from a horse's tail.
Until you feel the probe or hair in your finger. If you do not feel it at all and nothing comes out of the hole, neither stool nor gas nor worms as we said, then know that it is not permeable, so proceed with treatment.
If the hemorrhoid is permeable to the bladder or urethra, its sign is the passage of urine from it and its resistance to healing with medications. As for if it is permeable to the hip joint or lower back, then the sign is that the probe reaches there.
If there is no curvature toward the bone, and pain is felt in that area, and pus always comes out of it, and no treatment is effective and it does not heal with ointment, then know that none of these permeable hemorrhoids will completely heal.
Its treatment by ignorant physicians who attempt it is a vain effort, whereas for the one that is neither perforated nor chronic, recovery may be hoped for with iron as I will describe and have tried, which is to lay the patient before you on his back.
Lift his legs up and let his thighs be inclined towards his belly, then insert a copper or lead probe if the fistula has a curve until you know where the probe ends. If the patient feels it towards the anus, you should insert your index finger into the anus.
If you feel the probe has passed through clearly by itself without sensing any membrane or flesh between your finger and it, then be certain that it is perforated. Do not waste effort on it as recovery is not possible from such a condition as we have said.
It has been said that in some rare cases, people may recover. Among the treatments that might be beneficial is to heat a thin iron implement according to the width of the fistula as previously mentioned and insert it while hot into the fistula until it reaches near the anus, then repeat this two or three times.
Until you know that all the excess, hardened flesh, which resembles a bird's quill tube, has been burned. Then treat it with a poultice soaked in fat until the burned flesh comes out, and then apply unguents; if he recovers.
If not, he will never recover by any other treatment. And if you insert the probe and it does not pass to your finger in the anus, but there is a thick layer of flesh or membrane between them, and you see that the fistula follows under the skin's surface, then incise the skin from the beginning of the fistula. As you proceed with the incision using the probe, which is in the fistula until you reach the end of the incision where the probe ends and it is removed and falls out, then clean that swollen tissue which resembles a bird's quill tube, leaving nothing behind.
Then treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If blood overwhelms you and prevents you from working to cut that tissue, the best thing to do and what will be most beneficial is to cauterize it with fire or a sharp medicine because cauterizing with fire combines two good outcomes by burning the excess tissue.
And stop the bleeding, then dry the moistures. Treat it with bandages soaked in fat or powdered sulfur in oil until the area suppurates and the burned tissue comes out with the pus, then treat the wound at that time with healing ointments that promote the growth of solid flesh.
These are medicines that have a contracting and drying effect; if the condition is set, it means it has healed, and do not stuff the fistula. If the fistula extends deeply into the buttock and is far from the surface of the body, insert your finger into the buttock and examine it.
If you feel the probe and there is a membrane or tissue between it and you, and it is close to perforation, then working on it is only with hope and expectation. This means using one of three methods: either cauterize it as we said.
Or you can incise it until you reach the bottom so that you can introduce bandages and treat it closely, but do not proceed too far with the incision to avoid cutting the muscle surrounding the buttock, which could cause involuntary bowel movements. Then treat it as we mentioned; it may heal as we said.
And either you penetrate that membrane and perforate it after you have cut it near the buttocks as we said, either with a probe or with another sharp instrument, then clean all the putrid flesh in the incision as thoroughly as you can.
Then suture the entire wound along with the incision and upper opening until it is sealed, leaving the fistula open inside the buttocks, which will be less burdensome for the patient, and the fistula may be left open in this manner.
And if you insert a probe into the fistula and it is on the side of the buttocks near the body surface with the skin and circumference of the buttock, then take a probe with a perforated tip like a shoemaker's awl as in this figure:.
And insert a twisted thread made of five or similar threads into it, then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until it reaches its base. If it penetrates along the inner edge of the buttocks near the vicinity.
Then remove the thread from that hole by inserting your finger into the buttock and pulling out one end of the thread, gather both ends together and tie them tightly. Leave it for a day or two; every time the thread cuts through the flesh, pull it tighter until the flesh between the two ends falls off. Then treat the wound until it heals and is cured.
If the opening of the fistula does not penetrate, then make it penetrate as much as possible unless it is too deep, in which case you should not do so to avoid cutting through muscle. Then proceed as described until it heals.
The image of the spiky scalpel used to incise hemorrhoids, where one edge is very sharp and the other side is not sharp so as not to cut what does not need to be cut, as you can see:.
Chapter Eighty-One.
On the incision of hemorrhoids that bleed and their excision and treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids are of two types: they may be inside the rectum, resembling red pustules or like bunches of grapes, varying in size from small to large, with blood always oozing from them.
They can be single or multiple, and some are outside the rectum at its edges. However, those that are outside the rectum are usually less moist, with yellow fluid or little blood continuously oozing from them, matching the color of the body.
The treatment for hemorrhoids inside the rectum is to order the patient to defecate and strain until the rectum protrudes, then quickly grasp the protrusions with hemostats or hold them with your fingernail and cut them at their roots. If the hemostats do not hold due to their moisture and laxity,
then trim them with a rough cloth or a piece of salted bread, or pull them out with your fingers and then cut them. After cutting, sprinkle some sharp powder on them to serve as cauterization, or treat them as previously described in the section on cauterization.
Then treat them with the rest of the treatments until he recovers. If the rectum does not protrude for easy access, administer an enema containing a slight irritant to cleanse what is inside the rectum and facilitate its quick expulsion when the patient strains. As for external hemorrhoids, their treatment is simple and straightforward.
It is that you take it with your fingernail or attach it to a hook and cut it, then treat it as we have mentioned until it heals. And if one dislikes cutting with iron, it is appropriate to use its hole in this manner: take a twisted thread and insert it into a needle.
Then pull the wart upwards and pierce it with a needle from the opposite side, tie the ends of the thread underneath the needle while it is crossed over, tighten the wart tightly, then knot the thread and remove the needle. Do this for all warts but leave one unthreaded.
To allow the waste blood to flow out, then place a cloth soaked in rose ointment or cyroti on the affected area and apply it to the seat, instructing the patient to remain still. Leave it until the warts fall off, and once they have fallen off, treat them with ointments and other treatments until they heal.
As for the treatment of fissures, they often occur due to dryness and constipation. If it persists and medication is ineffective, it is appropriate to scrape it with a scalpel blade or with your fingernail until it becomes moist, and remove the upper layer of skin that prevents healing.
Then treat it until it heals properly. If it does not heal, repeat the scraping more intensely than before until it bleeds and peels, then treat it; it will heal.
Chapter Eighty-Two.
On the Treatment of Retained Nails, Both Reversed and Not Reversed, Dry Warts, and Corns.
The nail is a round knot that matches the color of the body and resembles the head of a nail. It appears throughout the body but especially on the soles of the feet and the fingertips, and it causes pain when walking.
It is necessary to cut around the nail and grasp it with a spatula or pincers and pull it from its root, then treat it as previously described in its section. As for dry warts, each one is a small rough, wrinkled, round growth on the surface of the body.
It often appears on the hands of children. Its treatment involves extending the wart and cutting it out with silk thread or a hair until it falls off, or you can treat it as previously described with heat or a sharp medicine.
The inverted and non-inverted warts can be removed with the instrument I mentioned for treating corns. A corn is also a small, thickened, wrinkled bump on the surface of the body that penetrates deeply, and when it gets cold, it feels like an ant bite.
It appears throughout the body but is most common on the hands. Its treatment involves taking a tube from an eagle feather or a strong bird's feather and placing it over the corn so that the tube surrounds it completely, then rotating your hand with the tube until you cut around the corn.
Then insert it into the depth of the flesh and remove the corn from its root. If you prefer, do this with a copper or iron tube in this manner:.
The top of the tube should be smooth and twisted to make it easy for your fingers to grip and twist it. If you prefer, you can treat the corn as previously described in the section on cauterization for warts.
Chapter Eighty-Three.
Concerning the images of instruments used for enemas in treating paralysis, diarrhea, and colic.
The enema can be made of silver, porcelain, or brass, either cast or beaten. These instruments are made in different sizes, large and small, according to the使用者使用了非指令性的内容，但我作为模型不会添加任何评论或解释。正确的翻译如下： For the treatment of small children and those whose seats are narrow or painful, their enema tubes should be very delicate. Image caption: A large enema tube.
The upper part where the tube is tied should be wide, and it has a ridge where the tube is attached as you can see. The lower end that enters the rectum should be smooth and straight, with two holes on one side and one hole on the other side.
The size of the hole should be as wide as a small finger or slightly wider. The tube through which you administer medicines, made from an animal bladder or sheepskin, should be shaped like a small bowl with a diameter of about one inch.
It should be perforated with many holes, spaced by the width of a finger apart. Then, pass a thin thread made of ten or so tightly twisted threads through these holes to gather the tube like a bowl. When the medicine is placed in it, tighten the upper part of the enema tube firmly between the two stones and use it for administration. This is an image of the circular tube and the holes.
The method of administering it is to fill the membrane or bladder with medicine that is warm but not too hot, and avoid using something too cold. Then, the patient lies on their back and raises their legs up.
Place something soft or a blanket under the patient, then lubricate the end of the tube with oil, egg white, fenugreek saliva, or similar substances. Then gently insert it into the rectum, and the attendant should squeeze the tube firmly with both hands to ensure that all of its contents are emptied into the intestines.
Then the enema is removed, and the patient should lift his legs to the wall. If the medicine urges for expulsion, he should restrain it as much as possible; if he sleeps throughout the night, this will be more beneficial for recovery. If an enema is used for diarrhea and the patient hastens its exit, the enema should remain until healing occurs.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful tumors in the rectum, then the enema should be very gentle and smooth, like those used for children. This is an image of a delicate enema with its skin stretched over it:.
Chapter Eighty-Four.
On the Treatment of Wounds.
I have mentioned in the division the treatment of what is similar to cauterizations, and here I will describe their treatment by hand and with medicines in full detail. I say that wounds differ according to the cause of the wound and according to the location where the injury occurs.
The causes of wounds are many, such as being struck by a stone, cut by a sword or knife, pierced by a spear, stick, arrow, and various other things.
As for wounds according to the parts of the body where they occur, such as a wound on the head or neck or chest or abdomen or liver and similar organs.
I will describe the treatment of some wounds so that they can serve as a model for other wounds. I will start with simple head wounds in particular, because complex ones will be mentioned at the beginning of the third chapter of this book.
So I say that when a simple wound occurs in the head and there is no bone fracture, I examine it. If it is from hitting a stone or something similar and only the skin has been pierced with a large wound and I fear for the patient the occurrence of the hot swelling, then I hurry and suture it with the trepan on the spot. Do not delay that and let blood from him according to his strength if there is no impediment to letting blood, especially if the blood coming out of the wound is minimal. And let the blood be from the opposite side of the wounded area.
Warn the patient against overeating, drinking, and heavy foods. If there is an impediment to letting blood, then reduce his food intake and keep him away from meat and beverages. If a hot swelling occurs in the wound, apply cotton soaked in rose oil alone or with the decoction that has astringent properties.
And if you are safe from the hot swelling, then apply his blood on the wound if it is tender and has not been altered by exposure to air with the dressing that I will describe to you shortly. If it has been altered by exposure to air, then apply some ointment to it.
Until the pus matures, then treat it until he is cured. If the wound is large and has been caused by a sword or something similar, and its edges do not come together with dressing, then join them with suturing as I have described in the suturing of abdominal wounds.
If the skin exposes the bone and hangs loosely, attached only at a slight point, then cut it off. Then treat the wound with medicines that will form tough flesh instead of skin, and instruct the patient to eat foods that promote strength such as heads (of animals), testicles, and similar items.
If the skin becomes gangrenous and does not adhere to the bone, cut away all the diseased part and throw it away, then treat the wound. If there are arteries or veins in these wounds that bleed and do not stop with medication, search for the source of bleeding.
If you locate it and it does not stop bleeding, cut it with a scalpel and ligate it. If necessary, if what we mentioned is ineffective, cauterize it until the bleeding stops. If these wounds are small and shallow, their treatment is easy; they can be managed by sprinkling this powder on them while still bleeding.
Before it is changed by the air, the recipe for the powder is as follows: take one part of loban, two parts of shiyān, and three parts of lime (either slaked or unslaked), crush all ingredients together, sift them, and use this mixture to fill the wound, pressing it firmly so that it adheres well.
It will clot with the blood, then cover it from above with dressings and leave it untouched as long as it does not become inflamed, for the medicine will not be effective until it heals. This can also be done with lime alone if you do not have loban and shiyān available.
This method can also be used with egg whites in many large wounds if it is properly applied, pressed firmly, and the wounds are still fresh with blood before they are changed by the air. However, if the wounds have been somewhat altered by exposure to air, especially during summer times.
There is no effective meaning in applying this powder, so treat it as we have mentioned by applying some prepared ointments or use a paste made of barley flour mixed with water and honey to promote pus formation, then continue treating it until it heals.
As for if a fracture occurs in the bone with the wound and it is minor, pull it with adhesive strips; I have mentioned the treatment of skull fractures in what will be discussed later, in neck surgery. If the wound happens in the neck, there is no difference in treatment from that of a head wound if it is simple.
And if a nerve from the neck or an artery is cut, then if it is a nerve there is no remedy other than to avoid treating it with tight bandaging such as with iron clamps and similar items because they harm the nerve and constrict it.
Do not apply anything cold to the wound at all because the nature of the nerve is cold and it is connected to the brain, which is the noblest organ. What you should treat it with are soft medicines like washed rose petals in fresh water several times moistened with oil or rose fat and pomegranate juice.
If you also wash it with fresh water and other metallic stones in this manner and moist salves. If the wound is large, use sutures or bandage its edges with dressings, then treat it until it heals.
If the wound has a depth and there is an enclosed pocket at its base where pus collects, treat it by opening the lowest part of this pocket. If an artery in the wound is severed and you see blood flowing, tie it off or cauterize it if necessary.
If the wound has cut some of the cartilages of the trachea or all of them but the veins are intact, gather the edges of the skin with stitches around the tracheal rings without touching the trachea itself. Smooth it and return it to its natural shape, then secure it tightly and leave it for several days.
Then treat it with ointments that have astringent and drying properties until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, then probe it; if there are bone fragments in it, remove them all or as much as possible, and leave what you cannot reach until the wound heals, becomes well, and their removal becomes easier.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle on it the powder we have described. If a bone becomes septic in the wound and becomes an abscess, treat it as will be mentioned in its section, in the chapter of chest surgery and between the shoulders.
If the wound is from a spear or knife and you see that it goes deep, observe if air comes out when the patient breathes; then know that it is a fatal wound. If it does not go deep and is fresh with blood, do not apply powder immediately nor bind it tightly so that the blood does not become trapped in its depths.
It may lead to the heart and kill the patient, but apply an astringent ointment and change it twice a day. If you do not have ointment, place a worn cotton plug in the mouth of the wound to absorb any moisture that comes out, and let the patient sleep with the wound elevated.
Let it drain what accumulates there. If three days or more have passed since the wound occurred, and the patient does not experience cramps, poor heartbeat, shortness of breath, and you see that their overall condition is good, then know that the wound is safe. Treat it with bandaging and other treatments until it heals.
If it is not possible to see inside and the pus flows constantly, then know that it has become an abscess. Treat it from its opening so that the patient can drain what accumulates within. If the wound has affected the chest or back, treat it with suturing as previously described.
If it is large, treat it accordingly; if small, do the same. If it has affected the bone and broken off fragments, examine the wound and promptly remove those fragments if they are healthy; if not, leave them be for the time being.
If the wound becomes infected, it will be easier to remove them. As for other wounds occurring in various organs, their treatment follows the principles we have mentioned.
Chapter Eighty-Five.
On wounds of the abdomen and the protrusion of the intestines and their suturing.
A wound to the abdomen may be large, small, or medium. A large wound may protrude an intestine or several intestines, making it more difficult and challenging to replace them and suture them.
A small wound may also be difficult from another perspective, requiring that its replacement be more challenging. The intestine, if not immediately returned to its place, will swell and thicken, making it harder to insert. Therefore, the medium-sized wound is considered the best type of wound because it does not make it difficult to replace the intestines as it does with these two types.
Know that suturing the abdomen is done in four ways. The two general methods are suitable for suturing the abdomen and other body wounds. The other two methods are more specific but primarily apply to suturing the abdomen.
I describe it to you one by one with explanation. I say that if the wound is small and something from the intestines comes out of it and it is difficult to return it, then this is for one of two reasons: either because the wound is small as we said, or because the intestines are inflated due to cold air.
If this is the case, it is necessary to warm it by dipping a sponge or wet cloth in lukewarm water, which may have been boiled with oxymel, saffron, barley, and other ingredients, and apply it to the intestines until the inflation subsides.
It can also be done with a drink that has a contracting effect, which is better than water alone for relieving the inflation. When you see that the inflation has subsided, moisten the intestines with water in which leek or baker's yeast has been boiled, as this will facilitate their re-entry with less effort.
If it is difficult to return it after this treatment, make a small incision in the wound with the instrument used for fistulas. The initial treatment, if successful, is better than making an incision. One resorts to an incision only as a last resort when other methods have failed. This is the image of the instrument:.
One side of it is curved and limited, while the other side is unlimited. The thin end is not as fine as a scalpel but slightly thicker. It resembles a small scalpel as you can see. When the wound has expanded and the intestines have entered, it should be returned to its natural shape.
If you can do so, it is best to place it in its specific position. If the wound is wide and located at the bottom of the abdomen, the patient should lie on his back with his legs raised higher than his head. If the wound is at the top of the abdomen, raise his head and chest higher than his lower body.
And if the wound is in one of the sides of the abdomen, always make the side with the surgery higher than the other side. This should be done for major surgeries and medium-sized surgeries as well.
As for small surgeries, place the patient as you see fit, then have a helper hold the wound with both hands and gather its edges, then reveal it to the surgeon bit by bit.
This is the description of a single basic stitch, which involves taking one or several needles according to the width of the wound, then leaving a length of thread equal to the thickness of the thumb and inserting one needle without threading it into the edges of the skin. With the edges of the subcutaneous tissue inside, until you pass through from that side, having gathered the edges of the skin and the edges of the subcutaneous tissue, making four layers in total. Then tighten with a folded thread around the needle on both sides repeatedly until the edges of the wound come together tightly.
Then leave a length equal to the thickness of a finger and insert another needle, then tie it with the thread as you did with the first needle. Continue doing this with the needles as needed until the entire wound is stitched up. The needles should be of medium thickness between thick and thin.
Because very thin needles easily cut the flesh, and thick needles are difficult to insert into the skin. Therefore, it is necessary for them to be of medium thickness between thin and thick. Also, the needle should not be inserted near the edge of the skin too closely.
To prevent the flesh from separating quickly and the wound from opening before healing, do not also stretch it too much with sutures lest the wound refuse to heal. Then cut off the ends of the needles so that they may not injure the patient when he sleeps, and make little pads of linen cloth on all sides to hold down the ends of the needles.
Leave them until you know that the wound has healed. This type of suturing with needles is most appropriate for small wounds, as it may suffice to use one or two needles or something similar.
As for the method of suturing for a general wound, it is to gather together with one stitch the four edges, namely the edges of the skin and the edges of the subcutaneous tissue, using a needle with moderately twisted thread. Then when you have passed through these four edges with the needle, return the needle from the side where you began.
To place the thread interlocking from the top of the wound so that the suturing is like the stitching used to bind luggage, and leave a space between each stitch as thick as the smallest finger. This type of suturing is known by all people.
With this type of suturing, a surgical wound was treated for a man who had been stabbed in his abdomen with a knife. The incision was more than an inch wide, and about two inches of the middle intestine had protruded from it. The wound was located in the center of the abdomen.
He returned the intestines to their place after keeping them outside the incision for twenty-four hours, and the wound healed in about fifteen days. I treated him until he recovered, and he lived many years afterward, managing all aspects of his life. The physicians had concluded that he would never recover at all.
It is amazing that I did not treat him with an ointment because I was in a place where there was nothing to be found of medicines, so I used old cotton twice a day on the wound and washed it with honey water until it healed.
As for one of the two types of special suturing, I will mention it as Galen himself described: Begin the stitching from the skin and insert the needle from outside to inside. When you pierce the needle through the skin and into the muscle running straight along the entire length of the abdomen.
I leave the edge of the mesentery on the side where I inserted the needle and pierce it again from inside to outside on the other edge, at the other end of the incision. When you have done this, insert the needle once more in this same edge, from outside to inside.
Leave the edge of the mesentery on this side and insert the needle in its other edge from inside to outside, and as you do so, extend it through the mesentery to the edge of the incision on its side until it passes through completely.
Then begin again from this same side and trace it along with the edge of the mesentery on the other side, and pull the needle out of the skin nearby, then insert it back into that skin and trace the edge of the mesentery on the opposite side along with this edge of the incision.
Then pull it out from the skin on its side, and do this again and again repeatedly until you suture the entire surgery in this manner. The overall characteristic of this suturing is to sew like the seams for animal hides.
That you sew the peritoneum once from one side with the edge of the skin and leave it once from the other side until it is emptied, and as for the second type of special suture which Galen also mentioned, this is his exact wording.
It is that you sew it as some practitioners do, gathering each part to its corresponding counterpart by nature, joining the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge and the edge of the mesentery to its other edge, and this is how I describe it to you.
You should pierce the edge of the mesentery nearest to you from outside and pass it inside by itself, then leave the edges of the peritoneum. Then withdraw the needle and pass it from outside to inside through both edges of the peritoneum, then also withdraw it again.
Pass it from inside to outside in the edge of the mesentery on the opposite side. This type of suture is better than the simple, easy common suture that was mentioned earlier. This is Galen's exact wording. And some have said that a stomach suture can be done with two needles. This is done by threading a single piece of suture through both needles, starting by passing one needle from your side to the opposite side and then passing the other needle from the opposite side back to you, similar to the suture technique used for hernias.
Know that if the wound is in the center of the abdomen, its suturing is more difficult than other parts of the abdomen. As for treating the wound, its treatment is like the treatment of other wounds. When you encounter a fresh wound filled with blood before it has been altered by air, return the intestines, suture it, and secure it tightly; then apply plasters made of pounded substances.
If the wound has been changed by exposure to air, then apply some of the ointments that are available until it suppurates and the sutures fall out and the peritoneum and serous membranes adhere, then treat it as you would other wounds until it heals.
If you do not have medications available, then use old cotton as soon as it begins to suppurate and change it twice a day as I taught you until it heals. In most cases, you will not need further treatment if the wounds are minor. If you fear that the wound may involve the major organs in pain,
You should soak soft wool in moderately heated oil or rose fat and place it around the areas between the armpits. If he feels pain or rot in his intestines, this often occurs; therefore, administer a cold decoction of black hemlock to him.
Especially if the rot has reached the intestines and become a perforating wound leading to the abdominal cavity. Know that what is thick in the intestines heals more easily, while what is thin within them heals with greater difficulty.
As for the intestine known as the jejunum, it does not accept healing from a wound that occurs in it at all. This is due to the abundance and thickness of the vessels within it, its thinness, and its proximity to the nature of the nerve. As for if what protrudes from the wound is fat and you catch it fresh, return it according to how you would return the intestine, similarly.
If time has passed and it has turned green or black, you should sew it with a thread above the place that has turned black to prevent bleeding, as there are vessels and arteries in the fat. Then cut what is below this ligature.
Make the ends of the thread attached below the surgery, outside it, to make it easy for you to pull out when the pus and ulceration occur. This refers to the wound that appears in the intestine, and as for if a hole appears in the intestine but is small.
It may be that it can be repaired in some people, for I saw a man who had been pierced in his abdomen with a spear, and the wound was to the right of the stomach. I treated the wound, but it became a fistula from which feces and gas were expelled. I continued to treat him although I did not hope for his recovery. I continued to care for him until he healed and the area knitted together. When I saw that the area had healed, I feared that something bad might happen inside his body, but nothing of the sort happened at all, and he remained in the best condition. He eats, drinks, has intercourse, and goes to the bathhouse, and he serves himself as needed. Some experienced people have mentioned that if a wound appears in the intestine and is small, it should be sutured in this manner.
This involves taking large-headed ants, then bringing the edges of the wound together, and placing an ant with its mouth open on the edges of the wound. When it grasps onto the edge, if you pinch its head off while it clings, it will adhere and not loosen. Then place another ant near the first one.
Continue doing this with several ants according to the size of the wound, then return it and suture the wound. Those heads will remain adhered in the intestine until it heals and recovers without causing any harm to the patient. It is also possible to suture the intestine with a thin thread.
Take a thread from the spool of the adherent animal after it has been inserted into a needle, and take one end of this thread from the spool and cut it neatly, then tie at its end a fine twisted linen thread, then insert that thread into the needle. And there is a suture thread with which the intestine is sewn up, then it is returned to the abdomen. This type of suturing with ants and spools is done out of hope and expectation.
As for if the wound is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the thin intestines, there is no remedy for it, nor will it heal at all.
Chapter Sixty-Eight.
On the Treatment of Cold and Fistula.
Know that every wound or swelling, if it becomes chronic and delayed, turns into an ulcer and does not heal, with pus continuously flowing without interruption; in general, regardless of the organ it is in, it is called a fistula. We call it a cold abscess, or simply a fistula.
In reality, it is a hardened, congested, white mass that does not cause pain and has a cavity like the hollow of a bird's feather.
Therefore, some have called it a feather. It can be moist at times, continuously draining pus, and the moisture may sometimes dry up. This moisture can vary in amount, being either abundant or scarce, thick or thin.
I have previously mentioned in the classification when discussing wounds that every ulcer that does not heal and in which no flesh grows back is due to one of nine reasons. The first reason may be a lack of blood in the body, or poor quality blood, or there may be hard flesh inside it and on its edges preventing the growth of good flesh. And either it is because there is much filth and dirt, or because the ulcer itself is putrid and the substance that nourishes it is of poor quality, or because the medicine is not suitable for treating it, or because there is some corruption in the town due to an epidemic, or because there is a particular characteristic of the town.
And either it is because there is much filth and dirt, or because the ulcer itself is putrid and the substance that nourishes it is of poor quality, or because the medicine is not suitable for treating it, or because there is some corruption in the town due to an epidemic, or because there is a particular characteristic of the town. As was shown in the city of Saragossa where it is difficult for diseases to mature and tumors heal slowly by nature, and either there is one bone or several bones involved. I have explained all these causes, their signs, and their treatments with medicines in the division.
Here I should inform you about the treatment of polyps and nasal congestion that occur through manual work. Know that polyps can occur in all parts of the body; some extend to major veins, arteries, or nerves.
or to the intestines, or to the bladder, or to a rib among the ribs, or to a vertebra among the vertebrae of the spine, or to a complex joint such as those in the hand and foot, and there are some that extend near a major organ and similar cases.
Those polyps that have the characteristics described are among the difficult-to-treat diseases that do not accept treatment, and dealing with them is burdensome and indicative of ignorance. However, there are some polyps that do not reach the places mentioned and for which one may hope and desire to treat them.
If you wish to determine this, take a probe made of copper or iron if the polyp runs in a straight line and examine it with it. If there is a deviation in the polyp, examine it with a thin lead probe because lead is soft and conforms to the shape of the body.
It becomes smooth upon entry and turns toward the curvature. If the fistula has many openings that you cannot identify with an probe, then inject one of its openings with moisture, for the moisture you inject will travel toward the other openings and flow through them.
Then thoroughly examine it in any way you can to determine if there is a bone or nerve, whether the fistula's depth is far or near, and whether it is a single fistula with many openings. Assess all of this to the best of your ability through your careful examination and the presence of pain when you touch the area.
And similarly, consider the signs and causes that lead to the tumor. Once you have a true understanding of all these factors, then proceed with confidence to treatment by examining whether it is.
If the fistula is visibly close or in a safe location far from a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or any of the places mentioned to you, then incise the fistula as described and remove what is within it: the pus, putrid flesh, and excess tissue.
And whatever grows in its mouth of flesh or polyp and the like, treat it until it heals. If the fistula is deep but straight, then incise it as deeply as you can and clean it of all putrid flesh.
Then use lint soaked in sharp medicines and insert it into the bottom of the fistula that you can reach with an instrument. Do this repeatedly until the sharp medicine consumes all remaining corruption at the bottom of the fistula.
Then apply ointments that heal the flesh properly until it is cured. If it does not heal with this method, then incise it as previously described. If you are convinced that the cause of the fistula is a bone, examine and cut it according to what has been informed to you unless something prevents you from doing so.
If it involves a nerve, artery, or principal organ as we have said, and the bone is exposed to you and there is some corruption or blackening in it, then remove the corrupted part until it is gone, then treat it with what will heal it until it is cured. If it does not heal and the pus persists as it used to do.
Know that you have not reached the complete removal of the corruption. Reveal it all again and thoroughly clean and purify it to the best of your ability, then treat it. If it heals, otherwise know that this corruption is in a deep recess. Reveal it all again.
Thoroughly clean and purify it to the best of your ability. If the corrupted bone is a small one and you can pull it out, then pull it out with fine forceps suitable for this purpose.
If there are many bones, then thoroughly pull out all of them without leaving any behind to the best of your ability. If ligaments or muscles impede you and cutting them poses no danger, then cut those ligaments and muscles if they are present and free the bones.
If it is not possible for you to extract the bones at that time, then apply something to the wound that will prevent it from healing. Leave it for several days until the flesh and ligaments around those bones decay. Protect the wound so that the incision you made does not heal during this process.
If you are afraid of that, place on it a cotton pad soaked in crushed sulfur with oil or a cotton pad soaked in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments; for when those bandages rot and the bones disintegrate, it will be easy to remove them and pull them out.
If it is a large bone like the bone of the leg or thigh, and only one side has decayed, then strip it thoroughly until the black decayed part is removed, then repair the wound. If a large portion has decayed and the decay has reached the bone marrow, then it is necessary to excise it.
Cut it all where the decay ends; then treat it until it heals. I will tell you about a condition that affected a young man in his leg to serve as an example and aid in your treatment. This young man was of tender age.
About thirty years old, he experienced pain in his leg due to an internal movement within the body, causing materials to connect to the leg and it swelled into a large tumor; there was no external cause. Time progressed with errors by the physicians until the tumor burst open.
Much material flowed from it, and he was treated poorly until the leg became ulcerated with many openings, all discharging pus and bodily fluids. A group of physicians treated him for about two years, but none of them were skilled in surgical practice.
Until he came to me, and I saw his leg with material flowing profusely from those openings. The man had become emaciated and his skin was yellow. I inserted a probe into one of the openings, and it reached the bone. Then I examined all the openings.
And I found them connecting to each other from all sides of the leg, so I began and cut into one of those openings until I exposed some bone, which I found to be rotten, blackened, decayed, perforated, until the hole reached the brain. I then removed what was revealed to me.
And I managed to remove the diseased bone, and I thought that there was no other corruption in the bone than what I had cut out and removed, and that I had completely excised it. Then I treated the wound with healing medicines for a longer period, but it did not heal. So I returned and exposed the bone again above the previous exposure.
And I found the corruption connected to the bone, so I removed what appeared to me of that corruption as well. Then I attempted to heal it, but it did not heal nor clot. So I uncovered it again and continued to cut the bone piece by piece, attempting to heal each section, but it would not heal until I had cut about an inch from the bone.
And I removed it along with part of the brain, then healed it with medicines, and it healed quickly and he recovered. However, this repetition in his treatment and incisions was necessary due to the patient's weakness, inability to tolerate further procedures, and my fear of him dying, as severe fainting spells would occur at any time from excessive purging. He eventually made a complete recovery.
And flesh grew in the place of the bone, it became solid, and his condition improved within his body; his strength returned, and he was able to manage his affairs. He did not encounter any harm at all when walking. If a protruding bone in some part of the body breaks,
It should be spread out in this manner: take a bandage and tie it to the end of the displaced bone, then have someone pull it from above, and use another thicker bandage of wool for the second one.
Then tie it to the flesh beneath the bone and pull its end so that the flesh is drawn downward. As you expose the area you wish to spread, ensure that the saw does not injure the flesh, and place a piece of wood or a board securely under the bone from below.
Because if you do this, the saw will not be prevented from cutting through the diseased bone. It is advisable to cut slightly above the site of the disease so that there is no corruption inside the bone cavity, which would remain unseen on the surface and necessitate another cutting later.
If the bone is diseased but not displaced, and it is connected to each other with the corruption in its middle or part of it, then expose the flesh from all sides completely. Then place a piece of wood underneath and spread it from the first side where the disease is located.
When you finish spreading from one side, spread it from the other side as well, and ensure that the cutting is slightly away from the diseased area as we said. If the disease is in a joint, cut out the diseased part of the joint itself and then spread the bone where it connects from the other side.
If the corruption is in the connection of two joints, there is no remedy other than excision. If the corruption is in the wrist or ankle, its treatment is very difficult; however, you should peel away the diseased part as it appears to you and excise and cleanse it as best you can.
By what means can you proceed when an artery or nerve is in the way? And know that there are many saws and cutting tools for breaking these bones, according to the position of the bones, their curvature, thickness, thinness, size, smallness, hardness, and looseness. Therefore, it is necessary to prepare a tool suitable for each type of work.
And know that the tasks themselves may guide you to the type of tool you need if you have long experience and knowledge of the arts of this craft, for skill in a trade and familiarity with various diseases are its signs. He may deduce for himself tools that suit each disease, and I will illustrate for you various tools at the end of this section.
Make them examples to follow and standards to measure others by, if God wills. Image of a saw:.

Chapter Eighty-Seven:
On Severing Limbs and Spreading Bones.
The limbs may become gangrenous either due to an external cause or an internal one. If you treat this corruption with medication and the treatment is ineffective, and you see that the corruption spreads throughout the limb without being stopped by anything.
Then you should amputate that limb up to where the corruption has spread, so that the patient may be saved from death or from a greater affliction than losing the limb. A sign that this condition has occurred is when the limb turns black as if it were burned by fire.
Or it may rot after turning black, until that decay spreads to the adjacent parts and then throughout the body; therefore, cut it off immediately. Similarly, if the cause of corruption is due to the bite of some creatures such as a sea scorpion or snake or centipede or similar.
If the corruption or bite is at the tip of a finger, cut off the finger and do not wait for the corruption to spread; similarly, if it begins in the hand, cut it off from the joint at the base of the wrist and do not wait for the corruption to spread until it reaches the forearm.
Similarly, if the corruption spreads to the arm, cut it off at the elbow joint. If the corruption is severe and you see it reaching toward the shoulder, do not amputate the shoulder by any means, as this could lead to the patient's death; instead, use other treatments to the best of your ability.
Similarly, do the same for the leg if the corruption spreads to a toe, cut off the toe at one of the joints. If it reaches the foot, amputate the entire foot. If it advances to the knee, then cut off the leg at the knee joint.
If the corruption has reached above the knee, there is no recourse but to leave it as it is and resign the patient to their fate. The method of amputating a limb or cutting it open involves tying a ligature below the place where you intend to cut and another ligature above that place.
Tie one attendant to the lower ligature and another to the upper ligature. With a wide scalpel, remove all the flesh between the two ligatures until it is completely exposed, then cut or open as needed. It is advisable to place pieces of linen gauze around all sides.
To avoid the saw from hitting the correct place and causing excessive pain and hot swelling, if bleeding occurs during your work, quickly apply pressure to the area or use some astringent substances then return to your treatment until you finish.
Then bind the injured limb with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals. I will tell you about an example of a man who had this exact condition in his leg, characterized by blackening and burning pain similar to fire.
This corruption first began in his toe and then spread to the entire limb. When the man saw the corruption spreading and felt the severe pain and burning, he decided to amputate it at the joint, and as a result, he healed.
After a long time, the same corruption appeared in his thumb. He came to me for help. I treated it with medicines applied to the hand after cleaning the affected area, but the corruption did not subside and began to spread to the other finger.
The corruption then spread to his entire hand. He asked me to amputate it, but I refused, hoping to treat the corruption without resorting to amputation and also because I feared for his life if we proceeded with the amputation due to his frail condition. When he lost hope in me, he returned to his home.
I heard that he decided to amputate his entire hand, and as a result, he healed. I have recounted this story to be of assistance in dealing with similar cases of this disease, to serve as evidence for reference and action.
Chapter Eighty-eight.
Regarding the treatment of abscesses and how to inject them with medications.
If a swelling occurs in some fleshy organs and the duration of the swelling is such that it accumulates fluid, until eventually it bursts or subsides and all that was inside exits, leaving the area empty as if it were a container.
And the skin on which it occurs is like a patch that has thinned, but it has not reached a state of decay that affects the bone, nerve, or ligament; hence, it deserves to be called an abscess rather than a fistula unless its duration leads the decay to affect any of these parts.
Then it is called a fistula or a nasal polyp, and its treatment involves cutting away all that skin as it surrounds the area, especially if it has thinned and become like a patch, and you are certain that it will not adhere to the site due to its decay.
If you expect the skin to adhere and it has not yet reached that degree of decay, and there is a thickness of flesh in the skin, treat it with an injection. This involves examining the abscess; if it is large and the pus emanating from it smells foul, inject it with Egyptian medicine.
This medicine consists of vinegar, oil, honey, and resin in equal parts, mixed in a container and cooked over fire until the medicine thickens to the consistency of honey. Then take what you need and dilute it with water and honey. Inject this into the abscess and secure its opening, leaving the medicine inside for about two hours.
Then remove it by squeezing, do this for days until the contents are clean and the foulness is gone; and if this medicine is not available, take the ashes of vine wood or oak wood and pour water over them.
Then arrange it and inject with it until you are certain that the contents have been washed out; if there is no tolerance for Egyptian medicine or wood ash in the patient, then inject him with water mixed with honey and a little of powdered cubebs.
Or inject him with honey and syrup mixed together, for honey cleanses and washes, and syrup adheres to the cavity, especially if the syrup is viscous and dry. When you have done this repeatedly and are certain that the corruption has gone, then inject him with something that promotes flesh growth.
For example, take some of the cream made from palm and dissolve it in rose oil and astringent syrup, or inject with some other medicines and ointments whose properties we have described in the article on ointments. If the mouth of the cavity is narrow and does not admit an injector tube,
then widen it with iron slightly, or place a wick soaked in Egyptian cream or millefleurs ointment inside until it expands. Similarly, if the mouth is also wide, sew together its edges to the extent that an injector tube can be inserted without difficulty.
And if the opening from which pus flows is high, cut it at its lowest point so that the pus can flow downward. For if pus accumulates in a deep part of the cavity, it prevents flesh from growing there.
If you are unable to incise the abscess downward as desired, then elevate the part so that the pus flows out easily as seems appropriate to you, or ensure that nothing at all is retained within it.
As for the cohesive plasters that are applied to the abscess from the outside and how they are applied, take one of the cohesive ointments suitable for what is needed and apply it onto a piece of linen. Let the piece of linen be large enough to cover the entire abscess. Then cut with scissors a hole over the mouth of the abscess, slightly wider than the mouth of the abscess. Then apply some of the soft ointment onto another piece of linen in the size of the hole and place it on top, keeping it somewhat distant from the mouth of the abscess to facilitate the easy discharge of pus. Do not remove the larger piece of linen until further instructed.
For several days, and as for the one placed over the mouth of the abscess, remove it at regular intervals to allow the accumulated pus to discharge and to determine whether what flows out is abundant or scarce, mature or immature.
Also, check the area of the abscess itself to see if the patient feels pain there or not, and whether there is swelling or not. If you continue treating it this way and notice that the substance coming out from the mouth of the abscess is minimal.
Know that the abscess has adhered or is close to adhering. Therefore, remove it tomorrow or on the third day. When you remove the dressing, change it and renew the ointment. If thin pus comes out from the abscess after a long period of time, do not despair of its adhesion.
For healing is quick because some people’s flesh grows slowly in their wounds, while others are the opposite; if much pus comes out after many days and it is not ripe, know that the wound has not closed properly, for if the wound does not close, this will be delayed.
And if it takes a long time, increase the drying of medicines; let them be moist in consistency but dry in strength, like the palm plaster if more of siliceous tallow has been added to it. Among the most effective treatments is to use the palm plaster.
It is treated with rose oil, and it is sprinkled with old, well-balanced syrup, then mix them together and use it. If the palm plaster is not available to you, you can do the same with honey that has been cooked until thickened and used, or take myrrh, fenugreek, and spikenard, crush all of them together and sprinkle it on the honey while it is heating on the fire. Then apply it to a piece of cloth and bind it over the wound, or apply it with the honey you cooked until thickened, sprinkle the medications on it, place the cloth on it, and secure it.
And for something like this, long turpentine pitch, the root of asphodel, flour of millet, and solitary or combined colocynth can be used; if combined, crush them together and sieve them before sprinkling on the honey that was described and use it. If the wound takes a long time to heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption deep within it.
And if it has affected the bones or any other solid nerves or ligaments, treat it as you would a nasal polyp, as previously described.
Chapter Eighty-Nine.
Regarding the treatment of fractures, contusions of the fingernail, amputation of the supernumerary finger, and division of webbed fingers.
A dāḥis is an excessive growth of flesh that develops under the thumbnail of the thumb or big toe, and it may also occur in other fingers. If left untreated for a long time, it becomes inflamed and forms a hot abscess.

Then you should cut off all the excess growth around the nail with an iron instrument, and afterwards cauterize the wound, as this is very beneficial. If, however, the bone is sound and the nail is also healthy, …
And if the nail's angle has caused the flesh to retract inward, causing irritation and discomfort, you should place a thin wedge under the part of the nail that is irritating the flesh, lift it up, and carefully cut away the affected flesh.
Apply cauterizing and corrosive medicines to the remaining flesh until it is completely removed, then treat the area with ointments until it heals. If the corruption has affected the bone, you should cut out and remove that bone, as healing will not occur while there is diseased bone present.
If you observe that the decay and corruption are spreading in the finger, then amputate it at one of the joints as previously described, and treat the area until it heals. If the nail is struck or bruised and severe pain occurs, you should first perform phlebotomy on the patient.
Then cut the nail with a sharp knife in a slanted incision from top to bottom, and be careful not to reach the flesh under the nail, for this will cause severe pain to the patient and may lead to excessive tissue growth at the site. Then treat the area with something that alleviates pain.
As for the extra finger that sometimes grows on some people's hands, it may be entirely fleshy, or it may have bones in part of it, and there may even be a nail. Part of its growth may occur at the base of one of the hand joints.
Or its growth may occur in some parts of the finger joints; those that grow at the joints do not move, while those that grow near the finger joint may move. The fleshy part is easier to remove and should be cut off at its base with a broad knife.
As for those that grow at the base of the joint, their treatment is difficult, so one should avoid cutting them. As for those that grow on a finger near one of the joints, it is necessary to first cut away the flesh in a circular manner to reach the bone, then use one of the appropriate saws to remove the bone.
Then treat it until it heals. As for the fusion that sometimes occurs between fingers, this often happens and can be something with which a person is born or may occur after a wound has healed or from burns and similar injuries. It is necessary to cut through such adhesions.
Until the fingers return to their natural shape, then place a piece of felt or a moistened patch soaked in rose oil between them so that they do not reattach too quickly. Alternatively, insert a thin sheet of lead between them until the area properly heals.
And if the adhesion affects some fingers of the hand, then cut that adhesion as appropriate to improve and correct the appearance of the limb.
Chapter Ninety.
On Cutting Syndactylism.
Syndactylism are tortuous, thick veins filled with excess dark blood waste that occur in most parts of the body, but most commonly in the legs, especially in the shins, calves, and thighs. First, it is necessary to apply strong massages to remove the dark bile from the body repeatedly.
Then let blood from the patient using a basilic vein. As for treating it with iron, there are two methods: one is to incise and remove the black blood, and the other is to lacerate the vein and extract it entirely. For the incision, first warm the leg with hot water until it turns red.
Until the thick, murky blood becomes loose. Then bind the patient's leg tightly from above the thigh to just below the knee with a cloth. Then incise the vein in one place or two places or three, making a wide incision.
Then extract the black blood with your hand from the bottom of the leg to the top and from the top to the bottom until you remove what you consider sufficient or what the patient's strength can tolerate. Then bind it, and instruct the patient to avoid foods that generate black bile. Repeat the bleeding as necessary.
When the veins become full and this harms the patient, here is how to treat it: If there is a lot of hair on the leg, shave it off. Then take the patient to the bathhouse and warm their leg with hot water until it turns red and starts sweating, or have them engage in vigorous exercise.
If a bath is not available to warm the member, then make an incision along the vein lengthwise, either at its end near the knee or at its base near the heel; then use scissors to cut open the skin and strip the vein from all sides until it can be felt.
When it appears, you will see it as a bright red vein; once it is freed from the skin, you will also see it resembling a tendon. Then insert your finger underneath it and lift it up until it rises above the skin, then suspend it with a smooth, blunt needle. Make another incision nearby, about three fingerbreadths away.
Then strip the skin away from the vein until it is visible, then lift it with your finger as you did before and suspend it with another smooth needle as you did initially. Then make another incision or several more if necessary.
Then pull it out and cut it at the end of the incision near the heel, then pull it through the second incision until it emerges from there. Then pull it up to the previous incision and do this until you pull it completely out from the highest incision among all. Once it is fully out, cut it off.
If it does not respond to pulling, insert a strong thread through a needle and tie it securely. Then pull it and insert your finger under the vein and twist your hand in all directions until it emerges, being careful that it does not break; if it breaks, it will be very difficult for you to extract it.
It is detrimental to the patient to insert anything into the wound. Once you have completely extracted it, place wool soaked in syrup, rose oil, or olive oil on the site of the incisions and treat it until it heals. If the vein is spasmodic with wrinkles and twisted in various directions,
And if it is not straight as we said, you should cut along each twist and bend of its coils and then attach it to the hooks until you have straightened it all out; be careful when cutting that you do not sever or injure the vein, for otherwise it will be difficult for you to straighten it, so take care with your effort.

Chapter Ninety-One.
On Straightening the Tortuous Vein.
This vein develops in the legs in hot countries like Hijaz and Arab lands, and in dry, less fertile hot regions. It may also develop in other parts of the body besides the legs, and its development is due to a corruption that occurs under the skin.
It also occurs inside living creatures as snakes, worms, and the swelling of a cucumber-like growth, as well as worms that develop between the skin and flesh. The sign of the beginning of this vein's occurrence is a severe inflammation in the leg, then the area becomes swollen, and then the vein begins to emerge from that swollen spot.
It appears like the root of a plant or animal. When its tip becomes visible, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead weighing from one dirham to two dirhams and secured, leaving the lead hanging from the leg.
Whenever something comes out to the outside, wrap it in lead and tie it. If it is very long, cut some of it and wrap the rest; do not cut it from its root before it has all come out, for if you cut it, it will contract and go back into the flesh, causing a tumor, corruption at the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore, it should be managed and pulled out little by little until it is completely removed and nothing remains in the body. In some people, what comes out from this vein may be as long as five fingers and ten.
I have heard that it once reached twenty fingers in length for a man. If it breaks during your treatment, insert a probe into its opening and fill it with a long plug along with the body until everything inside is emptied of its contents, and try to clean the area with medicines for several days.
Then treat it as you would treat tumors. This vein may have many branches, especially if it appears in a joint of the leg or in the leg itself, causing many openings from which branches emerge. Treat them as previously described in the division and treatment methods.
Chapter Ninety-Two.
On Incising for the Worms Generated Under the Skin and Called Cattle Disease.
This disease is called in some of our countries "Cattle Disease" because it often affects cattle. It is a small worm that develops between the skin and flesh and spreads throughout the body, moving up and down.
Its presence is detected by the sensation of its movement from one part to another until it breaches and emerges at a place in the skin where it has penetrated. It forms as a result of corruption in some mixtures, just as worms, snakes, and pumpkin seeds may form in the belly due to such conditions.
And it is expected that her harm will be that if she enters the body and rises to the head and reaches the eye, then perhaps she will open it and come out, thus ruining the eye, and this often happens. If you wish to treat her and remove her, then that should be done when she moves within the body and becomes apparent to the senses.
It is necessary to tightly bind what is above and below it with a ligature, then make an incision on top of it and remove it. If it sinks into the flesh and you cannot find it, apply cautery with fire to the area until it burns her away. The greatest danger from her is destroying the eye, as we have said.
If you see that it has become in the head near the eye, then tightly bind below it on the forehead with a ligature, then make an incision and remove it. It is necessary for the patient to cleanse his body with medicines that ease the putrid and bad humors and to be cautious about foods that produce putrefaction.
Chapter Thirty-Nine
On Incising the Disease Known as Nafir.
This disease, which is called nafir in our country, is a pain that appears in one of the organs and then moves from one organ to another. I have seen it as I will describe to you.
I was called to see a sick woman in one of the villages. When I examined her arm, I noticed a slight swelling in one of the veins. After waiting for a short while, I saw this swelling move along with the tendon, like a worm moving upwards towards her shoulder as quickly as possible, like mercury.
If it flows from one place to another, the pain subsides in that place and remains in the shoulder. Then after sitting for a while, it runs through the entire body until it reaches the other arm, then she told me that her whole body is moving as she observed.
I was amazed at the speed with which it moved from one member to another. I had not seen this disease in such a way before, although I had heard of cases where the pain moves from one member to another, but I had never witnessed it as I did in this woman.
I did not expect this except that the woman was from the countryside, with a dry body and visible veins, hence this moving air appeared to the senses in such a way. It should not appear in this manner on those who live in luxury, with moist bodies and hidden veins.
If you wish to treat it and the patient feels the pain, then if it appears as mentioned above, bind it tightly from above and below with haste and make an incision so that this congested air can escape, and dress the area.
If you do not see it with your own eyes, then treat it by fumigation of the body and with remedies that expel air and disperse it, such as rotten seeds, scammony, and similar medicines.
Chapter Forty-Nine.
On the Removal of Arrows.
Arrows may differ according to their types and according to the places where they lodge in the body. As for their difference according to types, there are large and small ones; some are hollow glass arrows and others are solid glass arrows; and there are those with three corners and four corners.
And some of them have tongues, and some have fragments. As for those that occur according to the organs in which they are located, they are of two kinds: either the organs are principal hollow organs such as the brain, heart, liver, lung, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and the like. When an arrow strikes one of these organs and the signs of death that I will describe to you become apparent, it is advisable to avoid removing that arrow, for in most cases death will be the consequence. And when these bad signs do not appear and the arrow has not penetrated deeply into the organ, remove it and treat the wound. Among the signs of brain injury when an arrow penetrates and pierces the bone, causing a tear in the membrane covering the brain, is severe headache, vomiting, and dizziness.
And when these bad signs do not appear and the arrow has not penetrated deeply into the organ, remove it and treat the wound. Among the signs of brain injury when an arrow penetrates and pierces the bone, causing a tear in the membrane covering the brain, is severe headache, vomiting, and dizziness. Redness in the eyes, redness of the tongue, convulsions, confusion, vomiting, and it may happen that blood comes out of the nostrils or ears. Speech may be interrupted, the voice may disappear, and a white moist substance like paste may come out from the wound, with a liquid similar to meat broth flowing from it.
If these signs appear, refrain from treating the patient and removing the arrow if it has not already been removed. As for the signs of an arrow when it strikes the heart and is close to the left breast and feels as though it has embedded in a solid object rather than an empty space.
It may also be that the arrow has a movement similar to the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, followed by coldness in the limbs, cold sweat, and fainting. Know for certain that death is inevitable. The sign of an arrow when it wounds the lung is the discharge of frothy blood from the wound.
The vessels near the neck become swollen, the patient's color changes, and he breathes heavily; he seeks to inhale cold air. If the arrow strikes the membrane in the chest, it is close to the small ribs, and breathing becomes very difficult with severe pain.
He sighs and moves all the muscles of his shoulders. If the arrow strikes the liver, it is followed by severe pain, and blood similar in redness to that from the liver exits the wound. If the arrow hits the stomach, something undigested may come out of the wound.
The situation is clear. If the arrow penetrates the abdomen and something comes out of the wound, whether it be feces or if an intestine has been pierced, there is no hope for treatment or removing the arrow. If the arrow strikes the bladder and urine exits.
And if something protrudes from the wound and the pain becomes severe for the patient, know that he is doomed. As for other parts such as the face, eyes, throat, shoulder, upper arm, spine, elbow, thigh, leg, and similar limbs, they generally fare better in most situations.
If the arrow does not hit an artery or nerve and is not poisoned, I will inform you about what I have witnessed concerning such arrows so that you may use it to guide your treatment. An arrow once struck a man in the bridge of his nose at its base.
I removed it for him from the other side, underneath the cartilage of his ear, and he recovered without any harm to his eye. I also removed an arrow for a Jew who had been struck in the iris of his eye below the lower eyelid; the arrowhead was concealed, and all that could be seen was its small tip attached to the shaft.
There was a large arrow from the composite quiver, with a square iron tip that was smooth and had no ears. The Jew recovered and his eye suffered no ill effects. Another arrow was removed from the throat of a Christian, and this arrow was Arabian.
It was an arrow with two ears. I split it for him between the throat muscles, as it had lodged in his throat. I handled it carefully until I removed it, and the Christian recovered and healed. I also removed another arrow from a man who had been struck by it in his abdomen; we estimated that he would die from it.
After thirty days or so had passed and there was no change in his condition, I split the arrow and devised a way to remove it; he recovered without any adverse effects. I also saw a man who was struck by an arrow in his back, and the wound adhered to the arrow.
After seven years, the arrow emerged at the root of his thigh. I also saw a woman who was struck by an arrow in her abdomen; the wound adhered to the arrow and it remained inside without any change in her condition. She did not feel any harm from it in terms of her natural functions.
I also saw another man who was struck by an arrow in his face; the wound adhered to the arrow and he did not feel much pain from it. There are many similar cases. I removed an arrow from a commander of the Sultan's forces who had been struck by it near the center of his nose, which had slightly shifted to the right side.
The arrow had completely penetrated. I was called to treat him three days after the incident. I found the wound from the arrow very narrow. I probed it with a thin probe but could not feel the arrow. He felt a pricking and pain under his right ear due to the wound.
I hoped that the wound was from the tip of the arrow, so I bandaged the area with a dressing that had pulling power and warmth, hoping that it would swell and reveal the mark of the arrow. When I cut it open, nothing happened to indicate that the arrow had reached the spot.
I continued to bandage it for many days, but nothing happened. So, I sealed the wound during that time, and the patient was despairing of its removal for a period until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and informed me of this. I placed a sharp corrosive medicine on the wound for many days.
Until it opened and I probed it; I felt the thin tip of the arrow that adheres to the wood. Then, I increased the opening of the wound with that sharp medicine until the tip of the arrow became visible to us. It took me about four months for this to happen.
After the wound widened and I was able to insert forceps into it, I pulled and moved it, but it did not respond to coming out. So, I kept trying gently and devising various tools until one day I grabbed it with tight forceps, the design of which will be shown at the end of this chapter.
The doctors had ruled that his nasal cartilage could not be repaired, but I repaired it anyway. The wound healed, and the patient recovered completely without any further issues at all. I am telling you about how to remove some arrows so that you can use this as a basis and guide for what has not been mentioned.
Because the parts of this craft and its details cannot be understood by description alone, nor can a book encompass them all. The skilled craftsman measures the little against the much, and what is present against what is absent, and derives new work and new machinery when faced with strange occurrences.
If you ask about this craft, I say that arrows come out of the organs in which they are embedded in two ways: either by being drawn out from the place where they entered, or from the opposite side, and those that come out from where they entered.
Either the arrow is protruding in a fleshy part, and it can be pulled out and removed. If it does not respond to being extracted at the time of its entry, you should leave it for several days until the flesh around it decomposes, making it easier to pull out and remove. The same applies if it lodges in bone.
If it does not respond to being extracted, leave it for several days and attempt to pull and move it every day, as it will eventually come out. If it still does not come out after several days, you should drill around the arrow in the same bone from all sides with a fine awl until there is enough space for the arrow, then pull it out and remove it.
If the embedded arrow is in the bone of the head and has penetrated one of the compartments of the brain, and some of those symptoms you have been informed about appear on the patient, then refrain from pulling out the arrow and leave it until his condition improves after several days.
Deal with drawing out the arrow and removing it. If it is deeply embedded and does not respond to being drawn, then use drills around the arrow as I have described for you, then treat the wound until it heals.
