As for if the arrow has concealed itself in a part of the body. And if it escapes the senses, probe for it with a探针。如果感觉到它，就用一些适合拔出它的工具将其拉出来。如果没有能力因为伤口狭窄和箭矢深入而无法触及，而且附近没有骨骼、神经或血管。
Make an incision on it until the wound widens and you can reach the arrow to remove it. If it has two barbs, free the flesh entangled in them from all sides by any means possible; if you cannot free the flesh, break off the barbs and untangle them until they are removed.
And when you attempt to remove the arrow from any part, use your fingers with forceps in all directions until it is free. Be as gentle as possible so that the arrow does not break, making it difficult for you to draw and extract it. If you cannot manage it within your available time.
Leave it for several days until the flesh around it begins to decay, then return to it, as it will be easier at that time. If bleeding occurs, use the treatments mentioned in its section. Be cautious not to cut a vein, nerve, or tendon, and employ ingenuity.
Use any means you can to free the arrow, doing so with gentleness, patience, and composure as I have described for you. It is best that when you are drawing out the arrow, you position the patient as they were when the arrow struck them, which is more appropriate.
If you cannot do that, use what is available to you in terms of shapes. As for the arrow that comes out from the opposite side, either some part of it protrudes outside, or you will feel its tip by touch near the top of the skin and see it protruding.
Make an incision for it, and let the incision be large enough to accommodate forceps. Then pull it out, as it will come easily. If it gets stuck in a bone, twist your hand around its curvature until the arrow affects the bone and widens its own path, then pull it out.
Otherwise, leave it for a few days and then try again until it comes out. If the arrow shaft is still in place, push it out with something appropriate. If the shaft has fallen off and you wish to push it, insert a hollow instrument into it so that its hollow part fits over the arrowhead, then push it out with it.
If the arrow is hollow, push it out with an instrument that fits into its hollow part; this will make it easier for the arrow to come out. If the arrow is poisoned, you should excise all the flesh that has been affected by the poison if possible, then treat it as needed.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, abdomen, bladder, or side and is close to an area that can be probed with a probe, and if you can make an incision, do so while being careful not to cut a vein or nerve, then remove it.
Then outline the wound if it needs stitching, and treat it until it heals. Image of the forceps used to extract arrows:
Its ends are like the beak of a bird, made so that when they grasp an arrow or something else, they do not let go. Various sizes are made from them: large, small, and medium-sized, all according to the size of the arrow, its thickness, and the width or narrowness of the wound.
Image of the hollow cannon:
Image of the solid musket:
This is solid at the end like a rod to make it easy for it to enter the hollow arrow and push it.
Chapter Fifty-Nine.
On Cutting Veins.
There are thirty veins in the body that are usually cut, sixteen of which are in the head: the two pulsating veins behind the ears known as the hesis, and the two veins visible in the temples, and the two veins at the corners of the eyes known as the nazir.
The upright vein in the middle of the forehead, the vein at the tip of the nose, and the two veins on the sides of the neck known as udajan, the two veins in the upper lip, and the two veins in the lower lip, which are known as jaharak, and the two veins under the tongue.
As for the veins that are cut in the arm and hand, there are five veins: one is called quifal, which is on the inner side, and it is known to the general public as the head vein. The other is al-akhal, which is the middle vein composed of a branch of basilic and a branch of quifal, and it is known to the general public as the body vein.
And Basilic, which is located on the inner side and is also called axillary, and it is commonly known as the abdominal vein or arm cord, and this is the one that adheres to it and is prominently visible above the thumb, and Assilm.
It is the vein between the little finger and the ring finger, which has two branches. In the leg and foot, there are three veins: one under the popliteal from the medial side, the second is the sesamoid on the inner side near the heel, and the sciatic vein on the medial side at the Achilles tendon.
In the other leg, there are also three veins like them. As for the two veins behind the ears, their benefit is in lancing them for chronic catarrhs, migraines, shingles, and chronic, poorly healing head sores. The method of lancing them will be described as follows.
It involves shaving the patient's head and vigorously rubbing the area behind where the veins are located with a coarse cloth. Then, the patient’s neck is constricted with a turban until the veins become visible behind the ears in the lower parts of the head. You should feel for their pulse under your finger; once you locate it, mark that spot with ink.
Then take a lancet-like scalpel, known as a nashl. Insert it under the vein through the skin until the scalpel reaches the bone. Then lift your hand with the vein and skin upwards and cut the vein along with the skin in one clean cut. The length of the incision should be about the width of two clenched fingers.
Or something similar, and let out as much blood as desired, then bandage them until they heal. It was mentioned earlier in the beginning of the book how to cut and cauterize them. As for the two arteries in the temples, their benefit is in lancing them for chronic migraines, severe headaches, persistent conjunctivitis, and acute purulent discharges directed towards the eyes.
How to dissect them as I describe to you: The patient should pull his neck with a turban until the veins become clearly visible, and you can feel their pulse under your fingers. At that moment, mark them with ink, then lift the skin above the vein from its base upward with your index finger.
Insert the scalpel from below and lift the vein upwards, then cut it as you did with the other two veins. Let out as much blood as needed, then loosen the patient's neck restraint and place your finger on the vein for a moment.
Then place a cotton pad and bandage on it, securing it tightly from above. Leave it until he recovers. The treatment of these veins has been mentioned earlier in the beginning of the book, along with their cutting and bleeding procedures. As for the venesection of the frontal vein, its benefit comes after that of the temporal vein for chronic ailments of the face such as psoriasis, ulcers, and persistent redness.
How to perform it as I describe to you: The patient should pull his neck with a turban until the vein becomes visible, then take the instrument called the faas, and this is its appearance:.
Place the prong at the head of the faas on the same vein and strike it from above with a comb or something similar. Let the blood flow to the desired amount, then loosen the patient's neck restraint and secure it until he recovers.
It may also be opened with a wide scalpel, except that the scalpel should not have a sharp tip like other scalpels; instead, it should have a slightly wider tip. Perform the venesection at an angle because the bone is close, and the scalpel might break if it were thin.
As for the two veins that are in the hollow of the eyes, their benefit is for eye ailments such as scabies, redness, ulcers, and facial diseases. As for how to puncture them, it is that the patient should tie his neck with a turban, then you puncture them while standing at his head, and let the puncturing be slightly angled towards the length with a small but somewhat wide lancet.
If the area has no flesh, then if the lancet is thin at the tip, it may break. Then let blood as needed and place a piece of cotton on them and wrap them for one night before removing it. As for puncturing the vein of the nose, it is beneficial for acute fever.
Severe headache and diseases of the face such as red spots that appear in the nose, especially if they are chronic. The method of puncturing is that the patient ties his neck and you hold his nose with your left hand, then take a thin long lancet.
And insert it in the middle of the nasal septum itself, between the partitions of the nose, straight ahead because the vein does not appear to the touch there; blood will immediately emerge. You should apply a little pressure with your hand on the lancet and let out as much blood as needed, then wrap it for one night because it quickly heals.
As for the jugular veins, their benefit from puncturing them is for shortness of breath, the onset of leprosy, and pigmented diseases that appear on the surface of the skin such as black vitiligo, carbuncles, bad ulcers, and other similar conditions. The method of puncturing them is to tie the patient under them at his neck with a strap, and the practitioner should stand at the patient's head.
The patient sits on a chair, then let the blood flow from the vein widely but slightly, then take an appropriate amount of blood or as needed, then do the same for the other vein, then loosen the tourniquet.
Tie the veins with moderate tightness so that the patient does not suffocate, and leave it until tomorrow as the wound will heal. As for the veins of the cheeks, their benefit is in letting them bleed after bleeding the temporal vein because it helps against ulcers in the mouth, decay of the gums, foul ulcers, splitting of the lips, and foul sores.
Those that are in the nose and around it, and how to bleed them: Have the patient sit before you and tie his neck with a turban, then turn his lips and look at the two veins which you see one on the right side of the lip and the other on the left, and identify them by their darkness.
This is also because around them are thin black veins, so cut them cleanly. If you are unsure and do not know which ones they are, aim to cut the larger and more prominent ones. Do the same for the two veins in the lower lip.
As for the two veins under the tongue, their benefit is in letting them bleed after bleeding the temporal vein for treating abscesses that occur in the throat due to hiccups and diseases of the mouth. To bleed them, have the patient sit before you facing the sun, raise his tongue, and look underneath it on one side for a vein and on the other side for another vein, both having a dark color.
Bleed them but be careful not to cut them deeply as there are arteries beneath them, and bleeding from those arteries might cause excessive blood loss. As for the three veins that are bled in the elbow, it is customary to bleed these veins in people generally, and their bleeding can be done in two ways.
Either sewing with a broad rose scissors or olive scissors to the thickness, or cutting with a knife-like scissors, which is called nashl. [And this is its image], the image of the broad rose scissors:.
It is broad as you see, suitable for opening hollow veins that are full, prominent, visible, tough, and contain thick, dark blood. This is the image of the olive scissors:.
This scissors is narrower and has a thinner tip, suitable for bleeding fine veins that contain thin, yellowish blood. This is the image of the nashl scissors:.
This nashl, which is suitable for cutting, comes in broad and fine types depending on the width and narrowness of the veins as well. It can also be used for other purposes, and it is well-known among practitioners.
As for the basiliq, which is one of these three veins, the benefit of bleeding it is that it draws blood from ailments located under the throat and neck, near the chest and abdomen. The person performing the bleed should be cautious and wary, as there is an artery below this vein; if they make a mistake and go too deep with the scissors, they may cut this artery.
This can cause excessive bleeding, so it should not be bled with a needle but rather by cutting with nashl. If the basiliq does not appear clearly, it is advisable to avoid it and instead go for another vein or seek one of its branches.
Or you can bleed the arm artery, which is clear, by cutting it with the nashl scissors as we have said. If you wish to bleed it specifically, before tightening the arm, you should palpate the area until you identify the pulse location and then mark it with ink.
Then tie the arm and cut the vein obliquely with the sharp knife as we said, and be careful that the incision is made away from the location of the artery. And when you see inflation in the place you marked with ink while tightening the ligature, then this inflation is the swelling of the artery, so avoid it.
If you see the blood during venesection jumping as urine does from a child, and the blood is thin and red, then know that it is arterial blood. At this moment, quickly place your finger on it for a long while, then remove your finger. If the bleeding stops, it often will stop; then tie the arm.
Leave it and warn the patient not to neglect it; let it be on his neck, and do not move it for days until he recovers. If the bleeding does not stop and overwhelms you, and no remedy is available at hand, then cut the artery if it appears to you, as its ends will contract and the bleeding will stop.
Or take the shell of a pistachio, split it, and take one half and tightly tie it with ligatures and bandages to the site of the vein for another day. If the bleeding stops, otherwise treat it as previously mentioned by applying pressure points to stop bleeding. Cutting the artery is not difficult in most cases.
Because of the small size of the incision and the effectiveness of the ligature on the arm, be aware of this. As for the black vein, the benefit of venesection is that it draws blood from the top of the head to the lower part of the body, as it is composed of a branch from the basilic vein and a branch from the cephalic vein, as we said.
It is advisable for the surgeon to operate on the neck of the person being treated, for below it lies a nerve. If he increases the number of stitches from his incision and injures the nerve, numbness will occur which is difficult to detect, and often does not heal at all. This nerve is often perceptible by touch.
If it is hidden in some people and thin, so that it cannot be seen, then you should make your incision with a scalpel and do your best to avoid the nerve. If the vein lies between two nerves, cut the vein longitudinally. As for the qifal vein, the benefit of ligating it is that it draws blood from the head.
It is beneficial for eye diseases, and in this particular vein, if you wish to ligate it; you should do so with stitches using the olive-shaped scalpel or the wide rose-shaped scalpel because it is safer than all other veins since there is no artery or nerve beneath it.
However, when ligating, you should avoid cutting only the muscle's head with the scalpel and seek a softer spot. It does no harm if it is not hit on the first attempt, but it may swell in some people if it is not ligated on the first attempt.
However, this swelling does him no harm. As for the method of venesection and its consequences, and what should be done in its correction: first, you should know that venesection is used solely to preserve health.
Or it is used for treating diseases. The venesection that is used to preserve and maintain health and to prevent the occurrence of diseases should be performed on one of the three veins in the elbow, namely the qifal, aqikhal, and basiliq. It should also be done at the beginning of spring when signs of fullness appear.
Phlebotomy should be performed on Sunday or Tuesday after three hours have passed since dawn, whereas phlebotomy used for treating diseases has no fixed time and can be done at any hour of the day or night as needed.
At every hour and at any time, it should not be done for boys until they reach fourteen years of age, nor for old men who have surpassed sixty years. If anyone decides to undergo phlebotomy for any reason.
It is necessary to cleanse his intestines with a mild enema before phlebotomy if there is much accumulated feces, so that during the procedure, putrefied waste does not draw into the veins and harm the principal organs. Phlebotomy should not be performed on someone who is nauseous, intoxicated, or drunk until these conditions subside.
He should also be cautious about phlebotomy immediately after vomiting, diarrhea, excessive intercourse, fatigue, exercise, staying up late, fasting, or any other matter that weakens the body physically or mentally. Then, before proceeding, check if the blood is thick by observing its consistency and take necessary measures to thin it if needed.
He should use food, drinks, and medicines if possible to achieve this. Then, he may enter the bathhouse if there is no prohibition or engage in some exercise to thin the blood, making his phlebotomy at the beginning of the day as we have said, and he should aim to keep his mind free from all negative mental distractions on that day.
Such as worries, anger, and fear, and from all physical burdens like excessive fatigue, overexertion, intercourse, and the like. Prepare for his presence items that people usually use by habit, such as various perfumes, flowers, amusements, and the like, each person according to what is possible for them.
Then the operator sits on a cushion that is higher than the one on which the patient sits, then he extends his arm and the operator strokes it with his hand two or three times, then he tightens the ligature with pincers and twists it two or three times, and let the tightening be moderate.
Because excessive tightening prevents the flow of blood, and if it is too loose it also prevents the flow of blood. After tightening, it is necessary for the operator to rub both hands of the patient together by pressing them against each other.
Until the veins become swollen and visible, then the operator wipes the scalpel with a little old oil, then he places his index finger of the left hand on the same vein slightly below the intended cutting point to avoid missing the vein and to guide the cut. Because there are veins that you find like a tendon, which retract when attempting to cut them, and there are others that are full of air; whenever the scalpel is placed on them, they collapse under the scalpel and deceive the operator, not allowing the scalpel to open the vein properly. Even if it does open the vein, it opens it narrowly.
Therefore, it is necessary for the operator to be cautious and deliberate in all these matters, then he should lower the scalpel; if the vein opens with that stroke, well and good, but if not, he should repeat it once again slightly below or above that spot without delay, provided the area does not become swollen.
If the area becomes swollen or if the patient is distressed, leave it for a day or two and do not tighten the ligature, as it may cause inflammation. Do not enter the bathhouse either. Then repeat the procedure if desired, especially if the scalpel penetrated but the opening was small and the blood flow was thin.
I fear that the amount of blood you want will not come out, so prepare the scalpel in the same hole and make it straight, and increase the opening a little. Do this quickly before the area becomes swollen, for in many people the area may swell with even a small incision.
If you see that it has swollen, do not return to it at all, for it will not help you. Place something of thick oil on it, for it facilitates the flow of blood and is better in this place than the oil itself or any other fats. And so.
Use thick oil in all your bloodletting from veins when the flow of blood is difficult. The elixir called Ferouk and Shakhzania can also be used if applied to the area, for they make thick blood thin and liquid.
If a large swelling occurs at the site of bleeding, it often happens, especially for those who have been bled only that once or whose vein incision was small. Act quickly and place on the swelling a sponge soaked in warm salt water, lightly applied for an hour; it will dissipate.
You should do this after the blood has completely come out of the vein itself or from another vein. If a few days later there is still some blackness or greenness in the area, it does no harm. If you wish, apply something of dissolved rue and honey.
Or something from the juice of fumitory or similar. Often a swelling and bad pimple occurs when bleeding basilic veins; place your hand on it, if you find that it oozes when you press it, then this is a bad pimple, so be careful not to put anything on it from what has been mentioned.
For it may well exude blood from an artery, but dress it with something that causes constriction to harden the area; then treat him with all other treatments until he recovers. And it is best to let out of each person's blood according to their strength and what appears of the prevailing color in the blood.
For if the blood is black, let it flow until it turns red; and if you see it thick, let it flow until it becomes thin; and if it is sharp, let it flow until its sharpness subsides. And for one who is full-bodied and strong and needs to have bloodletting done all at once.
Enlarge the sweat vein and let the lancet be wide; but for one who is weak, do the opposite. It should be drawn out in stages, and the puncture should be narrow. The best to use for venesection is a blade that is angled and curved, making a cut rather than stitches.
This type of venesection is free from bleeding and cutting a nerve, and it is better and safer than venesection by length or breadth. For one who often faints during venesection, it is advisable to feed him before the procedure with bread soaked in pomegranate water or scallion water.
If he is heated and you let out his blood in three or four sessions, if his temperament is cold, then he should take bread soaked in syrup of barberry or in sweetened honey scented with flowers or in fragrant herbal syrup before the venesection.
If fainting occurs during venesection and the cause is the excessive loss of blood, it is advisable to give him meat water and light fragrant syrup, use perfumed oils for anointing, massage his chest with it, and employ all that we have mentioned in the treatment of fainting.
Regarding vomiting, and as for one who wishes to let blood from his arm again, it is necessary for him whose aim is to vomit much but whose strength is weak that the blood be released little by little according to his strength over consecutive days.
And as for one who wishes to let blood from his arm again and whose body is strong, he should do so seven or nine hours after his first session. And as for one who wishes to draw blood from his body toward the opposite direction to which it has inclined, it is necessary to let blood for him on the second or third day.
And as for one who has much blood in his body that has become hot and intense, causing fever, it is necessary to remove the blood in one go and extract a large quantity, widening the incision until fainting occurs, after ensuring all conditions of venesection are properly attended to. And place your hand on his pulse as the blood flows to prevent death instead of fainting, for this often happens if the person performing venesection is ignorant and careless. It is not advisable to release the arm and let blood again if this occurs.
And if the sweat gland has closed and the blood is difficult to release, do not pinch it hard or twist it forcefully, as this is very bad. Instead, either leave it until you perform venesection again, or remove with a scalpel any coagulated blood in the sweat gland opening.
Or apply a bit of dissolved salt or a portion of the ferocious antidote or schyzania to it and pinch it gently until the blood comes out. If the vein has swollen, leave it alone.
Do not touch it until the swelling subsides. If necessity calls for bleeding again, either let blood above that site or let blood in the other arm or from another vein.
As for letting blood from the arm vein, it should be done instead of using achocha and parsley if they are not available or are scarce, since this vein is composed of them. The method of letting blood is that the patient should put his hand in hot water until the tendons become red and the vein becomes clearly visible, then tie a tourniquet above it with moderate tightness.
Then let the blood from the vein at a slight angle, neither too wide nor too long, and make the incision relatively wide. Let the bleeding be above the wrist joint slightly. If the blood does not flow easily, then put the hand back in the hot water container and allow the blood to run into the water.
Until your need is met. If you are in the summer days, you may not need to put the hand back in hot water. Most often, letting blood flow in hot water occurs during winter. Letting blood from this vein is safer than from all other veins. Because there is no harmful vein or nerve under it. As for letting blood from the asilim vein in the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments. The method of letting blood is to tie the wrist with a tourniquet or with your hand after putting it in hot water until the vein swells.
Because there is no harmful vein or nerve under it. As for letting blood from the asilim vein in the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments. The method of letting blood is to tie the wrist with a tourniquet or with your hand after putting it in hot water until the vein swells. It becomes very clear to the senses then let the blood from it at a slight angle, and even if you cut it completely, it will not harm it. Be cautious; do not press deeply with the lancet as there is a nerve under it, and the area is exposed without much flesh. Then put the hand back in hot water.
Let it flow in it, for if you do not refresh it with hot water, the blood will freeze in the mouth of the vein and stop flowing. When you have taken out as much blood as is needed, put oil and salt on the vein so that it does not heal too quickly.
And you should do the same with every narrow branch. As for the benefit of stopping it with the left hand, it is beneficial for diseases of the spleen, and you should do this as you did with the second one, equally. As for bleeding the safn, its benefit is for ailments in the lower body, such as diseases of the uterus.
And for the retention of menstruation, diseases of the kidneys, chronic sores on the thighs and legs, and similar ailments, the method of bleeding is as follows: He should put his foot in hot water and have it massaged until the veins swell. Then, above the knee joint, bind it tightly with a bandage, and the vein to be bled is located at the instep towards the thumb.
It branches out into many small branches on the leg. Bleed in the widest branch of it or at the instep where they come together, for this is better and safer. If you bleed on the surface of the leg, be careful not to injure the nerves beneath the skin.
Make your incision obliquely as if you were going to amputate it, and use a scalpel. If the blood does not come out, refresh his leg with hot water and let the blood flow freely until it stops. If the bleeder misses the vein on the first attempt.
Let him try bleeding a little above, for this place is safe and poses no danger if you are careful of the nerve as we said. Do the same with the safn on the other leg equally. As for the sciatic vein, its location is as we said at the heel from the inner side.
And the benefit of its removal for hip pain if it is due to hot blood and how to remove it: The patient should enter the bath and hurry, and bind his leg from the hip to four fingers above the heel with a thin long turban; otherwise, it will not appear.
When it appears, remove it according to your expertise, either by incision, which is better, or by amputation or by cutting it open if the place is safe. It is in most people extremely subtle, so if you do not find it.
And if it does not appear at all to the senses, then remove some of its branches, which are those that appear on the back of the foot towards the little toe and the ring finger, and be careful of the nerves, and let out as much blood as you wish.
Then release the binding and place a cotton pad on the site of the incision, and bind it; it will quickly heal.
Chapter Sixty-Six.
On Cupping and How to Use It.
Cupping instruments may be made of horns, wood, brass, or glass. Cupping is performed in two ways: one by making incisions and drawing blood, and the other without making incisions.
This cupping without making incisions is performed in two ways.
And the acupuncture points of the two temples, which are the two sides of the neck from both sides, and the point under the chin below the lower jaw from the mouth, and the acupuncture points of the shoulders, and the points at the coccyx on the buttocks, and the points in the middle of the thighs, and the points of the shins, and the points of the popliteal fossae.
Cupping therapy draws blood from the thin veins scattered in the flesh, and it does not weaken the body as much as bloodletting does. It should not be used, whether with heat or without, in any disease caused by excess until the entire body has been purged.
If there is a need for cupping due to a disease or as a preventive measure, it can be used at any time, whether at the beginning of the month, at its end, in the middle, or at any other time.
This is because there are people who, when their blood becomes excessive and they need to have it removed by cupping, feel heaviness and headaches in their head. Some of them experience fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck. Others may feel itching on their face and cheekbones, as well as dark spots and irritation in their eyes. There are also those who itch at the site of the acupuncture points.
Some people may become more talkative or laugh frequently. Others may taste blood in their mouth, have swollen gums, and spit blood. Some may sleep excessively, while others see visions of blood, redness, dead bodies, wounds, and similar images in their dreams. Whenever we observe any of these signs.
In particular, if there is something in the middle third of the month, we then order cupping after two or three hours have passed since daybreak. As for the benefit of spot cupping, it helps with heaviness in the head and fluid that drips into the eyes.
However, it should be done after purging the body. This cupping can serve as an alternative to bloodletting from the veins. It is important to avoid using it on those with cold brains or who have a cold, for it would greatly harm them.
Therefore, it should not be used by the elderly or those with cold diseases in their heads, and those who are addicted to it will develop forgetfulness.
Therefore, it is necessary to order the cupper to lower his hand slightly downward with the lancet, fearing the development of forgetfulness. As for cupping the shoulder, it serves as an alternative to bloodletting from the occipital vein and the basilic vein.
It is beneficial for asthma, shortness of breath, swelling of the respiratory organs, coughing, and fullness. It is necessary to raise cupping on the shoulder slightly because if it is directed downward, it would cause weakness in the heart and stomach.
As for cupping the temples, it is beneficial for sudden pains in the head, eye infections, migraines, choking, and pain at the roots of the teeth. It serves as an alternative to bloodletting from the basilic vein. You should instruct the cupper not to dig deep with the lancet so as not to cut an artery, which could cause excessive bleeding.
As for cupping under the chin, it is beneficial for abscesses in the mouth, gum disease, and other diseases of the mouth. It can serve as an alternative to bloodletting from the veins on the lips. As for cupping the shoulders, it is beneficial for palpitations caused by fullness and heat.
As for cupping the inner sides of the thighs, it is beneficial for what bloodletting from the three veins (basilic, occipital, and median cubital) would treat. This is because it draws blood from all those thin veins in the flesh and attracts these thin veins from thicker ones until the drawing reaches the three thick veins.
You should order the leechkeeper not to be excessive in applying the cupping glass, because the area is thin and has nerves and arteries beneath it. As for a single cupping session applied to the coccyx, it is beneficial for hemorrhoids of the rectum and ulcers of the lower region.
You should order the leechkeeper to use a large cupping glass, and it should be made of brass because the area requires strong suction, and a glass one might break due to excessive force. As for the cupping glasses used on the legs, they noticeably reduce swelling.
Because they draw blood from the entire body and are beneficial for chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder; they promote menstruation and are helpful for boils and varicose veins. They serve as an alternative to bleeding the ankles and knees, although they greatly weaken the body and cause fainting in most people. The benefit of cupping the popliteal areas is similar to that of cupping the legs.
The method of applying cupping glasses is as follows: place the cupping glass empty first and apply moderate suction. Do not keep the cups in one place for too long but place them quickly and remove them quickly so that the humors flow evenly to the area. Continue repeating this process until you see the area has turned red and opened up.
When the redness of the blood appears, then apply moderate suction gradually. Then observe the condition of the body; for those who have thin flesh with open pores, you should cup them only once to avoid ulceration at the site.
You should order the leechkeeper to widen the incision slightly and deepen it, adjusting the suction gently with careful movement. If the blood is thick, you should have them perform the procedure twice: in the first time, open a path for the thin and watery part of the blood, and in the second time, to thoroughly extract the thicker blood.
If the blood is very thick, the procedure should be repeated a third time to reach the goal. In general, if we want to extract a small amount of blood, one pass will suffice; if we wish to extract more blood, we repeat the process more times. If the blood is thick, we assume it will require more effort.
We should perform a deep bloodletting, and the moderate depth of puncture is only as deep as the skin. What should be used among ointments when applying leeches and water, and what the person undergoing bloodletting should beware of. As for those whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and with narrow pores.
The areas where the leeches will be applied should be ointmented with substances that are clarifying, softening, and dissolving. In summer, ointments such as Khiri oil, violet oil, sweet almond oil, or cucumber seed oil may be used.
In winter, ointments such as narcissus oil, lily oil, chamomile oil, or hyacinth oil may be used. If the phlegm is thick and cold, then use oils like murtajush oil, mandrake oil, frankincense oil, or spikenard oil and similar ones.
If the person undergoing bloodletting has wide pores and soft flesh, ointment should be avoided. These individuals should wash their leech bites after bleeding with rose water or cold water or wild grape water or pumpkin water or daisy water and similar ones. As for those whose blood is very moist,
They should wash their leech bites with vinegar or water from wormwood and sumac and similar ones. As for those whose phlegm is thick, they should wash their leech bites with old wine or murtajush water or spikenard decoction or chamomile decoction and similar ones. It is important to avoid bloodletting in a bathhouse or immediately after bathing.
It is advisable to perform cupping one hour or two hours after coming out of the bath, and it is not advisable for anyone to sleep after cupping. What should be done by the person undergoing cupping and the practitioner before and after cupping.
First, you should consider if the person undergoing cupping or bloodletting has jaundice and if his blood is sharp and inflamed; then he should take cooling agents such as pomegranate, chicory with vinegar, lettuce, scallions, radish, and similar. His diet should include thin gruel, mutton stews, and similar foods.
And if his constitution is cold, he should be given honey syrup, mubyah syrup or bazaar scallion syrup, and moderate aromatic wine that lies between old and new. He should be instructed to eat less, and his diet should include thin gruel, millet porridge, birds, and young pigeons stewed with eggs. It is advisable for the person to drink more on the day of cupping or bloodletting than he eats.
It may be advisable to give some people the Tariq al-Farouq elixir or musk medicine or siltha before cupping or bloodletting, or after it, to strengthen the principal organs and thin the blood. It should not be given by inexperienced practitioners.
As for unrestricted cupping, it is the type of cupping placed on the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, navel, and the area of the kidneys and upper thigh. These organs cannot tolerate restricted cupping, and what is intended with them is to draw blood from one part to another.
As we place the cupping vessel on the breasts in the case of epistaxis or to induce a cold vapor from the part if it has become congested, so do we place it on the abdomen and navel; for it loosens the part and warms it, removing pain by releasing that vapor.
It may be placed on the kidneys if there is an obstruction or stone, for its strength of suction may open the obstruction or dislodge the stone from its place. It also does this when applied to the liver and spleen in case of vapor that has become disturbed in them. These cupping vessels may be used empty by suction only.
It may be used with fire, and it may also be used filled with warm water for ailments of the sciatica. This is done by filling the cupping vessel, which should be large, with hot water alone or with water in which some herbs suitable for this purpose have been boiled.
Then it is placed, filled, on the affected area and held in place, removed, and reapplied as needed. This is an image of the cupping vessel that is used with fire:.
Its mouth should be two fingers wide as depicted, and its depth should be half a finger. There should be a small hole on one side, about halfway up, the size of which an acupuncture needle can fit. It is made of Chinese copper or yellow brass with a thick rim.
It should be smooth, level, and polished so as not to injure the part when it is applied. In the middle of it, there should be a protruding piece of copper or iron where the candle with fire is placed. These cupping vessels can also be made larger than described or smaller depending on the diseases.
And prepare it as needed, for the scarificator of children and thin people is different from that of men with robust bodies. As for how to place this scarificator on fire onto the part, light a wick made of flax or a small candle made of bitumen with fire, and place it in the middle of the erect rod which is at the center of the scarificator.
Let the fire rise upwards towards the bottom of the scarificator so that the patient's body does not get burned. Then place it on the part, and keep a finger on the hole we mentioned until when we hold the scarificator as needed, we remove the finger.
Steam will then come out of that hole, and the scarificator will release at its position. Then light the wick as described and repeat if necessary. As for the scarificator used to treat hydrocele with water, it does not have an erect rod or a hole.
It is used by filling it with water and placing it on the part only. This is its image:.
The larger this scarificator is, the more water it can hold, and thus it is better.
Chapter Seventy-Seven.
On the Application of Leeches.
Leeches are used in most cases on parts where scarificators cannot be applied, either due to their small size such as the lip and gum, or because the part is devoid of flesh like a finger or nose.
The method of using them is to select leeches from fresh, clean water, free from contaminants. Then leave them in freshwater for a day and night until they are hungry and empty inside. Then the body is first purged by bloodletting or cupping, and then the affected part is cleansed.
Until it turns red, then place it on the area; when it is full, it will fall off. If possible, sucking the area with a lancing instrument is more effective; otherwise, wash the area with vinegar and then with plenty of water, rub it, and press it. If this continues, blood will flow after the leech falls off, which is considered beneficial bleeding. Then wet a linen cloth in cold water.
Place it above until the bleeding stops. If there is excessive blood, sprinkle finely powdered saffron, oak bark, or similar astringents until the bleeding stops, or apply slices of peeled colocasia to the area.
Leave it until the colocasia slices adhere to the area, as this will stop the bleeding. If leeches need to be reapplied, do not use the same leech if possible; instead, apply fresh blood to the area to attract new leeches.
Or insert a needle into the area to release some blood, then place the leech; once it senses blood, it will adhere. If you want to remove it, sprinkle some geranium, salt, or ashes on it; it will detach from the site.
The Third Chapter.
On Bone Setting and Fracture Treatment.
This chapter is also part of the craft required in the practice of medicine, dealing with setting fractures and resetting dislocated bones.
Know, my children, that this chapter is claimed by ignorant physicians, common people, and those who have never examined any book of the ancients or read a single word about it.
For this reason, this art has become non-existent in our country, and I have never found anyone who excels in it at all. And I only gained what I have through the long hours of reading the books of the ancients and my dedication to understanding them until I extracted that knowledge from them, then I devoted myself to experience and practice throughout my life.
And I have illustrated for you in this chapter all that is within my knowledge on the subject. My experience has been applied to it after presenting it to you, purging it of unnecessary length, abbreviating it as much as possible, clarifying it to the utmost extent, and depicting many illustrations of the instruments used in it.
For it is an addition to the explanation as I did in the preceding chapters, and there is no power but with God, the Exalted, the Almighty.
The First Section.
It is best to present summaries and general points regarding the treatment of broken bones before we begin to describe each fractured or dislocated member one by one. It is necessary to mention in the beginning of this chapter some statements and sections. It first compels you to understand it and grasp its truth, both for yourself and for those who are eager to learn this noble craft.
So I say that when anyone sustains a fracture, dislocation, sprain, or fall, it is necessary first to proceed with reduction or purging, or both. Unless there is a hindrance such as weakness of strength or the one affected by it is a child or an extremely old person, or the time is excessively hot or cold.
Then he should limit his diet to cold legumes, poultry meat, and broth; avoid drinks, tough meats, overeating, and any food that fills the veins with blood until the swelling is safe and there is no risk of fluid accumulation in the area.
At that time, the patient should return to his usual regimen. When the broken bone begins to heal, the patient should be fed nutritious foods that are thick and hearty, such as tripe, rice, heads, feet, and calves of cattle, which have viscosity. Eggs, soft fish, thick drinks, and similar foods should also be part of this regimen, as it will make the healing of the fracture faster and better. Note that if bones are broken in strong men or elderly people, they cannot naturally reunite and heal as they once were.
Due to the dryness and hardness of their bones, it is not possible for them to naturally reconnect and heal. However, extremely soft bones, such as those of very young children, may unite and adhere. The body generates new tissue around the broken bone that resembles glue, which thickens and adheres to the bone, binding it together from all sides.
It binds together until it becomes as strong and secure as it was originally, so that nothing impedes its function.
For this reason, it is necessary to provide the patient with foods that have strength, viscosity, and thickness as we have said. And know that fractures differ in types according to the difference in body parts because a fracture of the shinbone differs from a skull fracture, and a rib fracture differs from a spinal fracture.
Similarly, all other body parts differ from one another, and we will come to describe each type of fracture explained in its chapter detailedly separately. Also, the type of bone fracture may itself differ.
Because it may be a transverse fracture without any fragments being produced, or it may be a longitudinal fracture with fragments that are either clean or not clean and protruding. The fracture may also occur with a wound or tear in the skin, or it may be a slight crack.
And each type has a specific method for its reduction, as will be mentioned in detail where appropriate. What identifies a bone fracture is its misalignment, irregularity, appearance to the senses, and brittleness when you touch it with your hand.
When there is no obvious misalignment or brittleness, and you do not feel any disturbance when palpating the bone, and the patient does not experience much pain, then there is no fracture. It may be a sprain or a slight crack, so it should not be moved by pulling or pressing at all.
Rather, apply the medicines that will be mentioned later which are appropriate for the condition, then bind it gently. Know that if the bone has split into two without producing any fragments, each part may have moved away from its partner.
You should proceed to correct and even out the condition before a hot swelling occurs. If a hot swelling does occur, wait until it subsides, then adjust it as best you can with gentleness and ingenuity.
Know that it is easier to set and adjust a bone without splinters than one with splinters, then secure it as will be mentioned later. If the bone contains splinters, it is necessary to extend the broken limb from both sides, whether hand or foot.
Use your hands if the limb is small, or use two ropes, or combine a rope with your hands. Place the limb in a straight position that matches its natural shape so that when the parts of the broken bone align, you should carefully and gently return any protrusions to their proper places using whatever method you can manage.
Strive to avoid causing pain or discomfort to the patient with your actions. Aim to align one bone with its counterpart in the best possible manner, and at this time, feel and examine them with your hand. If you notice anything out of place, correct and adjust it as much as you can.
Avoid excessive stretching and strong pulling, as many unskilled people often do. Such actions frequently cause hot swelling or delay healing in the limb, as I have observed repeatedly due to their mistakes.
After setting, perfecting, and securing the limb, ensure that it remains still and at ease. Advise the patient to avoid moving the limb whether awake or asleep, during transitions or agitation, while defecating, and in all movements, as much as possible. Ensure the limb is positioned so that it does not cause pain.
This is because when he feels pain or discomfort in the position of the limb, he should move it to another position where he does not feel any pain, and he should ensure that this new position is even and straight.
To prevent the limb from becoming crooked when it is set, and as for how to pull a broken limb, I will describe that to you. Know that broken limbs vary in size, large or small, and their form; those that are smaller, such as the arm, finger, and wrist, and the like.
For smaller bandages, they should be soft and delicate. However, for tougher areas such as the thigh, back, and chest, the bandages should be wide and sturdy because a broad ligature is necessary for large limbs, providing even tension around it without any unevenness or misalignment.
Then after you finish adjusting, apply the appropriate plaster. Apply gentle pressure to the site of the fracture, then begin wrapping the bandage around the site of the fracture itself for three or four turns as needed by the limb. Slightly tighten the bandage with your hand and then move it towards the upper part of the fracture site.
Apply less tension than you applied to the fractured area, then move slightly away from the site of the fracture and gradually loosen the tension a little. Take a suitable piece from the correct place, then take another bandage and wrap it around the fractured area as well.
Wrap for a few more turns, then move the wrapping to the lower part of the fracture. The tension and looseness of the wrap should be as described for the first upper wrapping. Then place gentle padding or cloth between the layers of bandages to straighten any misalignment of the fracture.
If it is crooked, do not put anything in it; then wrap another bandage around it, and then apply firm splints to these wrappings from your supplies if there is no swelling or hot inflammation in the limb.
If there is swelling or hot inflammation, apply something to reduce that swelling and dispel the puffiness, and leave it for several days; then at that time, secure the splints. The splints should be made of half sections of large reeds prepared with care, or they can be made of sieve wood.
Those that are made from pine resin, or date palm leaves, or strips of cloth, or rush matting, or similar materials available for this purpose. The width of each splint should be as described and in this particular shape.
Except that the splint placed directly on the fracture itself should be slightly thicker and wider than the other splints. As for the length of the splint, it should be made according to the size of the broken limb, whether large or small. The shape of the splint:.
Then secure the splints with another bandage as you did initially, and then tie them securely from above with strong threads as we mentioned regarding the binding; it should be tighter around the site of the fracture. The tighter the binding, the closer it is to the fracture; the farther from the fracture, the less tight the binding should be.
The threads should be of medium thickness and softness; they should preferably be made of wet linen, because if the threads are thick, as inexperienced people often do by making them from strips of twisted linen, it is a great mistake.
Because the tensioning becomes uneven, and thin threads are not suitable because you cannot achieve the desired tension with them. It should not be less than a finger's width between one bandage and another.
If the patient is harmed by the edges of the bandages after proper tensioning, place soft linen or fluffy wool underneath so that it does not cause any harm. As for when there is a fracture accompanied by a wound and a break in the skin occurs, we will discuss this separately.
Know that not every broken part should be bandaged from the first day. If the part is large, it should not be bandaged until after five or seven days, or more, depending on your assurance of avoiding the occurrence of a hot swelling.
This is the description of dressings used by earlier practitioners to treat fractures, dislocations, and other injuries. It is a general and brief dressing that stabilizes fractures and suits most temperaments, especially for children and women because it neither causes heat nor cold.
Take dust from the mill, which is the fine bran that sticks to the walls of the mill when grinding. Mix it as it is without sieving, using egg whites, and make the consistency neither too thick nor too thin, then apply it.
The description of another dressing for treating fractures, dislocations, and injuries: Take masha, ladan, aqakiya, rasan, mughat, and sugar from each ten dirhams; take mor and sadap from each five dirhams; and take athl twenty dirhams, and Armenian or Roman clay twenty dirhams. Pound everything and mix with water of thuja or with egg white if the patient’s constitution is delicate. Then use this dressing, for it is well composed; it quickly sets broken bones and suits most people due to its moderation.
The description of another dressing that is also beneficial for fractures and contusions: take ten dirhams each of sifted flour, fresh meat broth, and white poppy head; five dirhams each of rue and horehound in powder; six dirhams of Armenian clay; twenty dirhams which are all finely pounded and sieved, then mixed with water or egg white and used.
A dressing for joints and displaced bones to alleviate pain associated with them, as well as for bone concussion and fractures: take cleaned wool and dip it in vinegar and cooked oil, then apply it to the affected area. This dressing does not have the strength to set bones.
However, it is excellent for reducing hot swelling and relieving pain in particular. The description of another dressing to set a broken bone: take the leaves of black mulberry and wild opium poppy, pound them together, and apply them wet as a dressing. Another brief dressing is used when a fracture has begun to heal.
To further resolve the remaining swelling, take the root of white poppy, chamomile, violets, and cornmeal in equal parts; pound them all together and mix with glue if the part is not irritated. If it is irritated, then mix with cilantro water or plain water and use it.
The description of another stronger dressing for resolving solid swelling that occurs when a bone is healing: take the root of white poppy, flax seeds, fenugreek, rosemary, myrtle, violets, and chamomile in equal parts from each.
All should be pounded and mixed with water from the river or with fresh water or with plaster, depending on the heat of the limb and its calmness; as for Hippocrates, he did not mention in his book that something should be placed on the broken limb when setting it.
Except for the Qirwatyi made of wax and oil, nothing else; he described it as being medium between thickness and thinness. As for Galen, he thought that when setting a broken limb, things that are dry but have some heat should be applied, such as aloeswood, hellebore, frankincense, and the like as mentioned above.
A dressing for weakness and pain: Take mallow, lentils, human cut hair or bird feathers, okra, and equal parts of salt, all of which should be ground, sifted, mixed, and applied as a dressing. As for the amount that the bandage should allow to remain.
Then it should not be loosened until you observe; if the patient does not experience pain, itching, or movement of the broken bone from its place, do not loosen it for many days. If severe itching occurs in the area, or painful anxiety, or swelling appears.
Then act promptly and loosen it at the time, do not delay; remove the dressing from it. Then take a soft cloth or sponge freely wetted and soak it in warm water, then wash the area with it until the itching subsides and the pain eases, then let the limb rest for an hour.
Then apply clean wool soaked in vinegar and oil or rose fat, and bind it on for a night until you ensure that the hot swelling is prevented, the swelling of the limb subsides, and its pain disappears. Then return to gentle binding and minimal dressing, do not tighten as you did initially.
Be gentle with him until he recovers. If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, and all other symptoms have completely disappeared, and you need to apply a bandage or compress, prepare it as you did initially. If nothing has happened to the part as we mentioned.
Do not remove it except after three, four, five, or seven days, or even leave it for twenty days, all depending on what you see regarding the condition of the part as we have said, until the bone has united and the flesh is nearly healed around it.
At that time, also increase the compression more than your initial one and also increase the richness of the patient's diet as previously mentioned. If you see that the site of the fracture has dried up and wasted more than it should, then know that nourishment is unable to reach it.
So soak it in warm water each time you remove the bandage, and do this every three days. Also slightly reduce the compression, as this will facilitate nourishment reaching the part and promote quick healing. As for what ignorant bone setters do, causing the bone to break again.
If it does not heal properly at first and heals crookedly, then this is an error in their actions and a great danger. Even if it were correct, the early scholars would have mentioned it in their books and followed it, but I have seen none of them do so at all. The correct approach is not to follow it.
The Second Section.
On Contingent Fractures in the Head.
Types of accidental fractures in the head are many and their shapes differ, and their causes are diverse. Some fractures occur from a sword blow and may completely split the bone to reach the membrane beneath the bone. As when cleaving wood.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called a cleaving fracture. It may also be that the sword cuts part of the bone but only splits its surface without penetrating through it; this type of fracture is called an avulsion. The wound caused by these fractures may be either large or small.
There are fractures that are comminuted or contused, caused by a blow from a stone or falling on a stone or similar objects. This type of fracture may either penetrate nearly to the membrane beneath the bone or be limited to the surface of the bone.
The wound caused by these fractures may also be either large or small. There are also hidden fractures in the bone, as thin as a hair, which is a slight concussion.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called a hairline fracture. There is also a type that occurs from falling or being struck by a stone or similar objects, where the bone's surface is driven inward, much like how a metal pot develops an indentation when struck.
This most often occurs in the soft, pliable skulls of infants. There may be fragments that are displaced or not displaced for all these types of fractures, and we will discuss their treatment in due course. We will identify all these types of fractures through examination.
Examine it with probes and remove the diseased flesh from it. As for the hair-like type, it is recognized by exposing the bone and wiping and smearing ink on it; the fracture will appear black. As for the treatment of fractures, first observe the symptoms of the patient.
If you see from his symptoms a clear indication of fear, such as vomiting bile, stretching out, loss of reason, loss of voice, fainting, severe fever, protrusion and redness of the eyes, and similar symptoms, then do not approach the patient or treat him, for with these symptoms death is inevitable in most cases.
If you see symptoms that do not alarm you and you hope for his safety, then proceed with his treatment. This is the case when a wounded person comes to you at the beginning of his injury, especially if this occurs during the winter days.
It is essential to strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day, in any case. If it is during the summer days, it is necessary to hasten the removal of the bone before the seventh day so that what is under the bone, such as the membrane, does not become corrupted, thus avoiding those symptoms we have described.
If the fracture of the bone has reached the membrane covering the brain and there is also comminution and contusion, it is necessary to cut away the fractured and contused part as I will describe to you. This involves shaving the head of the injured patient.
Expose the bone from whichever side is convenient for you and according to the shape of the wound, and what causes least distress to the patient. If during the examination of the bone, bleeding or a hot swelling appears, address it appropriately.
It is that you fill the area with cloth soaked in syrup, rose oil, and fat; cleanse the wound until the swelling subsides and bleeding is avoided, then proceed to scrape the bone or extract it, which can be done in one of two ways.
As for one method, you should cut the bone with a fine, narrow-bladed saw, and this is its illustration:.
Then use another saw after this one, slightly wider, and this is its illustration:.
Then use yet another saw, wider than the second one. It is necessary to have several different saws, some wider and some shorter than others, with extremely sharp edges; they should be made of Indian iron or good steel.
Use a hammer to tap gently on the saw to avoid shaking the head and injuring it. If the bone is strong and hard, you should drill holes around it before using the saws with drills that are not deep, which they called non-penetrating drills.
Because they do not penetrate beyond the thickness of the skull bone. The drill has a circular flange below its sharp head, resembling a ring or small circle that prevents it from penetrating further than the thickness of the bone.
You should have many of these drills available, each suitable for the thickness of the bone. Each drill should match the length and width of its sharp tip to the thickness of that skull, so you are prepared with a suitable drill for every skull thickness. Here is an illustration of three types. Of punctures, large, medium, and small:.
As for the method of puncturing around a broken bone, you should place the puncture instrument on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated, then move the puncture instrument to another location. Leave a distance between each puncture equal to the thickness of a finger or something similar.
Then, cut between each two punctures using the appropriate tool with as much gentleness as possible, as we have said, until you remove the bone either by hand or with some other instrument that you have prepared for this purpose.
Use delicate tools such as cotton pads and clamps, and be extremely careful that the puncture or cutting instruments do not touch any surrounding tissues. Once you have removed the bone and separated it from adhered membranes if they were attached to it, you should clean and prepare the area thoroughly.
Smooth out any roughness remaining in the bone using another tool similar to a cutting instrument, but it should be thinner and more delicate than other cutting tools. If there are small pieces of bone or fragments left, gently remove them with the appropriate instruments you have.
Then, treat the wound afterward with bandages and ointments that will be mentioned. As for the other approach to this work, it is extremely simple and free from risk; Galen has described and highly praised it. This is his saying on the matter. First, you should start by exposing the part of the bone in the place where the fracture is most severe. Once you have exposed that part, place one end of this lens-like cutting instrument under it. This is its shape:.
It should be smooth in its lens-like part so that it does not cut anything, and sharp on the sides extending in length as you see, so that the lens-like part rests against the diaphragm and the cutting edge of the section is applied to the bone; then strike the section from one side.
Using a small hammer until all the bone is cut gently as it turns, and you are safe from any frightening accident to the membrane, even if the surgeon is the most ignorant and cowardly person in the world. Indeed, even if he were asleep.
If any part of the membrane remains attached to the bone in certain areas, then scrape it off with the lens-like end of the cutting instrument itself and gently remove it; it will come away without harm or fear.
And if the bone fracture did not penetrate to the membrane, and what was broken off from the bone is rough with small splinters remaining in it, then you should remove that roughness and extract those splinters using fine probes of various sizes so that you can use whichever one fits best for each situation as dictated by your work.
And the same applies to the broken bone and its shape; you should use the widest of those probes first for removing roughness from the bone, then use one that is finer than it, and continue this process step by step until you reach the finest and thinnest probe.
As for the smaller hairline cracks and minor fractures, you should apply treatment to each one according to what leads to its healing. This is something that is not hidden from anyone who has even the slightest experience in this craft and has understood what we have written and clarified about major fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed when you extract the bone, you should take a piece of linen cloth according to the size of the wound, dip it in rose oil, and place it over the mouth of the wound. Then take another folded or triangular cloth. Dip it in syrup and rose oil, then place it on the first cloth. Do this as lightly as possible so as not to burden the serosa. Then use a wide bandage on top but do not tighten it beyond what is necessary to secure the wound only.
Leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are safe from the hot swelling. Then loosen it and use after that medicines which have a drying effect, such as the root of saxifrage, powdered linseed, carthamus powder, safflower, and similar ones. Make an ointment from these drugs.
Apply it to the wound dry as it is. In general, use a medicine that cleanses without causing irritation. Strive to ensure that your treatment of the wound keeps it clean, free from grease and dirt, and do not allow pus to accumulate in it at all.
Because if pus accumulates on the membrane of the brain, it will damage and corrupt it, causing great harm to the patient. This often occurs in the serosa of the head when the bone is exposed, especially if care is neglected in its treatment.
If there is blackness on its surface, observe if the patient exhibits the symptoms we have mentioned. If so, know that he is surely doomed. Even if the blackness occurred due to a medicine applied and had the potential to cause such discoloration.
Take one part honey and three parts rose oil, mix them well, then apply it to a piece of cloth and place it on the abdomen. Then treat it with various treatments as necessary until it heals.
Chapter Three.
On the repair of the nose if it is broken.
Know that the upper part of the nose breaks entirely or partially on one side because it consists of two bones. The lower part, however, is cartilaginous and does not break; instead, it may suffer contusion, deformation, or perforation.
If one side breaks, you should insert your little finger into the nostril and align the break from inside with your index finger and thumb from outside until the nose returns to its natural shape. Do this gently and ensure that your actions do not cause pain to the patient.
If the break is in the upper part of the nose and your finger cannot reach it, you should smooth it with the tip of a slender splint. If the break affects both sides, do the same. It is best to set it on the first day after the fracture.
If possible, do so; otherwise, wait until the seventh or tenth day when the hot swelling has subsided. Then insert a thread of cotton cloth into the nostril if the break is on one side, or two threads if it is on both sides.
The threads should be of a thickness that fills the nostril. Some early surgeons mentioned soaking the threads in fat and changing them daily, but I do not see this as necessary. Instead, it is better to soak the threads in egg white mixed with flour dust.
Then leave the packing until the bone is set and the cartilage is firm, and you may insert into the nose for the site of the packing goose feathers wrapped in soft cloth, so that it prevents the fracture more effectively and so that the patient does not refuse to breathe.
This is not essential; if you wish, you may do it, or you can use dressings. If a hot swelling occurs in the nose during your work, apply a cerotic bandage or cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil or some ointment like diachylum.
If no hot swelling occurs, it is advisable to dress the outside with semolina and fenugreek flour mixed with egg white, then place a soft bandage over it, and do not bind the nose at all. If the nasal bones are fractured slightly,
or if they are crushed, it is advisable to incise them and remove the fragments with a suitable instrument, then suture the incision and treat it with ointments that promote healing. If there is a wound inside the nose, it should be treated with dressings and lead tubes should be used. until it heals.
Chapter Four.
On the reduction of the lower jaw when it is dislocated.
If the lower jaw is fractured but there is no accompanying wound, observe it. If the fracture is only on the outside and does not break into two parts and凹陷到内部，则其识别会较为容易。请重新提供需要翻译的原文，以便我可以准确地进行翻译。如果骨折仅在外侧并且没有分裂成两部分并向内凹陷，则其识别会较为容易。请确认是否需要进一步翻译或调整这段话。
It is appropriate, if the fracture is on the right side, to insert the index finger of the left hand into the patient's mouth. Similarly, if the fracture is on the left side, insert the index finger of the right hand and lift the hump of the fracture gently from inside to outside.
Use your other hand from outside the bone to control and align it. If the jaw fracture is split into two parts, it should be pulled evenly from both sides until it is properly aligned. If there is overgrowth in the teeth, ...
or if they separate, secure them with gold or silver thread or a wire until you expect them to stay. Then place a kerchief on the broken jaw and put a folded cloth over it. Place a large, tight bandage or a piece of leather sole equal in length to the jaw over the cloth. Then tie it from above as you see fit, ensuring it aligns properly so that it does not come loose. Instruct the patient to remain quiet and still and give them soft, easily digestible food. If you suspect any change in appearance, address it on the third day.
Then tie it from above as you see fit, ensuring it aligns properly so that it does not come loose. Instruct the patient to remain quiet and still and give them soft, easily digestible food. If you suspect any change in appearance, address it on the third day. Then repair the dressing with flour sieved from a millstone mixed with egg whites or semolina, after removing the kerchief. Place a soft bandage over the dressing. As long as the dressing adheres and there is no change in the condition of the bone, leave it as it is.
Do not remove it until the injury is healed and the fracture has consolidated, as this often takes about three weeks. If inflammation with heat appears during this period, use what we have mentioned repeatedly to alleviate it until the swelling subsides.
And if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, you should examine it. If a piece or pieces of bone have become dislodged, handle their removal with care using whatever tool seems appropriate for the task. If the mouth of the wound is narrow, widen it with scissors as needed.
Then, after removing those pieces of bone and if nothing remains, outline the mouth of the wound if it is wide; otherwise, apply one of the ointments suitable for that purpose. And sew the wound until it heals.
Chapter Five.
On the Treatment of the Clavicle When It Is Fractured.
The clavicle most often breaks in front, at the end of the shoulder, and its fracture occurs in one of three ways: either it breaks and splits into two parts without producing any fragments, which is easiest to treat.
Or it may produce fragments, which is more difficult to treat. If the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, you should have two assistants present: one to secure the arm adjacent to the broken clavicle, and the other to extend the neck toward the opposite side.
Then, straighten the fracture with your fingers until its shape is as it should be, without any protrusions or misalignments. If more extension is needed, place a ball of cloth or cotton under the armpit of the patient.
Adjust the position of the clavicle as needed and extend it while lifting. Press on the ball with your hand until the fracture is properly aligned. If you cannot pull the end of the clavicle outward because it has moved inward, the patient should lie face down.
A pillow of medium height should be placed under his armpit, and the servant should press his armpit downwards so that the clavicle in the depth rises upwards. Then the fracture is corrected and smoothed with your fingers.
If you feel that a fragment of the clavicle has broken and is moving, you should incise it and gently remove that fragment. If the fragment is embedded in the bone, devise a method to cut it using one of the tools prepared for this purpose. After placing the instrument that protects the viscera under the clavicle, which is made of wood or iron, and this is its image:.
It resembles a spoon without a bowl, and its width should be according to the size of the bone, whether large or small. As for its length, it should be appropriate for the work you need to do. It should have two ends, one wider and the other narrower, as you can see.
If the wound you incised when removing a bone fragment is wide and there is no risk of inflammation, then sew the edges of the wound together. If the wound is small or if you fear inflammation, dress the wound with a bandage, the size of which should be proportional to the size of the incision.
If inflammation occurs, apply the wound with rose oil and vinegar or drink, and treat it accordingly. As for setting a bone without a wound or incision, wrap the bone with a dressing made from flour powder mixed with egg whites. Place a soft bandage on it, then place a ball under his armpit if necessary. Then take a long scarf, about an inch wide or so, and wrap a folded bandage over the padding and dressing. Finally, take a splint made from a thin piece of wood. Its width should be three fingers, and in length it should be the same. Then wrap it in a cloth, then wrap that cloth along with the splint in the part of the turban that covers the site of the fracture, then tighten the turban around the fracture as it rotates. Place it around his neck and under his healthy armpit and under the affected armpit, and wrap it several times on each side. And you know well how tightening it properly controls the fracture securely, as this is not hidden from you.
The main goal is that the splint should not move from the broken bone. For this reason, it is necessary to check on the patient every day. Whenever the bandage loosens and you see that the splint has moved, fix it and tighten the bandage. Then make sure the patient sleeps on his back and place a small pillow under his armpit when he sleeps at night.
To raise his shoulder off the side so that the fracture of the collarbone rises with the shoulder, or tie his arm to his neck. Do not loosen the bandage unless an incident such as itching or swelling occurs in the area after twelve days.
Then renew the dressing if you see that it is necessary to do so. Tighten it and leave it until the fracture heals and becomes complicated. The clavicle fracture becomes more severe and stronger after twenty-eight days. It may also occur in some people in less than that.
Chapter Six.
On the setting of a broken shoulder. The shoulder is rarely broken in its broad part; rather, it is broken at its edges. Whenever a part of it breaks or breaks in the middle, and this can only be known by touch. According to the shape of the break, treatment should be administered.
Smooth it and restore it to its natural shape by any means possible, then apply dust from a millstone mixed with egg white and soft wax. Place over this a folded cloth pad, then put on a wide splint made of wood.
Make the splint as wide as the shoulder or slightly wider. If there is a depression under the splint in any part of the shoulder, smooth out that depression with soft wax so that the splint lies flat. Then secure it from above with a long headcloth.
Secure it firmly and make sure the splint does not move from its place. Check the bandage every day; whenever the bandage loosens, tighten it and adjust the splint if it has moved. Let the patient lie on their healthy side. The shoulder will heal in twenty days.
It may take one day or twenty-five days. Once this period is complete, remove the bandage with confidence, for it is among bones that are not feared to be crushed or fractured. If a bone shard protrudes and irritates under the skin, cut into it and extract the shard. And follow what was mentioned regarding the fracture of the collarbone for soothing any hot swelling that may occur.
Chapter Seven.
On healing a broken rib.
The chest may be fractured in the middle, but this is rare. Its sides are more likely to be broken and fragmented. If the middle part is fractured, it will tilt downward, causing severe pain, difficulty breathing, and coughing.
It may also cause bleeding, and the fracture in the bone becomes evident. To treat it, the patient should lie on their back with a pillow placed between their shoulders. Then, press their armpits, gather the ribs with hands from both sides, and carefully align them as much as possible.
Continue until the shape of the bone returns to its proper form. Then, apply bandages and a splint. Place a splint made from a thin piece of lime wood or cypress, or something similar that is light, wrapped in cloth over it. Then carefully secure it to the broken bone so that it does not shift. Pass the bandage around the curvature several times and tie it tightly, then check the bandage at all times and retighten it whenever it loosens.
If the need arises to remove it during eating due to pain, swelling, or discomfort in the area, immediately loosen it and remove the bandage. Address any issues that arise with appropriate treatment, then replace the bandage if necessary. And keep it tight until he is healed.
Chapter Eight.
On healing broken ribs. It is known that fractures occur in the ribs in the thick parts near the back, while their front ends are more likely to suffer contusions because they are cartilaginous. This can be easily discerned by touch when examining with fingers.
Repair it by aligning the fracture with your fingers until the shape is properly set, then bandage it. If necessary, secure the broken bone with a splint. If the fractured ribs are inclined inward, severe pain will occur in the injured person.
And in cases where the protrusion is like a sharp projection, it presses against the membrane causing severe difficulty in breathing and coughing, as well as expelling large amounts of blood. This condition is difficult to treat, and the early practitioners devised many ingenious solutions for it.
Some have said that the patient's diet should consist of foods that produce gas and bloating, causing the abdomen to expand and push the fracture outward. We disagree with this approach because it may confirm the development of a hot swelling. If such a swelling has not yet occurred, it might induce its formation; if it has already developed, it will exacerbate and reinforce it.
