Some suggest placing a leech on the affected area and sucking it forcefully, which seems to follow reasonable logic, though there is concern that the weakened state of the area might cause excessive bleeding. Others have suggested covering the area with wool soaked in hot oil.
It should be packed between the ribs until it is full, so that when wrapped around, the bandage lies smoothly. Then, treat the patient for a protrusion with appropriate diet and medication, as severe discomfort may make it unbearable for the patient. The bone was pressing painfully against the membrane, and we were concerned for the patient's safety. Therefore, it is necessary to make an incision at the site and expose the broken rib, then place a device that protects the serosa as previously described, carefully cut the bone, and remove it.
Then we stitch together the edges of the wound if it is large and treat it with ointments until it heals. If a hot swelling appears during this time, apply bandages soaked in rose oil to the area and also treat the patient internally with remedies that reduce the swelling.
He should lie on the side that is more comfortable for him until he heals.
Chapter Nine.
On the Repair of Spinal and Neck Vertebrae.
As for the neck bones, if they sustain a fracture, which is rare, but more commonly they suffer contusions. The same applies to the vertebrae of the spine. If this occurs and you wish to determine whether recovery is possible or not. Observe if his hands have become weak, numb, and lifeless, unable to move, stretch, or grasp. If you prick them with a needle and he does not feel it or experience pain, know that in most cases he will not recover and is likely to perish.
If, however, he can move them and feel the prick or touch, then know that the spinal cord is safe and the patient will recover with treatment. If a vertebra in the spine is similarly affected and you wish to determine whether recovery is possible or not,
Examine his legs; if you see that they have become weak and exhibit the same symptoms as the hands, and when he lies on his back, gas and feces are expelled involuntarily, and when he lies on his stomach, urine is released involuntarily. And if he lies on his back and is unable to urinate, know that he is doomed; therefore, do not bother with treating him. If none of this occurs to him, then the situation is less serious. The treatment for what has occurred is to aim at calming the hot tumor.
Apply rose oil alone or mixed with roasted egg yolks to the injured vertebra three times a day until the hot swelling subsides. Then apply one of the reinforcing and drying bandages, secure it with a cloth strip, and instruct him to rest and remain quiet.
He should not sleep on the side where he feels pain until he is healed. If a fragment of bone or something else has become loose due to the injury, you should cut through the skin and remove that piece of bone. Then bring the edges of the wound together if it is large by suturing and treat with healing ointments until it heals. If the last bone of the sacrum, which is the coccyx, is fractured, you should insert your left thumb into the coccyx.
Align the broken bone with the other hand as well as you can, then apply a bandage and splint if necessary, and secure it.
If you feel a broken fragment in it, cut through the tissue and remove it, then treat the wound as previously described until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
On the Treatment of Hip Fractures.
Rarely are the bones of the spine broken; if they are broken, it is because they fracture at their ends and split lengthwise, tilting inward. This causes pain in the affected area, bruising, and numbness in the leg on the side where the break occurred.
To treat it, run your hand over it until you locate the fracture and determine its shape. If the fracture is only at the ends, smooth out the break as best you can so that its shape resembles its natural form.
If the fracture is lengthwise or has tilted inward, lay the patient on their stomach so you can set the fracture. Once it is smoothed out, wrap it with bandages and then apply a splint made of wood or leather, securing it tightly.
Do not fear that the fracture will move or that the splint will shift. Adjust the padding from the flanks to ensure even compression, and instruct the patient to sleep on their back or on the correct side. If a hot swelling occurs,
stop stretching and treating it until the swelling subsides, and apply what is necessary to reduce the swelling as previously described. Then return to properly setting and securing it. If there are shards or fragments in the bone or at its ends, they should not be removed.
Do not disturb it; instead, smooth it from the outside as we have said and leave it to heal without further manipulation.
Chapter Eleven.
On the Treatment of a Fractured Humerus.
The elbow joint is what lies between the forearm and the top of the shoulder. If it is broken, it should be set in one of two ways. One way is to take a curved, smooth stick of medium thickness as shown in this illustration:.
Then two straps are tied to its ends, and it is hung from a high place while the patient sits on a chair. The broken arm is then placed on the stick until the armpit is pressed against the middle of the curve. Then something heavy is suspended above it.
Or a servant can extend it downward, then the doctor sets the fracture with both hands until the break is properly aligned. The other method is to have the patient lie face down and suspend his hand from his neck with a strap.
Then order two servants to control the area above and below the fracture with their hands, one for each side. If you want a stronger extension, secure the area under and over the fracture with straps, and have each servant pull in opposite directions.
If the fracture is near the shoulder end, the strap should be placed under the armpit with the other part beneath the fracture towards the elbow. Similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, the straps should be placed over that area and on the elbow itself.
Then align the fracture gently without force until it is properly set and well-aligned. If no inflammatory swelling appears, apply compression; if it does, wait until the seventh day to compress it. Place a moistened cloth soaked in vinegar and rose oil on it.
Until the swelling subsides, then at that time bind it; the method of binding is to carry the bandage over the fracture, then place a strip of new cloth on top of the bandage, then bring the arm together with the shoulder, and place his hand open on his shoulder.
And place the bandages and binding on the shoulder and arm so that the arm serves as a substitute for splints, unless something prevents you from doing so or there is no change in the broken bone. If you fear any change in the condition of the broken bone, then use splints.
This means placing a splint directly over the fracture that is wider and stronger than other splints, with a space of one finger's width between each splint. The length of the splints should be according to the length of the fracture, adding three finger-widths on each side.
Then bind the splints with the binding method mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
It should be that your binding is tighter around the site of the fracture.
The binding should be less tight as it moves away from the fracture. If you see that placing the splints and binding as we have described is appropriate when setting the limb, then do so.
If you fear a hot swelling, then leave the binding and splints until the seventh day as we have said. Then check the ligature every three days to ensure there is no itching, swelling, or obstruction of nutrition reaching the limb due to excessive binding. Adjust this as described.
If you are confident that none of these issues will occur, then do not loosen the ligature except after many days. The patient should lie on their back with their hand on their stomach, and a cushion filled with wool should be placed under the armpit to support it properly.
Examine at all times, whether day or night, to ensure that the shape of the broken limb does not deteriorate or that the ligaments do not become lax; correct all of this with your effort. Make the diet of the patient as we have recommended, initially making it light so that when the bone is ready to strengthen, it can do so.
You should then make his diet more substantial, for it is customary for the arm and leg to strengthen in forty days. At that time, he should be allowed to bathe and treated with ointments suitable for this purpose. If the fracture is severe and comminuted.
Do not release the bandage for fifty days or even up to two months.
Chapter Twelve.
On the Healing of a Broken Arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, known as the ulna and the radius. One is smaller and is adjacent to the thumb, while the other is larger and is situated below the smaller one. It may happen that only the larger bone (the ulna) breaks, or only the smaller bone (the radius) breaks, or both may break simultaneously.
When the upper smaller bone (radius) breaks, its healing is easier and recovery is faster. When the lower larger bone (ulna) breaks, the fracture is poor and recovery is difficult. The worst case is when both bones break simultaneously. If it is the bone where the upper smaller bone (radius) has broken,
The physician, when setting it, should make the extension slight and gently until it is even. If the larger bone (ulna) is broken, a stronger extension should be made. If both bones are broken simultaneously, an extremely strong extension should be applied.
It is appropriate to place the hand in a position when setting and extending it, lying flat on a cushion with the thumb raised higher than all the fingers, and the little finger lower than the other fingers, and the patient sitting cross-legged.
And let the cushion be at his level in height so that the patient does not strain himself.
Then a helper extends the arm from below, either by hand or with a strap, and another helper does the same from above. Then the doctor aligns the bone until it is in the best possible position. If there are shards in the fractured bone, they must be removed.
Place each shard in its proper place to the best of your ability. If a free-floating shard appears that is piercing the skin and cannot be set, then cut it out according to the method described previously. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, I have devoted a separate section to it.
Refer to that section for treatment. If a hot swelling appears at the beginning of setting the fracture, apply a bandage made of quilted cloth worked with rose oil and white beeswax, neither too thick nor too thin, and wrap it tightly enough so that when the swelling subsides.
Remove the quilted bandage and apply a dressing prepared from flour dust mixed with egg white, then secure it with splints. The splint placed directly over the fracture should be slightly wider and stronger. Know that in most cases, the number of splints for the arm is six at most.
Whether the fracture is in one ulna or both, make your bandaging tighter and stronger at the site of the fracture. Whenever you wrap upwards or downwards, make the wrapping slightly looser as previously mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
Let the patch that is wrapped around the fracture be soft and moist, not too hard. And let the thread with which it is tied be of linen, medium between thinness and thickness as we have described, and examine the part and ligature after days.
If something needs to be fixed, such as itching that occurs in the part, it is necessary to unwrap the part with warm water until the itching subsides and then leave the part unbound for a night so that it may rest, then resume binding.
If the binding has become loose or the bone has shifted or something similar, fix all of it to the best of your ability. Also observe if the nutrition is unable to reach the part due to excessive binding; then you should loosen it slightly and leave it for days until the nutrition flows to it, then bind it again.
If nothing mentioned above occurs to the patient, it is not advisable to remove the binding until after twenty days or so. Then hang the patient's arm around his neck and let his arm be in a moderate position, and guard against erratic movements with all his effort, and make his sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture of the arm is healed in thirty days or in thirty-two, or it may be healed in twenty-eight days, all depending on the conditions of the humors and states of strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
On the Healing of Fractures of the Hand and Fingers.
Fractures of the metacarpus and phalanges are rare, while contusions are common. When a fracture or contusion occurs to the hand,
The patient should sit upright on a chair that is level.
Then he places his hand on it, extended. The servant then extends the broken bone while the doctor aligns it until it fits well together. At this point, the bandage and splint should be applied if no hot swelling has occurred, then a plaster cast is applied from above as needed for the location.
If it has been wrapped in a soft piece of cloth, and if the fracture is towards the palm side, make a ball-like shape with the cloth and have the patient grasp it with their injured hand, then secure it with a long strip of cloth.
The plaster cast should be made of leather that has some flexibility so that the skin can move with the bending of the entire hand, and it should be secured appropriately. If the fracture is on the outer side, a plaster cast should be placed from above and another one from below the wrist to keep the hand open and upright.
Then apply the bandage as the hand is wrapped and tie between the fingers with a lace. If the fracture affects one of the phalanges, especially the thumb, align it properly and secure it with the hand bandage. If desired, you can also make a small separate splint to stabilize the fracture.
Do not move it. If the fracture is in other fingers such as the middle, index, little, or ring finger, align them and secure them with the next healthy finger or bind all of them together, which is better, or place a small separate splint on them.
As we said for the thumb, check for any hot swelling both during and after setting the fracture. Address it appropriately as described previously if such a condition occurs.
Chapter Fourteen.
In the repair of a fractured thigh.
The thigh bone is often fractured and can be detected by sensation because it moves both forward and backward. Its repair involves binding a ligature above the fracture and another below it, with the patient lying face up.
Then each ligature is stretched by an assistant on its side with moderation. This is if the fracture is in the middle of the bone; however, if it is near the base of the thigh, a soft ligature, such as from wool or something similar, should be applied.
Near the base of the thigh towards the groin so that the stretching is upwards, and the other ligature below the fracture. Similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, the ligature should be near the knee so that the stretching is downwards. Then, the physician should adjust the bone with both hands until it returns to its natural shape.
The bone will heal well; therefore, the bandages and compression should be maintained unless a hot swelling occurs in the limb. If such a swelling does occur, leave it for days until the swelling subsides, then resume treatment.
As for the compression, a wide, firm bandage should be wound around the fracture twice or three times, leaving any excess. Then wrap the leg so that the heel is at the base of the calf and pass a long thread between the thigh and the leg near the knee from below, with the ends of the thread emerging from above on both sides.
Then wrap the remaining bandage around the leg and thigh. Place plaster casts directly over the fracture site on the thigh, and another cast on the shinbone. Then stuff the space between the thigh and the leg with soft fabric to ensure even compression.
Then begin with a strong binding from the middle over the site of the fracture, three or four times around, and as you move the bandage farther away from the site of the fracture, your binding should be less tight and softer.
Then, take the ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and the leg, and tie it to the splints above. Then pass the ends of the thread down until you reach the heel of the foot, and also tighten them with the splints on the other side.
To prevent the bandage from shifting from its place, leave it on as long as there is no discoloration, swelling, or puffiness in the affected area. If any of these symptoms occur, immediately loosen it and correct everything that has happened, as we have mentioned repeatedly.
If there is a sharp piece of bone causing irritation, it should be smoothed if possible. If not, incise the area and remove it, then treat the wound as previously described until it heals. This fracture may also be stabilized from the thigh without adding splints to the leg.
As we mentioned for the forearm and arm, however, if it is set in this way, it does not cause the patient to limp. If you set it alone without adding the leg to it, the patient will inevitably limp forever.
Know that the thigh takes about fifty days to heal, or a little more or less, depending on differences in temperament and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the Fracture of the Knee Joint.
The knee joint is rarely fractured, but it often sustains bruises. If a fracture does occur, it will either be a split or fragmentation of its parts, whether accompanied by a wound or not.
You can determine all of this by touch. To set it, align the dispersed parts with your fingers until they come together and fit properly as much as possible, then apply a bandage and if necessary, place a circular splint on top.
It should be securely fastened from above as appropriate, then all conditions described in the treatment of other fractures, such as hot swelling and similar issues, should be attended to by addressing each symptom appropriately until recovery.
Chapter Sixteen.
On the Treatment of a Fractured Leg.
The leg has two bones, one thick called the thigh bone, and the other thin called the shin bone. Both are susceptible to the same types of fractures as the bones of the arm. Therefore, treating it is like treating the arm, and the procedure is the same. If both bones are fractured, the leg will turn in all directions. If only the thinner bone is broken, the leg will turn forward. If the thicker bone is broken, especially from below, this should be obvious to you.
Use traction and alignment, and bind the splints accordingly. However, if the fracture of the leg is severe with many fragments, the traction should be gentler and you should proceed carefully in setting it.
And in the treatment of the leg there is something additional compared to the arm, which is that when you have set the splints and completed all your work, take two handfuls of pine splinters used for smoothing the gaps between planks or made from date palm leaves or similar material.
Choose from them pieces that are neither too thick nor too thin, and let their length be from the knee to the bottom of the leg. Then wrap two layers of cloth around each piece, one on the side of the leg and the other on the opposite side.
From the knee to the bottom of the foot, then tie the two handfuls in three places at each end and in the middle. This will prevent the leg from tilting to the right or left and will ensure good immobilization. A groove made of wood can also be used along the length of the leg.
It should be placed in it to keep it from moving, especially when there is a wound with the fracture. Then check the leg every day with great care for swelling or puffiness or other signs. Whenever any of these signs appear, counter them appropriately until healing occurs. The bone of the leg heals in about thirty days or so.
Chapter Seventeen.
On Fractures of the Leg Bones and Fingers.
As for the heel, it is never fractured. As for the leg bones, they may be fractured, but fingers rarely suffer fractures; rather, they usually experience contusions in most cases.
If a man's bones are fractured and you see those bones protruding toward one another, then let the patient stand on his foot upright as if he were walking, then place your foot on the part that has risen from those bones and press them down and smooth them.
Until they return to their proper places, then take the bandage and support; place a small board with two flat ends under the sole of the foot, then bind the foot tightly after wrapping it with cloth and all other necessities.
After three or four days, loosen the ligature; you will find the bones level whether they were broken or dislocated. The treatment is as described, and if any of the fingers are fractured, brace them and set them as described for setting the fingers of the hand.
Then make a splint for the broken finger that extends along the length of the finger, and let it be slightly wider than the finger. Place under the foot the board I described to you and bind it tightly. If two, three, or more bones are broken.
Make a splint for each finger, slightly grooved in a soft cloth, and bind the board securely at the bottom of the foot. Let each end of the board protrude slightly beyond the arch of the foot to provide good support.
You should not forget to attend to all the symptoms we have mentioned for other fractures as well, and counter each symptom appropriately.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the fracture of a woman's pubic bone and the man's penis.
When a woman's vagina is fractured, seat her in a squatting position, then gradually lean her towards her back little by little. Then the midwife should fill her vagina with cotton until it is full and becomes like a ball inside her vagina.
Then shake the woman and gradually raise her hips little by little, for that cotton will come out until it becomes like a ball at the entrance of her vagina, thus healing the bone fracture. Then place a cushion on her back; when she wants to urinate, remove the cotton gently so she can urinate.
Return it in the same manner as it was first inserted, then return to her original cushion. Do this for seven days or so until it is healed. If you wish, take a sheep's bladder and attach a tube to its mouth, then insert the entire bladder into her vagina.
Then blow into the tube with force until the bladder inflates inside the vagina, as this will help the fracture to heal. Then fill it with cotton and keep her for days on what has been described until she is healed. As for when the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, use these methods to set it.
And smoothing it out is as described for the hip bone. The proper approach to these rare fractures, which rarely occur, especially for those who have some experience and understand this book well, should not be hidden from you. The fracture itself will often guide you in most cases.
Follow the correct method for setting and securing it, so understand. As for a man when his thigh is fractured, if you insert a goose neck into it, then wrap it with a bandage and bind it; leave it for three days or so until it heals.
Chapter Nineteen.
On the healing of bone fractures when accompanied by a wound.
It is advisable for one who encounters a fracture accompanied by a wound, especially if the bone is large such as the thigh bone or the humerus or similar, to proceed immediately to extract it if the conditions for extraction assist you, as we have presented before.
If the wound is bleeding, it is advisable to proceed by applying a powdered substance like "zaj" (a type of plaster) if nothing else is available, and then proceed with setting the fracture on that same day without delaying.
If no hot swelling occurs, then if a hot swelling does occur, delay setting it until the ninth day so that the hot swelling subsides. Do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, as doing so would lead to adverse outcomes.
If the broken bone is protruding on the skin and exposed, it is advisable to gently attempt to push it back and smooth it with your hands gradually. If you are unable to do so with your hands, then use a tool made of iron, which should be about seven or eight finger breadths in length. And adjust its size according to the wound.
Therefore, it is advisable for the physician to prepare three or four of these tools, according to what is needed for treatment in each type of fracture. They should be rounded and slightly thick so that they do not bend when pressure is applied during work.
They should be sharp at one end with a bend, and the top part should be thicker while from the middle to the bottom it should be very thin. This is its shape:.
It is called in Greek "perne," meaning a small lever. It should be made so that its curved sharp end fits the protruding part of the bone, and with it, push once to make the bone return and partially realign itself, then use it to even out the broken ends against each other.
If the broken end is thin and not properly grasped by the instrument, it should be cut so that the instrument can grasp it. If it is impossible to realign the bone as described, then cut it in a manner similar to what has been mentioned.
Or spread it with one of the files as much as possible, then smooth out any roughness or thin layers left in the bone. If you realign the bone and the patient experiences severe painful discomfort after realignment, know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you are able to return it to its natural position, do so, for you will provide great benefit to the patient. When you have completed setting the bone, soak a cloth in a contracting black liquid and especially do this if it is summer. Do not place a ceroty or anything with oil on the wound.
To prevent rot and corruption from occurring, then apply splints after you have set the bone, and leave the wound uncovered by cutting appropriate holes in the bandages with scissors over the wound.
Be extremely cautious not to tighten the wound along with the fracture, for many ignorant doctors have done this and caused either death or consumption or catarrh in their patients. Your tightening should be gentle and relaxed, contrary to how other fractures are tightened.
If the wound is poor or if it is large and you fear some of the harmful symptoms we have described, and he feels painful anxiety in the area, then you should not apply splints but make firm bandages from cloth in the place of the splints and secure them.
If, after one or two days, you see that the wound has begun to produce pus, then remove the dressing that was placed on it with a decoction, and then use bandages and ointments that we usually use to treat wounds, such as the quadruple ointment and similar ones, and you should loosen the ligature.
Examine the wound every morning and evening until it heals, and you should position the limb so that pus can easily drain downward. If many days pass and the wound does not heal or the pus does not stop flowing, then know that there are small bone fragments present.
You should probe the wound with an instrument; whatever of those fragments is loose, remove and extract it. Whatever is not loose and causes irritation and pain to the limb, crush and remove it by any means possible.
If the wound develops pus, decay, or any other type of corruption and putrefaction, then you should treat each condition with a corresponding treatment as described earlier in its section.
When a large bone such as the femur or humerus is fractured and affects the limb, you must not attempt to pull or extract it. Often, attempting to do so can lead to death, so leave it and allow it to become infected; perhaps it will naturally discharge after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see room for treatment, otherwise leave it alone.
Chapter Twenty.
On the treatment of complications that occur after some fractures.
This complication often occurs after the fracture has healed, especially near joints, deforming the shape of the limb and sometimes preventing it from performing its natural function.
If you examine the complication and find it soft, use astringent medicines such as sage, frankincense, aloes, galangal, and hellebore, or similar remedies. Take some or all of these and mix them with an astringent decoction, egg white, or vinegar.
Apply it to the complication with effort and wrap it tightly, leaving it on for many days without removing it. Then remove it and repeat with another treatment until the complication subsides, if God wills, or you can secure a sheet of lead tightly around it.
Lead has a property that removes all that has settled in the limbs. If the complication has become hardened and severe, and the need arises to remove it, make an incision at its top and cut off the protruding part or strip it with some forceps until it is removed, then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On the treatment of fractures when they have healed but the limb remains thin and not as it was originally.
If the bones are set and the part remains thin and weak afterwards, this is due to many reasons. One reason may be due to the excessive loosening and improper retightening of ligaments, or it may be because of over-tightening the ligaments so that nourishment cannot reach the part. Another reason could be excessive stretching, or performing overly vigorous movements at inappropriate times, or there may be a lack of blood in the patient's body and weakness.
The treatment for this involves nourishing the patient and strengthening their body until the blood increases within it. Using baths, bringing joy and happiness to the patient, and similar methods should be employed. Then apply pitch to the affected part so that it attracts abundant nutrition, or use warm water for gentle stretching. Until the nourishment flows through it and it returns to its natural form.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On the Treatment of Broken Bones That Are Set Crooked and Prevent Their Proper Function.
When a part that has been set after healing becomes crooked, or the broken bone protrudes, or the appearance of the part becomes deformed and ugly. Unless the part is prevented from its natural function, it is not appropriate to accept the claim that the bone should be broken again from the joint, as many ignorant physicians and bone setters used to do this in our country.
This action is highly reprehensible and can lead to significant risks, with the easiest consequence being damage. However, if the crookedness and deformation are still soft, it should be gently stretched with water in which relaxing herbs have been boiled, such as mallow leaves and roots, chamomile, and similar plants.
It is treated with relaxing dressings such as well-made diascorides, or saliva from the root of the snail is taken and mixed with chicken fat and schirj oil, then it is dressed with it, or fatty figs are taken and crushed with pigeon droppings and similar medicines known as ulcer-breaking remedies.
And to dissolve the stiffness, constant gentle massage with the hands is used, along with moving the limb in all directions at all times. If the curvature has advanced and hardened, and iron must be used for its treatment due to necessity.
It is necessary to incise it above and release the bone connection, cutting any excess stiffness or bone with gentle cuts, using care and attention. Then treat the wound as previously mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
The jaw is when one of the joints dislocates from its proper position, impeding movement, deforming the appearance of the part, and causing severe pain to the patient. When it occurs in anyone, it is necessary to immediately act to reduce it back into place.
It must never be delayed, for if it is, the area will swell and become hard to reduce. Therefore, it should not be delayed or moved when swollen, as this often causes painful spasms and discomfort to the patient.
However, if this happens, it is necessary to immediately bleed the patient, then leave them until the swelling subsides slightly. Then, the part should be treated with hot water and oil, and gently reduced back into place. Each part should be treated as described in its respective section.
I have also arranged the chapters on fractures in the same order as those for wounds, from top to bottom of the body.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On the treatment of the lower jaw.
Dislocation of the jaw is rare, and it occurs in two ways: either the bones move slightly from their positions and become relaxed a little, or they dislocate completely, causing full relaxation. Until it reaches the chest area, causing saliva to flow out and the patient is unable to hold it back; he cannot close his jaw or speak properly. If the dislocation is slight, in most cases, it can return to its normal position by itself with minimal effort.
If the dislocation is complete, it should be promptly reduced without delay. This is done by having an attendant hold the patient's head while the physician inserts the thumb of one hand into the base of the jaw inside the mouth if the dislocation is on one side.
Or if the dislocation is on both sides, he inserts both thumbs inside the mouth and uses his other fingers from outside to guide it. He then instructs the patient to relax their jaw and move it freely in all directions, while the physician guides the jaw back into place.
If it is difficult to reduce the dislocation, especially if both jaws are involved, use bandages with hot water and ointment to make reduction easier. Do not delay in reducing them as we have said. Once they are back in place and the patient's mouth has closed without any relaxation, then apply compresses made from cloths soaked in a mixture of wax, oil, and pitch.
Then tie it gently with a loose bandage, and let the patient sleep on his back with his head propped up between two pillows so that he does not move to the right or left, nor strain to chew anything, but rather make his food liquid and soft.
Until the pain subsides and his jaw heals, let him eat whatever he wishes but do so gently; he must not strain to open his mouth when eating, drinking, or yawning until his jaw is healed and he is fully recovered.
If the jaws are difficult to reposition when they become dislocated and do not return to their proper places, it often results in fevers, persistent headaches, and sometimes causes the patient's abdomen to be upset, or leads to repeated vomiting. If you see these symptoms, know that the condition is serious and damaging.
And it often happens that a person dies from this condition within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On Repositioning the Clavicle and the End of the Shoulder.
As for the clavicle, it does not dislocate inward due to its connection with the chest, but it can dislocate outward and this can be detected by touch. To set it, the patient should lie on his back and extend his arms.
Then press the dislocated area with your palm firmly, and it will return to its place. Then apply a bandage and dressing and secure it tightly. As for the end of the clavicle near the shoulder joint, it rarely dislocates; however, if it does, it should be repositioned as described and adjusted as you see fit.
Then apply the bandage, padding, and compression, and instruct the patient to rest and remain still until he recovers. With this same treatment, you should also address the displacement of the shoulder when it has also moved from its proper position.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On Reducing the Dislocation of the Shoulder.
Know that the shoulder dislocates in three ways: the first is when it dislocates toward the armpit downward; the second is when it dislocates toward the chest; and rarely, it may dislocate above the shoulder.
It does not dislocate to the back due to the position of the shoulder blade, nor does it dislocate to the front due to the presence of the nerve. It most commonly dislocates and moves downward toward the armpit, especially in those with little flesh, as it dislocates quickly and reduces quickly in them.
As for those with abundant flesh, it is the opposite; meaning that it dislocates with difficulty and reduces with difficulty. And sometimes a person may receive a blow or fall, causing the shoulder to swell and become hot, leading them to believe that it has dislocated. It is necessary to examine this carefully to determine its true condition.
At that time, proceed with its treatment. To identify a dislocation downward toward the armpit, if you compare the dislocated shoulder with the healthy one, you will find a clear difference between them. You will notice an indentation at the head of the dislocated shoulder, and when you touch under the armpit, you will feel the head of the shoulder like an egg.
The patient is unable to raise his hand to his ear or to move it in any way, and similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest or upwards, you will find this evident to the touch, not hidden.
This dislocation may be easily reduced if the bone is soft or the patient is a child. To reduce it, have a servant raise his hand above, and place your thumbs under the armpit, then lift the joint forcefully to its proper position while the servant raises his hand and extends it upwards.
Then gently guide it downwards, and it will quickly return to its place. If it does not return as mentioned above, and the dislocation has been there for many days, the patient should bathe in hot water and use a decoction that relaxes and softens, such as cooking the root of colocynth, fenugreek, and lemon balm in water and using it.
Then the patient lies on his back, and a ball of wool is placed under his armpit, which should be moderately firm. The physician then places the patient's heel on the ball and holds the patient's hand with his own, extending it downwards.
The physician pushes the ball with his foot; it will return to its proper place. If you prefer, reduce it in this manner: have a man taller than the patient stand on one side and insert his shoulder under the patient's armpit, lifting the armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in the air. Another servant should pull the patient's hand downwards towards his abdomen. If the patient is light, it is advisable to attach something to make him heavier; then the jaw will return immediately. It can also be reduced in another manner.
It is that you focus on the ground a long piece of wood whose head is round like the pestle, not too thick nor too thin, then place it under the armpit of the patient after placing a soft cloth on top of the wooden head. The patient stands along the length of the wood.
Then extend his hand downward from the other side and also stretch his body forcefully from the other side, for the joint will return to its place quickly. If it is difficult to do so with all of what has been mentioned, use this treatment: take a piece of wood about two cubits long. And its width is four fingers, and its thickness is two fingers, it should have a round head to facilitate its entry into the armpit's depth as shown in this illustration:.
Then tie a soft cloth around the round head to prevent the wood from harming the patient. Place it under the armpit, extend the entire hand and arm downward along the wood, and secure the wood against the shoulder, forearm, and hand.
Then place the arm on a crossbeam. Support with the beam and extend the hand downward while leaving the rest of the body hanging from the other side, for the joint will enter promptly. Once it has entered in whatever manner possible, you should place under the armpit a moderate-sized ball of wool.
Then the prepared bandage, made from flour dust mixed with frankincense and egg whites, is applied all over the shoulder as it wraps around. Then tightly secure the ball under the armpit and tie the bandages firmly over it. Finally, suspend his hand to his neck.
He should leave it unmoved for seven days, and it is best to give the patient a light diet so that the limb becomes stronger, which will speed its healing. Then after either seven or five days, he should train it with movement; if it remains stable and does not weaken, then it has healed.
If the joint dislocates frequently due to moisture affecting it or another reason, cauterization with three superficial burns should be used as previously described in the section on cauterization. If you have done all of this and released the ligament after seven days but the joint remains unstable.
And if you repeatedly apply bandages and compression but it does not stabilize, and it falls and becomes loose, and you cannot raise it above, then know that the nerve in the shoulder joint has been severed or stretched or loosened. Then know that the joint will never remain stable in its place.
As for the jaw that is toward the chest and breast and to the back, its reduction is by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns, and all forms of bandaging and treatment should be used until it heals. If stiffness occurs in the limb and movement becomes slower after healing.
Let the patient frequently use the bath to soften that stiffness and return it to its original nature.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On the Treatment of the Elbow Joint.
The elbow joint dislocates with difficulty, and it also returns with difficulty. It can dislocate in all directions, especially forward or backward. Its reduction is not hidden from you because it occurs under observation and palpation, to any form of dislocation.
When you join the dislocated elbow with the sound one, it becomes clearly apparent by the deformation of the joint, and he is unable to bend his arm or touch his shoulder with it. You should immediately reduce the dislocation before a hot swelling affects him.
If a hot swelling affects him, it will be difficult to reduce the dislocation, and he may never fully recover, especially if the dislocation is towards the back; this is the worst type of dislocation among all types and causes the most severe pain, often leading to death.
To reduce it if it can be returned, have a servant extend both his arms with his hands outstretched. The physician places his hands above and below the elbow, pushing the joint with his thumbs together or with the base of his palm until it returns to its proper place.
As for a dislocation towards the front, it may be reduced by bending the hand once and striking the base of the palm against the shoulder that is opposite. If this does not suffice to return the joint, use extreme force, which involves two servants extending the arm.
Two servants should also hold the patient to prevent movement during the extension. Then, after wrapping a folded cloth or a wide piece of fabric around his arm, if the physician handles the joint with his hands, he should smear it with oil to facilitate easy sliding of the joint.
Then, push the joint forcefully until it returns to its place. After it has returned, a bandage that includes compressing and drying should be applied, along with egg whites, and tightly secured. The arm should then be suspended from the patient's neck for several days.
Then release it. If the joint is firmly in place, free the ligament but leave it alone; if you see that the joint has not yet become firm and well-set, replace the bandage and ligature and leave it for days until it becomes firm, then remove it. If swelling occurs after the return of the joint and its release.
If movement is difficult, let him use moistening in the bath and gentle massage and kneading until it becomes soft; or place a thick leather casing from a fat ram on the joint, then secure it and leave it for a day and night. Then remove it and have him enter the bath. When he sweats, rub the joint moderately with his sweat.
Then reapply the leather casing once, twice, and three times with baths until it softens. If you wish to apply moistened ox dung mixed with fat and secure it, do so repeatedly; it will soften and return to its original condition.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On the Treatment of the Wrist Joint.
The wrist often dislocates and it is easier to reduce its joint compared to other joints, except that it is necessary to promptly reduce the joint at the moment it dislocates before it becomes displaced or a hot swelling occurs.
To reduce the joint, place the patient's wrist on a board and have an assistant extend their hand. The physician should place their hand on the protruding part of the joint and push it until it returns, but it is necessary to observe whether the joint has moved to the palm side.
Let the patient place his hand on the board during traction and counter-traction; if the jaw protrudes toward the palm, let him place his inner hand on the board so that the physician's hand falls directly on the joint. If it returns [to its proper position] at this point, otherwise bind it with a bandage to reduce swelling.
Leave it and do not attempt to readjust it, for it cannot bear such treatment and you will not be able to return it after a few days; unless the joint remains in its proper position, it does no harm to the patient except if the hand relaxes and is unable to grasp anything.
At that moment, you will know that the nerve has been severed or bruised; there is no remedy for it except to cauterize it, which may be helpful or may not benefit at all. Once the wrist has been realigned, apply the bandage as described and secure it, then leave it for five days.
Then remove the bandage and train the hand. If its movement is difficult and stiffness appears, soften it with warm water and massage repeatedly until it becomes supple.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On the Treatment of Finger Joints.
Fingers may dislocate in any direction. When a finger dislocates towards the palm or the back of the hand, extend the finger and push the joint with your thumb until it returns to its place, then bind the tip of the finger and suspend it toward the direction in which it dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release and extend it so that it becomes aligned over the course of that day. If at night, bind it as described; during the day, continue to remove the bandage and train it with movement, and rebind it at night. Repeat this for several days until it strengthens.
And do the same if it becomes dislocated towards the palm of the hand; bind it in the same direction as before, and proceed as you did at first until it heals. Do this also whenever it becomes dislocated in any other direction.
Chapter Thirty.
On the Treatment of Spinal Vertebra Dislocation.
When a vertebra of the spine or neck becomes completely dislocated, or when many vertebrae are displaced from their proper positions, there is no treatment because death quickly follows for the patient.
The sign of this is that the patient's excretion comes out involuntarily, and they are unable to control it. Often, some of their limbs become weak or paralyzed, either both legs, or one or both arms. If a single vertebra is displaced from its proper position, it often leads to further displacement.
It can dislocate in four directions: if it moves backward, it is called a hunchback. As for its treatment, one must observe: if the hunchback occurred during childhood, there is no cure and it will never heal. However, if it occurred due to a fall or a blow or something similar.
They have described various treatments for it at length, but most of them do not yield much benefit. I have summarized the essentials that suffice and explained them in a way that clarifies their meaning better than the extensive descriptions provided by others. I have also depicted the instrument differently from what was previously shown and explained.
I say that a hunchback that occurs in the chest from the front cannot be treated, and it will not heal. The same applies to those occurring on the sides. Only those that occur in the back can be treated as I shall describe, which is to lay the patient face down.
On a level workbench near a wall, spread a moist towel underneath it so as not to hurt his chest. Then place a vertical plank embedded in a hole in the ground at one end of the bench towards his head and another towards his feet at the other end of the bench.
And a servant holds the plank, and let it not be securely fixed in the hole; another servant holds the other plank in that manner. Then wrap around the patient's chest and under his armpits with a soft but snug cloth bandage, and extend one end of the bandage to the plank at his head and secure it there.
Then secure with another bandage above his hips, above his knees, and at his toes. Then gather all the ties together and fasten them to the other plank at his feet. Then each servant pulls on the plank in his direction by the tie so that the planks do not move from their fixed positions.
However, they are not securely fixed as we said. The physician places his hands firmly on the knot so that it returns or places a board over it; then he leans on the board with his feet until it returns. If it does not return with this treatment, take a board that is about three cubits long.
And dig into the wall near the patient a place where one end of the board can be inserted. Then place the middle of the board on the hump, and the physician places his feet on the other end of the board and pulls it tightly until the knot is compressed.
And it returns to its place. If you wish to make it with a lever that is twisted by hand, then fix two planks in the ground at the head of the patient at the end of the bench, each about an arm's length long, and similarly two more at his feet at the other end of the bench.
And let there be one cubit between each board, and make a hole in each board through which the spindle passes. Secure all the boards firmly in the ground so that they do not move at all, and insert a round stick, which is the spindle around which the ligature is twisted in the holes of the two boards.
At one end, there is a hole in which a stick of one cubit length is secured for twisting. The same is done in the other two boards. Then, the ligature that was tied around the patient's chest is attached to the spindle at his head, and the ligatures that were tied around his legs are attached to the spindle at his feet.
Then, a servant stands by each spindle and twists it with his hand using the twisting tool, while the doctor adjusts the swelling as previously described. This is an image of the spindles, the frame, and the patient:.
Then, after the vertebra returns to its place and the area is leveled, it is necessary to apply a plaster bandage dried with egg white, followed by a pad. Then place a splint made of wood above the bandage, which should have a width of about three fingers, and its length should be approximately where the swelling was.
And then place some correct stitches and bind with the appropriate ligature. The patient should use gentle food until he recovers. If there are any lumps remaining in the area at the end of the healing process, it is necessary to use medicines that relax and soften, along with using the described splint for a long period.
A lead plate may be used for this purpose. Sometimes, a lump appears at the end of the stitches on the spine, and it is assumed to be dislocation; however, it may be an excess bone that has grown, so do not treat it with this method, as it could cause death.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On the Treatment of Dislocated Hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint are subject to dislocation only, and they do not suffer from the slight displacement or narrowing that other joints may experience. The hip joint can become dislocated in four ways.
It can dislocate inward, outward, forward, and backward. Dislocation inward is the most common, while dislocation forward or backward is less frequent. A sign of inward dislocation is that when you compare the lengths of the healthy leg and the affected leg with the feet together, the affected leg appears longer.
The knee on the affected side will protrude more than the healthy one, and the patient will not be able to bend the leg at the groin. The area near the groin will appear swollen and clearly distended because the head of the femur has moved there.
A sign of dislocation outward is that the symptoms are opposite to those described. A sign of dislocation forward is that the patient can fully extend the leg without pain, but if they attempt to walk or bend the knee, they cannot do so forward without discomfort.
The patient will experience difficulty in urination, and their groin will be tense. When walking, they will walk on their heel. A sign of dislocation backward is that the knee cannot be straightened or bent without first bending the hip, and the leg will also appear shorter than the other.
The groin will be relaxed, and the head of the femur will be clearly visible in the position of the waist. As for treating these types of dislocations, if the dislocation is old and has caused prolonged suffering without any attempt to reduce it, there is no cure.
It should not be exposed, and as for the one that was recently dislocated, whether it is of any type from the four aspects of the jaw, you should immediately twist the joint and stretch it inward and outward and move it right and left, for perhaps [it may help].
He returns without needing any other treatment. If he does not return like this, it is necessary to prepare a strong servant who can extend his leg from below, either with his hands or with a sling tied above the knee, and another servant to support him from above.
By inserting his hands under his armpits, then securing a soft sling at the root of the thigh, and another servant holding the end of the sling, he is pulled either from the front, from the groin area to the neck area, or from behind, to the back area.
They all pull at once until the patient is lifted off the ground and remains suspended. This type of pulling is common to the four types. If the jaw returns as we have said, otherwise, each type requires a specific treatment that I will mention.
As for its specific treatment if the jaw is dislocated inward, the patient should lie on his healthy side, then a sling should be placed at the root of the thigh between the head of the femur and the area below the groin.
Then extend the sling from the side of the groin upwards to the top of the body towards the neck, then another strong servant takes hold with his arms and embraces the thick part of the patient's thigh and pulls outward very strongly; it will return to its place.
This type is easier than all other types of treatment used to restore this organ. If it proves impossible for you and the patient absolutely refuses this type of treatment, then you should bind both legs of the patient with a strong but flexible bandage at the heels and knees.
Each should be spaced four finger breadths apart, and the affected leg should be extended more than the other by two finger breadths. Then, suspend the patient from the head using a beam in the house, with the distance from the ground being about two forearm lengths.
Then, order a strong servant to embrace the head of the thigh, and another servant should hold the patient. The other servant should push firmly, causing the joint to quickly return to its place. As for its specific reduction when the jaw is dislocated outward.
The patient should lie down on the bed as described for one with kyphosis. The bandage should be tightened around his affected leg and across his chest. Then, place two beams, one at his feet and the other at his head. Additionally, place an extra beam in the middle of the bed secured very tightly.
A wet cloth should be wrapped around it to prevent it from harming the patient, and the beam should be placed between his thighs to prevent him from dropping downward when stretched. Then, each servant pulls in their respective directions, while the doctor adjusts the hip with both hands; if it responds and returns.
Otherwise, place the board on him and press it as described for kyphosis, except that the patient should lie on his healthy side. If the dislocation is forward, then extend the patient's leg once while he is in this description on the bed.
The doctor places his right hand on the diseased joint, then presses it with the other hand, and stretches the pressure downward toward the knee. If the dislocation is backward, it should not be extended downward.
It should be raised from the ground and should be placed on a solid surface, as it should also extend from his nose to his hip outward, according to how he lies on the operating table, with his face up and restraints tightened.
As we said before, the pressure with the board should also be used on the place to which the joint has dislocated. When the hip joint returns as it should, and its return is not hidden from you, which is evident when you can straighten the patient's leg.
When you see them level and the patient can draw back and extend the leg without difficulty, then know that the limb has returned as it should. At this point, join the thighs together and apply bandages tightly so that the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient must remain still for three or four days, then loosen the restraints and bandages and measure the leg against the other. If they are equal in length, then know that the joint has stabilized; release the patient to walk. If you notice any slackness in it.
Return, dress the wound, and bind it as you did initially. Leave it for three more days, then loosen it and allow him to walk on it gradually for several days until it strengthens, so he may walk normally, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
On the Treatment of Dislocated Kneecap.
The knee dislocates in three ways: inward, outward, and downward, which means to the rear; it does not dislocate forward at all. The sign of its dislocation is that you instruct the patient to draw up his leg toward his thigh.
If it does not adhere to the thigh, then know that the knee is dislocated. To reduce all forms of its dislocation, seat the patient sitting with his legs extended if he can manage this; have a servant sit behind him to hold his waist and slightly turn it backward; then sit on his thighs.
Lean your back against his face and place his leg between yours; then grasp his knee with both hands, interlocking your fingers around it; press the sides of his knee firmly with your hands; another servant extends his leg until the knee returns to its proper position.
The sign of its return is that the leg adheres gently to the thigh without being forced; then dress it and bind the leg to the thigh with a bandage for three or four days, then release them. He should not use it except for little walking for several days until it strengthens.
If it is difficult for you to return it as described, then use strong traction with the bandages that I have previously described for treating the hip until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On the Treatment of Dislocated Heel.
The heel may become slightly loose, or it may come completely detached; its detachment may be either inward or outward. The sign of its detachment is that you will see the heel swollen and protruding in the direction to which it has separated.
As for the treatment of its slight loosening, it is easy to return it by gently stretching with your hands until it returns to its place. As for its complete detachment, it should be that the patient sits down and a strong servant holds him firmly from behind at his waist.
Then, with your right hand, grasp his foot from the top and with your left hand from below at the ankle. Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand twice, then stretch it with your left hand while gently pushing the front of the foot toward the leg with your right hand without using force.
Do this twice as described, then in the third time push the front of the foot toward the leg while pulling at the ankle. If it returns in one or two attempts in this manner and you see that the foot is level, otherwise repeat the procedure on it, for it will return.
If it refuses to return as described, then lay the patient on his back on the ground and secure a lever firmly in the ground between his thighs, wrapping it with cloth so as not to injure the patient. Then a servant should hold his thigh steady.
Then another servant extends the leg either with his hands or by using a rope tied around the ankle. Both servants then pull in opposite directions while the lever remains between the patient's thighs to prevent his body from being pulled downward during the extension. The physician then aligns the detachment with his hands.
Another servant holds the healthy leg below, for the bone will quickly return to its place. When it returns and you see that it has returned correctly, apply the bandage and secure it tightly with straps, and bind the foot firmly below with a strap. Be careful of the nerve that is above the heel from behind.
So that the binding is not too tight and does not hurt him. Then leave it for two or three days; if the binding loosens, retighten it, then release it on the third or fourth day. The patient must abstain from walking for forty days.
If he attempts to walk before this period, there is no guarantee that the bone will not dislocate and become irreparable, as treatment may not be effective afterward. If inflammation occurs, you should use what has been previously described in other parts of the text for treatment and poultices until it subsides.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On the Treatment of Finger Fractures of the Leg.
It is necessary to realign what has become dislocated with a gentle pull without violence, which is not difficult but rather easy. If the bone is in one of the segments at the back of the foot, the patient should place their foot on an even surface of the ground.
Or place the foot on a board while the patient is standing as if walking, then step on it with your foot firmly until it returns to its proper position and you see that it is aligned without any lumps appearing in the area.
Then place a board under the sole of his foot that covers the entire foot and has two supports, then secure it tightly for three days. Then release it but keep him from walking for many days until it strengthens and you are assured there is no return to dislocation, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
Concerning the types of suturing for wounds or fractures or both.
When any of these occur and one attempts to treat and set them, they often lead to death.
Therefore, one should not attempt to treat such cases unless they are skilled in the craft, experienced, gentle, cautious, and neither reckless nor overconfident. Initially, they should use only medicines that relieve hot swellings and leave the patient at ease.
To the extent, O Lord, except what one hopes to recover from damage with a light illness and where some hope is apparent; then immediately at the outset, before the hot swelling occurs, attempt to treat it.
If the part returns to what was intended, then use methods to relieve hot swellings and treat the wound with appropriate drying ointments. If the suturing is accompanied by a fracture and fragments of bone become dislodged, then attempt to remove them. Follow in this regard what we have mentioned regarding simple diseases as discussed earlier in their respective places.
Strive to your best effort and purify yourself from entering the path of risk, as I have advised you earlier. This will preserve your reputation and safeguard your dignity, if God wills.
